NOW READY.
THE SECOND EDITION
or
Fair girls $ gray Horses,
WITH OTHER VERSES.
By Will H. Ogilvie.
Bound in silk cloth, gilt top, with silk marker; >9O
pages, ks. 6d. post-free from Thb Bollbtin Office.
Sand stamps of any Australasian■ province;
postal note; or P. 0.0.
PUBLISHERS' NOTE.—This is the largest
volume of verse yet issued by an Australian writer,
containing 99 poems. Twenty-three pieces have been
added to those appearing in the first edition, includ-
ing several hitherto unpublished.
Among these additional pieces are the fine horse-
ballad, “How We Won the Ribbon;” the lilting
lines entitled “ The Bush, My Lover,” perhaps the
finest lyric utterance yet inspired by the spirit of
Australia; and other excellent verses.
More than ever the volume now deserves Victor
Daley’s eulogium as “the most charming book of
Australian verse that has yet appeared.”
4
SOME PRESS OPINIONS ON THE FIRST EDITION.
A beautiful volume . . . the real West has never been
so celebrated. — Sydney Morning Herald.
Mr. W. H. Ogilvie has achieved a distinct success, and
elevated the horse to the topmost realm of romance.—
South Australian Register.
He holds his own as an Australian poet, racy of the
soil, with any who has yet appeared.— Freeman’s Journal.
He combines the spirited ring of Paterson with the
more delicate intuition of Lawson and Daley . . . One
of the best collections of Australian verse we have seen
since Gordon. — Sydney Daily Telegraph.
Mr. Ogilvie gives us the brightness, beauty, and
glamour of the Australian uplands, and his ballads are
full of life and the joys of life ... his verses will make
many a bushman’s blood run fast in sympathy.— Evening
News.
There is a sweetness about his work —a passionate ten-
derness —an ideality of conception—an eloquence of poetic
diction—that places him on a fair level with the best Aus-
tralian poets. It is a book of genuine heart-throbs.—
Grenfell Vedette.
Never a man who has ridden a good horse, never a man
who has loved a fair girl, will read these verses without a
quickening of the blood and a firing of the pulses.—Otago
Witness.
The most spontaneous and bubblingly lyrical verse that
has yet appeared in Australia.— S.A. Critic.
There is all the buoyancy, the lustiness of youth, the
joie-de-vivre of the man who rejoices in the fresh air and
the fine, free up-country life—all this there is in Mr.
Ogilvie’s verse, and much more that is eminently sane and
healthy, a characteristic production of a wholesome
mind.— New Zealand Mail.
Every lover of good English, true sentiment, and out-
spoken regard for Australia as a land to live in and be
proud of, should get this volume.— Warren Herald.
A writer of exquisite verse.— Newcastle Morning
Herald.
New milk in the desert. ... If your liver is out of
erder buy a copy of “ Fair Girls and Gray Horses.”—
Orange Leader.
Will last for all time as one of the best contributions to
Australian song.— Richmond River Express.
A charming addition to our rapidly-growing literature.
—Albury Border Post.

weekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
American newspaper,

upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader well

The American Rejection of Poe.
Accepted authors are like those old estates
which were held by the annual rent of a rose
or a piece of fruit: we have nothing to do but to
enjoy them and pay them a passing tribute of
{iraise. A poet such as Poe, however, is like the
eudal tenures which were retained on condition
of service at arms. Every new admirer has to
fight against the prejudices and lingering maligni-
ties which obscure and injure his chief. Burke
oomplained that with all his services to the state
he could get no credence or acceptance anywhere.
At every gate he had to show his passport. In
his own country, at least, Poe’s fame is continu-
ally under arrest, and his friends have always
to be giving bail for him. Perhaps this demand
for defence evokes a love and loyalty which are in
themselves a reward.
Why is it that America has always set its face
against Poe? What defect was there in his life
and art, or what deficiency in the American char-
acter and aesthetic sense, or what incompatibility
between these factors in the case, to produce such
a result ? That to a great extent he is ignored
and repudiated is unquestionable. His life has
been written and his works edited of late in a
spirit of cold hostility. Volumes of specimen
selections of prose or verse appear with his work
omitted. In those foolish lists of American great
men which it was the fashion recently to cause
sohool-children to memorise, he was always left
out. Meanwhile, Europe has but one opinion in
the matter; and whereas Tennyson is domesti-
cated in English-speaking lands, Poe is domiciled
and a dominant force wherever there is a living
literature.
Poe never had a good back, such as the New
England writers obtained, to push him to the
front and keep him there. He was of the South—
the very incarnation of the South ; and the South
has always ordered its authors to move on, for
fear they might die on the parish. The South
wreaked itself on politics—ruined itself by politics
—and has never had the will or desire to stand up
for its greatest son. The North has always had
plenty of plain livers and high thinkers who ought
to have welcomed the martyr of thought and
imagination; but some exotic in Poe, which
hinted of another clime and age, repelled these
cold and clannish spirits. So, homeless in his
life, Poe is still beating about like the Flying
Dutchman, ever seeking and always denied a har-
bour in his country-people’s hearts.
Poe had, of course, a part in this tragedy of
errors and misconceptions—but, as I should
judge, an entirely honorable one. There are
three excellent ways in which a man can
get himself disliked by his fellows: he may
stand aloof from them, he may indulge
in the practice of irony, and he may be
“ever right, Menenius, ever right.” Poe was an
offender in all these respects. He never seems to
have had an intimate friend —anyone who could
do for him what Hamlet craved of Horatio with
his dying breath. Somebody said of Calhoun that
he looked like one who had lost the power of com-
municating with his fellow beings. A like spell
of isolation is upon Poe. Wanting in humor, he
sometimes tried to range his mind with others by
the use of irony : or he assumed an air which I
suppose he thought that of a man of the world,
but which is quite detestable. He wrote an essay
on Diddling as an exact science, and people
jumped to the conclusion that he was Jeremy him-
self in person. He took a grim delight in scenes
of horror, and people imagined he acted them in
life. “The Raven ” has been described as an
utterance of remorse. Remorse for what? I have
read everything that has been gathered about
Poe, and I cannot, for my life, imagine him as
anything but a stainless and chivalrous knight.
The few, trivial, and usually unsubstantiated
smutches which microscopic industry has found
on his armor would not show at all against a
panoply less pure and white.
I remember reading an anecdote of a lieutenant
in the British Navy who entertained Byron on his
ship in the Levant. Byron was proud of his sea-
manship, and the acute officer would carefully have
something disarranged in the top hamper of the
ship before the poet came on deck in the morn-
ing. When the latter did so, he would cock his eye
aloft and immediately discover and point out the
irregularity. The lieutenant would apologise, and
have it remedied. Byron liked that lieutenant,
and men in general like those who give them
something to forgive. Poe, a logic machine, was
absolutely incapable of those pleasing flaws and.-
deficiencies _ which allow other people to have
a good opinion of themselves. He always added
up true. The tradition is that he was a drunkard.
There is not evidence enough against him to hang
a dog. All the testimony actually produced—all
the witnesses who give their names and addresses,
people who lived with him and knew him best,
deny it. That he was easily affected by liquor and
sometimes overcome by it, is possible—and what
does it matter ? That there was any debauchery
is impossible. His poverty proves it—the amount
of work he did proves it; and, most of all, the
quality of what he wrote, which grew in power
and concentration to the last. There is more
plausibility in the accusation of irregularity in
money matters. In a life so harassed as Poe’s, a
few ragged debts might easily be left. But here
again there is nothing definite. Nobody has come
forward with notes of hand or evidences of defal-
cation. On the contrary, letter after letter has come
to light showing Poe’s scrupulous exactitude about
obligations. Practically, he was cheated by almost
everyone with whom he came in contact —and then
these, to shield themselves, cried after him “Stop
thief ! ” He built up two or three magazines
for others, and when, dissatisfied with the pittance
thrown him, he designed a magazine of his own,
he was laughed at and decried. Really, my own
grievance against Poe is that he was too good. He
ought k> have taken to the road and compelled a
just tribute at the point of the pistol.
Poe’s principles of criticism are true, enough
within limits, but they are far from being the
whole truth. His lack of humor, deficient know-
ledge of human nature, and insensibility to that
side of greatness which results from mere mass,
quite incapacitated him from criticising the
mightiest works of literature. But he never
attempted such criticism ; and for the work he
had to do—the appreciation of our modem English
or American masters—he was almost infallible.
And surely no writer has ever praised his
contemporaries and rivals as he did. He seems to
have written with no thought of self, and with
a humility almost pathetic. He may be said
to have discovered Hawthorne, and he crowned
him king of the short story. His article on Bryant
is still a just estimate. The innocently imitative
quality of Longfellow’s genius offended him, but
he speaks of the New England poet otherwise with
respect, and calls him the leading poet of the
day. He fairly returned Lowell’s praise. His
enthusiasm for Tennyson was excessive: it was
idolatry. He pointed out Mrs. Browning’s faults,
but wrote of her with a fervor which no one else
has imitated. His eulogy of the singularly
neglected R. H. Horne sets one in a glow. This
high and generous appreciation of the best in
contemporary literature was coupled with a de-
cided distaste for trash —and, unfortunately, his
calling as a critic compelled him to deal more with
trash than with excellence. He wrote his Dunciad,
and after his death the dunces had their revenge.
Every one of Poe’s greater poems is a distinct
and original effort. He could not repeat himself.
In the case of the majority of poets, the style is
the same throughout—or at most they have two or
three different manners. It would not be difficult,
for example, to piece together, into a seamless whole,
portions of separate poems by Wordsworth or Ten-
nyson. But each one of Poe’s is a vital entity—
born once, and not again. He is not, in poetry, one
of those constellations which spread over half the
sky, which hold their heads in the zenith while their
skirts are obscured below the horizon, —rather, he
is a small compact cluster of stars. If we could
imagine the stars of the Pleiades differently colored
—one red, one yellow, one green, and so forth,
but each one vividly aflame in its several hue—we
should get a good image of Poe’s poetry. He is
not, like Shelley, a poet of the fourth dimension,
yet neither is he distinctly sensuous, and he fur-
nishes but few copy-book maxims or proverbial
phrases. Rather in him imagery, diction, music,
merge into one effect, as fire is a compound of a
hundred different things. His thought, too, does
not obtrude itself. He has, indeed, what I might
call the sentiment of profundity rather than
special precision of thought.
Poe’s tales seem to me the third collection in
point of merit in literature—the other two being
the “Arabian Nights” and “Boccaccio.” He
has not the humor of the one nor the human
nature of the other; but he surpasses them both
in depth and imagination, - and for originality
he is unrivalled anywhere. No one else
has opened so many paths, burst into so
many new regions of romance. Indeed, as one
sees authors all over the world painfully following
in his tracks, each one exploring a single region
which Poe discovered and dismissed m a few
pages, one feels that he was the compendium of
all possible literary pioneers and explorers—a
dozen Columbuses rolled into one.
There is a small group of Poe’s tales, usually
f assed over, which is worth a moment’s mention.
t consists of “ The Power of Words,” “The Col-
loquy of Monos and Una,” “The Conversation of
Eiros and Charmion,” “Shadow, a Fable,” and
“Silence, a Parable.” They are not wanting in a
certain alloy of De Quinceyism which at times
mars Poe’s style of perfect plainness ; but they are
singularly impressive in thought. They have that
manner or sentiment of profundity which I have
spoken of, more even than his poems; and they
lead up to Poe’s final work, “ Eureka.”
“ Eureka ” has, I judge, been less read than
anything else Poe wrote. Certainly it bas
been little discussed. The average critic pro-
bably finds it difficult to place, and so lets it
alone. It is difficult to place. It is too scien-
tific for rhapsody —too plain for mysticism ;
and yet it is. hardly either science or meta-
physics. It might be tersely described as the
ideas of Spinoza in the language of Newton. Poe
as a thinker resembles those old Greek philosophers
—Pythagoras, Parmenides, or Empedocles—who
chanted in verses their luminous guesses as to the
origin and constitution of things, without troubling
themselves as to any analysis of their knowledge.
Coleridge said of Spinoza that if It rather than I
was the central faot of existence, Spinoza would be
right, It and not I was the basis of the Pre-
Socratio Greek thinkers ; and perhaps our moat
modern philosophy has the same foundation.
Schopenhauer’s substitution of Will for conscious-
ness as the final fact, and the Darwinian theory,
both tend that way. Without knowing anything
of Schopenhauer, and anterior to Darwin, Poe’s
thought also tends that way. He has noth-
ing of the mathematical pedantry of Spinoza,
and of course none of the immense scientific
detail of the evolutionists ; but I do not see
why his guess is not as good as theirs. In one
very startling idea he seems to have been antici-
pated. Deducing that the Universe is finite—
mainly because laws cannot be conceived to exist
in the unlimited—he goes on to say there may yet
exist other worlds and other universes, each in the
bosom of its own private and peculiar God. Car-
dinal Newman is authority for the statement that
Franklin used to dally with this idea in conversa-
tion. Poe, while in Philadelphia, may possibly
have heard of Franklin’s speculation. I can recall
nothing like it elsewhere.
I have not space to follow Poe into the other
spheres of his intellectual activity—into his studies
in Landscape Gardening and Household Decoration,
on Versification and the Philosophy of Composition,
and much else. Poe, in my judgment, was the great-
est intellect America has produced—assuredly the
best artist. He reminds me of a sower stalking
down a furrow and scatteringbroadcast seed which
a multitude of crows attendant upon him appro-
priate to their own use and behoof without a single
croak of thanks. In a crude new world, a spirit
was born to whom even the old world, where time
has mellowed and enriched men’s lives by layer
on layer of myth and metaphysic, drift after drift
of legend and history, decay above decay
of citadels and cities and empires—to whom
even this soil and surrounding would have
seemed harsh and strange. The crude new
world could make nothing of this spirit, except
that it was not worth while to waste good provi-
sions on such an uninvited guest, and that it was
best to huddle him into his grave with lies. But
enough ! The little that Poe got is gone. The
much that he gave remains—a glory forever.
CHARLES LEONARD MOORE, in Chicago Dial.
The New Ogilvic.
Roderic Quinn writes regarding the new edition
of Will Ogilvie’s “ Fair Girls and Gray Horses ”
Ogilvie, like Gordon and Daley, is the child of
association. He lives under alien skies, but his
mind is old-world. He is racy of our soil and
something more besides, because no poet worth a fig
is only racy of the soil. He uses Australia
as a canvas for recollections begotten before he
saw these shores, but he would have found
as good a canvas under other stars. And,
talking about stars, his poetry is as strewn
with them as Richter’s path of dream. He
has the Outer Vision in perfection, the faculty of
landscape painting and surface description. But
more than with these poetry concerns itself with
Inner Vision, sounds that appeal to the Listening
Ear a,nd sights that are seen by the Seeing Eye.
In this, too, Ogilvie does not lack.
One day on the Barrier, when the glass had
climbed above the century and the country through
which I travelled began to glitter like broken
glass, I came on a garden green with English
grass. The mulga ridges round about were
crowned with brown, and a bastard mallee in the
near distance drooped sick leaves earthward. A
sense that for countless generations had rested
and refreshed itself on emerald hill - sides
leapt to the spot of old-world green and for the
moment my eyes were glad as a tired fellow who
quits the dusty road to lie awhile in a meadow of
rich clover. Now, the mulga is green after a
certain fashion, and the mailee greener, but green-
est of all the quandong, when it is in season and
the fruit hang among its leaves like many rubies.
But these trees are green with a different green to
those of the old world, and perhaps I am wrong,
but I do not think they affect us quite as happily.
Thus, when an artist or a poet tells me he is going
to England, I do not believe that it is altogether
fame or coin that is calling him. The thought is
sometimes with me that ancient conditions are
sununoning him to their embrace, and that, per-
haps, he desires to see an English lane in June.
When our fathers came here their minds were
hung with pictures of lassies, maids and colleens,
some with a sprig of heather in their hair, some
with a rose there, and the rest with Irish sham-
rocks : not an emotion but suggested something
sweet and green and scented, or something sour
and grey and ugly ; every passion in an appropriate
setting. The pictures passed with them (alas, that
mental possessions are not heritable, like landed
ones !) and we, under new conditions, are some-
times hard put to it to fitly stage the ancient
drama.
Ogilvie knows what old-world green is, and he
knows the Australian green-of-grey, but he is at
best when dealing with themes tinted by the
former. “ The Bush, My Lover,” is a fascinating
poem, but is it quite as true, as convincing, as the
villanelle a half-dozen poems further on :
Last night in Memory’s boughs aswing
When none but I had power to hear,
A wee brown mavis tried to sing.
But ah ! the wild notes would not ring
As once they rang, so loud and clear 1
Last night in Memory 's boughs aswing.
I saw the rowan-clusters cling,
And far away and yet so near
A wee brown mavis tried to sing.
The wee brown mavis and the rowan clusters,
these are only names to us who have not been out
of Australia. In writing this villanelle, one might
have imagined Memory’s boughs, but which of us
born far from where he builds His nest would have
conceived the wee brown mavis clinging there with
tense, chill claws ? Some of us might have heard
or read of the association of bird and bush, but in
that the idea was gained from mouth of man or
book of man would we be poetically honest in
using it ? And if we do not use the mavis, what
bird is left for us to use ? A score of birds occur
to one—birds which we know and are beginning
to love, but none of them will cling so fitly as the
wee brown mavis. I would like very much to
have a mavis and a rowan bush in my garden. I
think, nay I am almost sure, that I would give a
gum-tree and a parrot for them.
Ogilvie is sentiment and recollection. He sings
from the heart, gladdening us like clear water and
fresh air. He is never gloomy. Sunlight and
starlight forever enrich his singing and charm his
sighs. Though like Gordon in his Arabian love of
the horse, he never attempts that ecstasy of the
damned soul which at times lifts Gordon so
sublimely.
Out of the new poems in the new Book of Ogil-
vie, and in the “Fair Girls’” section, read me
“Gold Tresses ” and say how sweet it is. Then for
color and sparkle, followed fast by sadness, take
“ Perhaps To-night.” Of the new “Gray Horses ”
“The Silent Squadron” impresses me most. It
is a. picture of dead men who ride with a vaoant
saddle in their midst—
They keep my place
In their ghostly ranks; and I catch my breath 1
Yet hand to rein
There are better men
Riding to-night with the Steeds of Death.
It is in “Other Verses,” however, that the second
edition lords it over the first. It is in other
verses, also, that Ogilvie is always at his best.
Remember “Rainbows and Witches,” “To a
Bunch of Heather,” and “Bowmont Water.”
Amongst the new pieces are the Villanelle quoted
from, “ The Bush, My Lover,” and “ The City of
Gray Griefs” all of them beloved. “Fairy
Tales ” deserves a sentence to itself.
And now, having read Ogilvie once, you must
read him twice, but when you have read him
twice you will not need reminding to read him
again, but you will be so altogether charmed with
him that his book will be very constantly at your
elbow, like a good friend who tells you pleasant
news.
Lee’s Shakespeare.
Arthur H. Adams writes some stray im-
pressions ot Shakespeare the man, suggested
by Sidney Lee’s new “Life ”
How many people know that Will had three
brothers and a sister ? One is curious about the
obscure lives of these members of his family: what
were they doing while elder brother Bill was coin-
ing money out of a new play called Hamlet? Gil-
bert Shakespeare seems to have done nothing but
live to a patriarchal age, while Richard and Ed-
mond, the latter having followed “the profession,”
each died before reaching 30 years. It is hard to
imagine that anybody could be a brother of
William and not show signs of extraordinary
talent; but probably the Shakespeare stock could
support only its one splendid blossom. Genius
flowers only once, and then the plant is deli-
berately rooted out and thrown on the dust-heap.
The poet’s only son, Hammet, died young, but of
his two daughters, Judith lived to the age of 77,
and—note the sterility of the exhausted stock-
survived all her family; while Susannah, who was
reported to be “witty above her sex,” married
twice, but died without issue. The dream-
children which Shakespeare created had drained
the vitality from those mortal children that were
also his.
Very curious must the poet’s home-life have
been. Forced into a marriage with Anne Hathaway
at the age of 18&, it was only by long absences in
London that Will was able to get along with a
wife ten years older than himself. Two Shottery
husbandmen considerately secured a deed—a kind
of legal Elizabethan “ Why don’t you marry the
girl V’ —to compel the adventurous butchers ap-
prentice to save the reputation of the mature
Anne ; and six months after the marriage Susannah
Shakespeare was born. Will seems to have made
the best of a bad job—the probabilities point to
Will being the pigeon who was plucked by an
Anne old enough to know better—and he left his
wife much alone till middle-age suggested the com-
fort to be found on a quiet hearth-stone. Then, a
rich man, he returned to his native village. But
again one wants to know whether Anne was
ever dimly conscious of the fact that she had
fot for a husband the greatest poet in the world?
'robably not—witness the poet’s best bedstead.
And the mother ? Did she guess her wild son’s
inherent greatness, or did she rather reprove his
adventurous spirit, and regret that her Will had
not settled down quietly to the butchering busi-
ness, where he could have done a solid cash trade
behind the block ? I fancy that during the family’s
troublous times of debt and disaster, Mistress
Shakespeare, senior, must have often deplored to
her next-door neighbor the promptings which had
led her eldest son to go to London town and be-
come a pestiferous player. I am sure that she
never could be persuaded to visit a theatre even to
see her son in Ins most successful pieces.
In one way Will’s greatness seems to have been
thrust upon him—by a sudden departure from
his native village consequent on a little poaching
affair on the preserves of Sir Thos. Lucy. What
a pity that they did not transport for poaching in
those days; for undiscovered Australia might
have seen the gentle Will editor of the First Fleet
Herald, or have had the pleasure of watching some
Wilson Barrett of thelGth century acting “Ham-
let ” for the firs.t time on any stage —one half
Wilson and the other half Barrett.
The return of the gentle Will to his native town
must have astonished the Stratford-on-Avonians.
After an absence of 11 years, he suddenly dawned
on the villagers as the generous reliever of his
father’s pecuniary difficulties, and the purchaser
of the largest house in the town—and, in brief,
Will Shakespeare of Stratford-on-Avon, gentle-
man I Then he applied for a coat-of-arms, and,
after a long struggle and a series of large ■ heraldic
lies, he got it. The “ country-gentleman ” Tenny-
son fades besides the aristocratic leanings of the
country’s greatest poet. Will knew what he was
about, and his creditors knew that if they owed
him money the gentle Will would extract his
pound of flesh to the last ounce in the village law-
courts.
Another hitherto unemphasised fact in Shake-
speare’s life is his enormous riches. His income
during the latter part of his life was £6OO a year,
which, according to the purchasing power of money
in those days, was equivalent to an income of
£4BOO at the present time. Will’s income was
chiefly due to his position in his profession, and to
the fact that he held some shares in the Globe
Theatre. It is grievous to relate that for his
actual literary work he got only a moderate re-
venue. He “pursued literature ” from the secure
vantage of a large professional income. Yet his
plays were immensely popular at the outset; and
the applause of their first nights must have been
something to remember. Hamlet, especially,
was a huge success.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“In truth it may be said,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ that the
latest addition to the House of Lords, in the per-
son of Earl Poulett (pronounced Pawlet), has been
dragged out of the gutter. It was in the clammy
murkiness of Islington, but a few yards from the
‘Angel’—that world-famous ‘pub.’ that the
reporter found the organ-grinder peer, and broke
the news to him of his father’s death. For years
Viscount Hinton has ground his barrel-organ all
over London, and been really dependent on the
coppers of the crowd. A card proclaimed his
name and the reigning earl’s unfatherly neglect of
him. The new' ‘ herl ’ looks like a ‘ gone-to-the-
deuoity ’ masher, speaks Cockney thick, chews
cigarettes continually, and has a frail, effeminate
appearance—his most aristocratic point. His
father married his mother because lie had bet
,£5OO he would wed the first woman he met on his
return to England from a trip. The son arrived
six months after the ceremony, and the fight now
will be as to whether the earl or an outsider
‘anticipated the starter.’ The ‘estate’ is worth
many thousands a year, and has the patronage of
three clerical ‘livings ’; the title is two centuries
old. Earl Poulett, when clown at the ‘Surrey,’
married the columbine who now becomes the
countess. So many peers have gone into the gut-
ter that it’s only fair one should come from there
occasionally.”
The Eastern Queen.
There dwells a lady in a land of beauty,
Green are her sandals and a-glance with flowers ;
Her fair face shames the countenance of Duty,
Oft winning men to lazy love for hours.
Sometime in purple vesture doth she dally
To watch her great gold suitor disappear,
And sometimes flocks of white sky-sheep doth
rally,—
Her shepherd winds low-laughing in the rear.
Twain wooers hath she —one a royal rover,
With lovely largesse of down-falling gold ;
And one a dark, shy, silent, silky lover,
Who wraps him round in sable velvet fold.
Day after day with various, violet garland,
And wreathed roses hearted like Red Mars,
The first one robes her, and from stilly starland
The second brings a coronal of stars.
Her minion train, alert to bring her pleasure,
Heaps high her green lap with a laughing host,
While all day long, in deep, delicious measure,
The minstrel sea harps to a golden coast.
Alas ! all things fade fast with our forgetting,
And now that green queen-face seems pale and
dim ;
For, 10, it chanced one day in crimson setting
The red sun summoned and I followed him.
Poolamacca, N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
In the Pace of the Dead.
“ Not the least of Life’s ironies is that there is no
earthly rapture comparable to the joy in the face of the
dead.”
The artist wins plaudits by showing
The loveliest prize of Earth’s race ;
His Helen with young life is glowing,
All human hopes summed in her face.
His name would be borne o’er the oceans,
His fame to the poles would be spread,
Could he add to her play of emotions
The joy in the face of the dead!
Enthroned by the love of a nation
The actor rings clear in his part,
The gamut from grief to elation,
His face is transformed by his art.
What lacks in his strong histrionic
Appeals to the heart and the head ?
Whispers Death, with hoarse accent sardonic,
“ The joy in the face of the dead ! ”
The jockey is thrilled by the thunder,
Sweet as peace after fever and fret,
That hails his great win as a wonder ;
His price above rubies is set.
His face blazons forth his glad story
Whence triumph exultant is shed,
Yet its brightness is dulled by the glory
Of the joy in the face of the dead.
Enthralled by white arms, clinging kisses,
The lover quaffs passion’s strong wine,
Yet, sweet as the draught is, he misses
A joy he can never define.
The rose out of Eden, the fairest,
Would come, with Love’s secret flushed red,
Could he find in the eyes of his dearest
The joy in the face of the dead !
Vic.
ETHEL CASTILLA.
A Dirge.
Come not with sundered flowers to strew her
grave,
Nor be there any curtain but the grass,
Dewed by the night, and by the winds that pass,
Tranced with the slumber of the level wave ;
Or if one cloud of the empyrean nave
Shall float a shadow on her shrouded face,
Be it the shrine of this mysterious place,
Bestowing shelter she for ever gave ;
And if the anthem of this holy rood
Fall from the throat of some forgotten bird,
Faint with the press of heaven upon his wings,
Be it the bruised fragrance that is stirred
In the sad heart, remembering happier things,
That are the angels of this solitude.
HUBERT CHURCH.

yankee Doodle
IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Hoodie was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, MARCH 18, 1899.
The Unification Ghost.
A few years ago, when long Dibbs was at
the head of affairs in New South Wales and
Butcher Patterson was making an incredible
mess of things Victorian, the former submit-
ted to the latter his famous scheme for the
unification of the two provinces. It fell flat
for the sufficient reason that Butcher Pat-
terson wasn't capable of recognising a good
thing when he saw it. A brighter man in
Patterson’s position would have snatched at
the idea with both hands and as many feet as
he had, and driven the long, stumbling
Dibbs into at least an effort to carry it out.
He would not only have snatched at the idea,
but he would have spent his days and nights
on Dibbs’s doorstep, and followed him to the
hotel where he had his ginger-beer, and
called to him through the window while he
was in bed, in order to make sure that he
didn’t forget about it. These remarks are
made because there are only two methods
of Australian union—Federation and unifica-
tion ; and the unquiet bones of the latter
idea are again stirring a little. The lengthy
ex-Republican is digging them up once more,
and presentingtheir bleached fragments to the
public ; and, while opposing all idea of
Federation, the little Dibbs clan and the anti-
Federationists of the N.S.W. Council assert
that theiryearning for unification is something
awful. Also, they begin to beseech the Corn-
stalk people to reject Federation in order
tha,t it may get the greater blessing of unifi-
cation later on. The Bulletin believes
cordially in unification if it could be ob-
tained, but it believes in Federation because
it doesn’t see the slightest hope of getting
the other thing. And its private conviction
is that the Dibbs clan believes in neither of
them, and that it only urges the people to
reject Federation now for the sake of unifi-
cation later on, because it knows that it can’t
possibly get unification later on. Also that
the said crowd, if it saw unification really
coming, would do anything short of murder
to prevent it. In other words, it asks the
country to drop the bone for the sake of the
shadow, because it knows perfectly well that
the shadow isn’t there.
* » * *
If Brown Patterson had managed to
seize and fix his fleeting Dibbs a few years
ago, and the proposed union had come off,
the situation would have been something as
follows : Both Victoria and New South
Wales would have vanished off the map, and
there would have arisen in their stead one
province called something or other—the
name doesn’t matter. There would have
been one Governor, one Ministry, one Par-
liament, one capital, railway system, debt,
deficiency, tariff, and one borrow. There
would have been no border, and the Murray
would have flowed through one province in-
stead of between two. There would no
longer have been any differential railway
rates. Melbourne, instead of being the big
city of a province of 80,000 square miles,
would become the biggest city of a joint
province of 400,000 square miles. Sydney,
instead of being the biggest city of
a great province, would be the second city
of a province not very much greater.
Sydney and Melbourne would have com-
peted on absolutely even terms for the
trade of the country lying between them, for
the railway rates and all other circumstances
would be even, and, in the main, the nearest
city would get the business every time. And
then, so far as Sydney was concerned, the
real excitement would begin.
* • * *
The half-way town between the two big
cities is Junee, in New South Wales. South
of that, when differential rates and borders
and border tariffs and separate railway
managements were abolished, all the
trade would naturally gravitate Mel-
bournewards. So would a very large part
of the western trade. Instead of there
being a large province with its centre
in Sydney and a very small one with
its centre in Melbourne, there would be one
huge province, about half of which would
regard Melbourne as its natural centre.
Also Melbourne, instead of Sydney, Would
be the city which a good half of the popula-
tion would regard as its commercial head-
quarters. Instead of a political boundary on
the Murray, there would be a kind of unde-
fined commercial boundary at Junee, on one
side of which all business would tend south,
while on the other side it would tend north.
South of Junee all new railways would point
Melbourne wards, for a fairly commanding
vote in the joint Parliament would belong to
that part of the population whose com-
mercial interests lay by the Yarra, and it
would object to paying extra charges to go
a longer way round to Sydney when it could
take a shorter road to Melbourne. And if
the joint province divided again any time in
the future the new border would never be at
the Murray as the old one was. The people
of the whole State would have a voice in the
matter, and they would fix the frontier
according to their convenience. It might,
in the event of a unification and a second
separation, be on the Murrumbidgee, but
that would be all that New South Wales
could hope for.
* * * *
This is how things would be if New
South Wales and Victoria were unified. If
all Australia was similarly treated, then not
only would Melbourne become the trading
and social centre of all the country as far as
the Murrumbidgee and even beyond it, but
Adelaide would silently annex a vast slab of
western, and Brisbane a considerable piece
of northern, N.S.W. There would be no
violent upheaval or disruption in the pro-
cess. But in the absence of borders and
border tariffs and differential railway rates
and separate railway managements, trade
would drift to the nearest city, and the
people whose trade went there would natur-
ally come to regard that nearest city as their
capital in a social sense, and to forget the
one which used to be their headquarters in the
separation days. All this would be good for
the south and west and north. It would
give these districts a shorter road to the sea ;
it would put them close to cities that are now
inastate of artificial remoteness, and, by bring-
ing them closer to greatcentres, itwould make
places rich that are now poor, and bring
population where there is now only ’possum
and native bear. But, while it would be
good for the country, it would probably be
not at all good for Sydney. Under Federa-
tion the people of the Riverina can’t join
Victoria, however much they might want to
do so. The possible 150,000 who might de-
sire to separate would be voted down by the
1,200,000 other Cornstalks who would refuse,
like Pharaoh, to let them go. The 1,100,000
inhabitants of Victoria who would like to
shift their border north to the Murrumbidgee
wouldn’t count; they would have no voice
in the matter. But if there was unification,
and years afterwards a new separation move-
ment arose, these latter would count. On
the present figures probably 1,250,000
voters from the Northern Riverina to the
ocean would vote for casting in their lot with
Melbourne, and their decision would fix the
new boundary 100 miles north of where it is
now. And, even if the unification proved
permanent and there never was a new Sepa-
ration movement, Sydney would never again
be the commercial centre of so large a terri-
tory as at present. Geography is against it,
and fno argument will turn 386 miles into
293.
• * ♦ m
Therefore, when a little clan of the bit-
terest Provincialists and counter-jumpers in
New South Wales—men to whom Sydney
and its trading interests are the beginning
and middle and end of the universe—profess
to advocate unification as against Federation,
this paper, for one, doesn’t believe them.
And when they suggest that the present
chance of Federation should be sacrificed in
the hope of getting unification later on, they
are painfully suggestive of the confidence
gentleman at the public-house corner—the
man with the rich uncle, just dead, in Fiji.
Probably, after all, Butcher Patterson
wasn’t such a fool as he looked when he let
Dibbs’s invitation to unify pass practically
unnoticed. It is 1000 to 1 that Dibbs never
really meant it, after all. If he is anything
like the sort of Dibbs whose long figure
wanders around to-day, he certainly didn’t.
The Crown Defender.
The legal expenses of Burgess’ defence, at Toowoomba
(Q.), were paid by a struggling selector near that place.
To the average individual such a statement as the
above conveys no meaning in particular, yet
beneath the surface it concentrates and sum-
marises.a whole volume of injustice and cruelty,
and a bitter curse on the judicial system of Aus-
tralia. The men who have been wrongfully con-
victed and imprisoned in Australia number many.
Those who have incurred tremendous expense in
addition to great mental anxiety in clearing them-
selves of unfounded accusations may be numbered
by hundreds. And those still more unfortunate
wretches who, having no means, were incapable
of incurring expense, and so have taken what
chances they might get and battled through—
successfully or otherwise—as best they could, are
more numerous and more to be pitied still, It
is not only a want of common justice but
a hideous want of common-sense on the
part of the community that permits this
system to continue. Any man is liable to find
himself any day an object of suspicion with re-
spect to some crime or alleged crime. Scores of
cases have occurred in recent years where men
have been charged with crimes which not only did
they not commit, but which Were never committed
by anybody, and, if they succeed in proving their
innocence, a Weary and ironical community merely
remarks over its beer that they were lucky, tb get
off. It does not seem to have occurred to the
community aforesaid that it alone is directly re-
sponsible for these outrages upon some of its indi-
vidual members. For it is just as much the duty
of a people, through its Government, to provide
the money and legal machinery for the defence of
accused persons as it is to provide means for their
prosecution and punishment. The history of this
as well as of every other nation shows it to have
been exceptionally alert to find means for the pro-
secution of the accused, but the proper defence of
the said accused is nobody’s business. It is one of
those ideas for which the country “is not yet
ripe. ”
* * * *
If a murder is committed ; or if a giddy damsel
in short skirts tearfully makes complaint of some
bold, bad man ; or if a paunchy citizen, proceeding
homewards with somewhat uneven gait of an
evening, worries the weary policeman, resting his
generous feet on the suburban pavement, with a
dream of violent robbery; the law skirmishes
round impetuously until it arrests somebody.
Often it arrests the handiest available man who
seems to be nobody in particular, or to have no
friends. Then the whole police and detective
force is available to make the man fit the crime,
and should that consummation seem at all likely,
sundry law clerks and a Crown Solicitor arid a
Crown Prosecutor are also available to further
work up and prepare the case for a Judge
and jury. Meanwhile, the accused is care-
fully shut up. If he has no money he can’t
hire a solicitor to defend him, or to hunt up evi-
dence in his favor. He can’t get out to hunt it up
himself. He isn’t allowed to see newspapers, so
that he doesn’t know what evidence he has to con-
fute ; a little of it certainly becomes known to
him when the police are procuring his committal,
but only a little. Therefore he doesn’t know what
witnesses he should call to demonstrate his inno-
cence. If he is a penniless man, and no strug-
gling selector puts up the money to hire the
necessary lawyer, he has no one whom he can
send to interview these witnesses and collect
their evidence—except, perhaps, his enemies, the
police, and their interest lies rather in getting his
witnesses out of the way. For if the prisoner does
demonstrate his innocence, then the police are
thereby found guilty of being a set of incapables,
who have arrested the wrong man and let the
right man have abundant time to get away. Until
close up to the time of his trial, in fact, the penni-
less prisoner has no semblance of a defence, and
then he often has only a semblance. A per-
functory and poorly-paid (if paid at all) lawyer
is provided at the last moment only in
case of murder, be it noted; in other cases
prisoner has to go undefended. The perfunctory
lawyer has no time or money to scour the country
looking up possible witnesses and working up the
details of his case, nor has he any police or detec-
tive force to do it for him. As often as not he
merely cross-examines the witnesses for the prose-
cution, and says that the prisoner has an alibi, and
there the perfunctory business ends.
* » * •
What the great perfunctory community seems
utterly unable to realise is that it wants for
its own protection a Crown Defence Department
as elaborate as the Department of the Crown
Prosecutor, supplied with money, legal talent,
a detective force of its own to hunt up evi-
dence for the defence, and all the other
accessories of the business. In this matter the
public can’t trust the police, for the public’s
interests and those of the police are not identical.
When a sensational crime has been committed the
interests of the force lie in getting a conviction
against somebody. The average bobby, being
human, would rather convict the right man than
the wrong one. Still, if he is too stupid to find
the right man, he has to find somebody, if he can ;
his reputation, his prospects of promotion, of
kudos, of increased salary, are all at stake. If he
fail very often he may even lose his miserable
billet as an ordinary constable ; and, so far as the
interests of the police (as apart from its con-
science) are concerned, it answers quite as well to
get the wrong man and persuade the public that
he is the right one, as to get the actual criminal.
But the public interest lies in quite a different
direction. If there is a homicidal maniac
about, the public wants the right maniac
caught and caged, so that it won’t have
its skull staved in as it goes home at night.
The policeman may get as much promotion
for capturing some harmless nomad and convincing
a jury that he is the real homicide, but that doesn’t
make the public’s skull any safer. It would be
better for the public that twenty policemen were
“fired ” for incapacity, providing the twenty-first
caught the real criminal, than that the first bobby
was promoted for hanging the wrong suspect, and
the real danger was left at large. But it would
not be better for the twenty policemen.
• * * *
And similarly when the short-skirted damsel of
romance tells her hysterical tale of assault, the
detective’s most obvious interest lies in coiivictin?
somebody of the alleged assault. The public’s
doesn t at least not always. If the story is true,
the male public wants to find the real assaulter if
it takes ten years to do it, for the safety of ’its
female belongings. If it isn’t true, it wants the
short-skirted romancer put away in a safe place
lest she brings a similar charge some day against
Itself. In short, what best pays the police is to
get somebody convicted—the wrong man if the
right one can’t be found. And the only thing that
pays the public is to convict the right man, or else
get the detectives who can’t find the right man
fired out for incompetence.
* * * •
Every time the police get a conviction against
the wrong person they let the right person escape.
If there was a real crime they let a real criminal
escape ; if there was no real crime they let a false
accuser escape. In either case the escapee is a
public danger. And for that reason the public
wants a State Defence Department to be a check
on the police, and to do its best to present the case
for the accused, and to prevent the police getting
convictions against the innocent and thereby
securing the escape of the guilty. In this nutter
Burgess is a case in point. For a little while,
when only the police case was known, it looked
more than even betting that Burgess would be
hanged. If there had been no struggling selector
to do what a State Defence Department should
have done, and provide him with legal aid and some-
one to look up witnesses for him, it is quite possible
he would have been hanged—whereas now it is
nearly certain that he is innocent. It would have
been to the profit of the police to convict and hang
Burgess in any case ; whether he was innocent Or
guilty, they would thereby gain the credit <qf
solving a great mystery, and save themselves much
further labor, and escape the further gibes bf the
press and the public. It was rtot tp the profit Of
the community to hang Burgess if he was inhO-
cebt, even if the police walked .themselves into
permanent cripples hiiiiting for the real offender
h,nd the jeers of the press drove them into a lunatic
asyluni; for then the public would still be liable
to be mysteriously butchered at night while driving
hoiAe from Gatton, and the public doesn’t want to
be mysteriously bu tchered. That wide discrepancy
bf interests is just where it comes in that the
public badly want a thoroughly-equipped State
Defence Department—and the police badly don’t.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Poems of Jones.
Sydney Telegraph has invented something
new :
On the 28th July last .... the electors of N.S. Wales,
by a practically unanimous vote, declared that certain
principles of the Convention Bill were such as, even for
the sake of Federation, this colony should not accept.
Let’s see. On that occasion Reid went to the
country as boss of the New Bill party and
Barton as leader of the Old Billites. And
Reid lost three of his colleagues in the practi-
cally unanimous vote, and all his majority ; he
came back with his personal following so badly
licked that his own party and the Labor men
together only showed about four votes more
than the Barton crowd alone. There is the
germ of a great idea hereabouts. Some day
the present writer will start a new daily paper,
and run the Telegraph for the Ananias cham-
pionship. Ho will persistently state that a
short, stout person named Gibbs was Premier
from 1891 to 1896, and that he was then suc-
ceeded by Pumpkin Smith—John Haynes and
Gormly and David Storey having ineffectually
attempted to form a Ministry amid great
tumult, during which Parliament House was
set on fire on eight occasions. And thereafter
Pumpkin Smith went to England and be-
came Prime Minister there. As for Reid he
will simply be mentioned as the man who used
to clean up Parliament House and scrub the
front steps. The writer is thinking of invent-
ing a perfectly new Premier, to be called
William Jenkins Crump, K.C.M.G., and, by
persistently alluding to the many years covered
by his six successive Premierships and all the
bad things done therein, he will probably make
Crump a much-detested historical character
in the end, and efface Parkes altogether.
When a paper goes in for inventing new his*
tory, it is as well to do it on a grand scale, not
merely go pottering around with small inven-
tions. The new organ will talk enthusiastically
about the great things that were done under
the Hogsflesh Ministry, till N.S.W. will at
last begin to believe that there really was a
Hogsflesh Ministry once, and will go round
looking for Hogsflesh to form another Ministry
like it and save the country again. And it
will point out that the only Australian poet
ever worth reading was Samuel Jones, and
hire clever people to write extracts from the
soul-stirring lines of Jones till the booksellers
will be besieged with demands for Jones’s un-
published volume. There is a great future for
the organ which invents its history and its
politics and its murders and its literary idols
as it goes along—if the thing is only done well.
But the D.T.’s half-hearted way of doing a
small, mean invention here and there has no
grit in it.
N.S.W. Travelling-Stock Reserves.
Ed. Bulletin. —Riding down a Western river
one hot day last year, I met 12,000 sheep.
They were not pleasant to behold, and their
march was something like Napoleon’s from
Moscow, for many dead and dying littered the
bare lane behind. The 12,000 were divided
into three classes. The strongest were in the
lead ; then a feebler division ; and, lastly, the
“ hospital,” a smaller mob, with a bigger
“spread” and a longer “tail,” consisting of
the utterly destitute. The latter drifted piti-
fully through a mirage, a mile behind the second
mob, and were obviously doomed—young sheep
—mostly maiden ewes and four - tooth
wethers —some of the best from a big Bar-
won run where they breed good sheep.
Drover stated that they were travelling for
grass, and that he hoped to get some
above Narromine. Meanwhile, was keeping
the mob alive on myall and wilga, doing three-
mile stages. Pointed to a belt of hazy scrub


across the plain, and said he hoped to stay there
a day, because he knew of old that it was a
good, thick scrub, and plenty of it. Having
but just come through it, however, I was a > e
to tell him that two small boys were actively
engaged lopping as much as they could get a •
for about two thousand sheep, the properly o
an adjacent “cocky”; whereupon the drover
got down from his horse blaspheming furiously,
and stating his opinion unflatteringly or t le
Deity, the country, the chief inspector, the
drought, and the Government. But most un-
flatteringly of the Government. This by way ot
introduction. Now, it is the law of the roads that
every man travelling thereon must look after
himself. To get “feed” —if possible, legally,
but still to get it—is the instinct of self-pre-
servation. No man who is anything of a man
can see his stock starve without exhausting
every legal and illegal means in his power to
provide them with food. He will cut fences ;
he will argue wordily that a man’s house-pad-
dock, or his wheatfield, is a reserve ; he will
get his sheep “ boxed ” with the station sheep.
He will do all that he can to save his charges,
and, personally, will be thought little the less
of, even by the men whose grass he raids.
Without animus, they will proceed to make
existence a burden for the drover. They will
hunt him in to the half-mile, and threaten
him with the Courts, and bring him
back to burn his dead, and chase him
with summonses, and lay informations
against him, and deal it out to him generally.
That is their idea of self-preservation. There
is no need of the little tin-plate, with T.S.R.
painted on it, that the C.I. causes to be nailed
to the trees in order to let you know that there
lies a stock route. It is only necessary to look
about you. You cannot confound a T.S.R.
with a cultivation-paddock. The point of that
cocky’s feeding his sheep upon the wilgas of
the Reserve was that he was within his rights,
for he holds a lease of the land from the
Government. So why shouldn’t he take what
he can get out of it ? Nearly everyone con-
cerned is agreed that the leasing system is a
bad one. But what to do ? Whether the
Reserves are leased or not they are invariably
eaten out, and, if unleased, generally
more so. The lessee takes all he can
get for his money, but at the same
time does not want to see the country
utterly destroyed by indiscriminate scrub-
cutting, etc. When there is no lessee, all the
neighborhood swoops down on the least ves-
tige of grass, and there is no one to prevent
damage and destruction. A suggestion was
before the Central Board of Advice of the Stock
Boards to the effect that a rate should be levied
on all travelling stock, from the proceeds of
which caretakers should be paid to look after
the reserves and generally prevent abuses.
That is better than nothing, but why should
not the general revenue be called upon to pro-
vide funds for the carrying out of such a
scheme as this ? The humble but necessary
sheep keeps the wolf from the Australian door,
and the encouragement he gets for so doing is
about as helpful as a word of advice to a
drowning man. There are scores of things
that the pampered province of N.S. Wales
could better do without than an efficient system
of supervising the T.S.R’s. There are the
Premier, the Chief Inspector of Stock, the
George-street tramway, the Victoria Markets.
But we eat mutton, and we wear wool, and we
buy drinks and other necessaries of life with
the money that other people give us for these
commodities—and we starve and neglect our
stock, seeking a mark' >r an existence, because
we don’t see them coming, or are too stupid to
recognise that it does not pay to overlook them,
or too callous to understand that it is miserable
cruelty.—A.

A Cry from Par Back.
Far-out Wilcannia, sick of placing dependence
upon the River Darling, w'hich seems to have
been more frequently unnavigable during the
last half-dozen years than for the previous
generation, has renewed the agitation for the
extension of the railway from Cobar. Cobar’s
sudden mining activity was a bad knock to
Wilcannia. The Nyngan to Cobar line was
authorised and built as a section of a railway
via Wilcannia to Broken Hill. But when the
section became payable in itself the idea that
it was a section was lost. Wilcannia, there-
fore, is now a voice crying in the wilderness ;
and until the drought breaks up its principal
occupation will consist of privately cutting
mulga for its starving stock and publicly de-
claring what a splendid district it has got hold
of. The Wilcannia line is described in the
Sydney dailies as “impossible,” and it is just
about impossible on the present system of rail-
way construction. A possible railway is one
that will pay about per cent, to cover the
interest on the money borrowed in England,
and while it is an article of the Australian
faith that the money for railway construction
must be borrowed, and that it is a blighting,
soul-destroying sin to think of building rail-
ways without borrowing the money, most of
the lines that Australia badly wants are im-
possible. If N.S. W. proceeded to raise another
•£1,500,000 a year revenue, however, for public
works purposes—as that lightly-taxed province
could easily do—the situation would be entirely
different. The Wilcannia line would be a
cheap one to construct. The construction of it
would find a lot of better work for the unem-
ployed than sand-shifting and rail-painting.
It would, probably, pay 1 per cent, on the
cost of construction. It would add to the
value of local lands and thereby increase the
land-tax revenue and the revenue of the Lands
Department. It would enable a lot more
people to live in the district, which would mean
more income-tax, and Customs and excise, and
postal and telegraphic, and probate revenue
and dog-tax, and fines for being drunk and
other income. Among them all the line would
probably pay, directly and indirectly, fully 2\
per cent. And this, combined with the fact
that every increase of population, and every
increase in the means of communication, make
the country stronger for defence, would almost
certainly make the Wilcannia line worth
building—with N.S. W.’s own money. But it
is difficult to see how it would, either directly
or indirectly, pay the full interest on borrowed
■, i„„,v iih borrowing is Australia s
corn pie te"gospel Wilcannia’s prospects are the
thin edge of a shadow.
Truckling to tie 'Tecs.
Police influence in the law-courts would be
Sin the soul of the Victorian Democracy
just now if that docile body hadeerasoul to
agitate. Two or three weeks back Ihe Bulle-
tin published a complaint from the would-be
prosecutor of conspirators Dungey and Brom-
LEY that two leading Melbourne barristers had
refused a brief “ as they say they cannot afford
to offend the police.” Last week a glaring: case
of legal truckling to the ’tecs occurred at Melb.
General Sessions. A young man was charged
with stealing a watch under extraordinary and
decidedly hazy circumstances, for it was not
even proved that the man who missed his
watch had lost it whilst the prisoner was in
the same building with him. Two detectives
made the arrest and they swore that the
prisoner said to them, “ I don’t think you 11 find
the watch ; it’s in a boiling-down shop —which
suggestion would have been by no means an
admission of guilt, anyway. Also, the detec-
tives stated they tried to trick the prisoner
into a confession by saying—“ Mr. Nettleshlp
does not wish to prosecute if he gets his watch
back ... if you can get the watch no doubt it
will do' a great deal in your favor, and we
might be able to have you dealt with at the
lower court.” All of which the prisoner de-
clared to be false. The story of the conversa-
tion between himself and the self-alleged in-
quisitors was a fabrication, said the prisoner,
and it would seem that the jury believed him,
for, after a short retirement, they found him
not guilty. During the jury’s brief absence
Judge Casey took the opportunity to reprimand
the detectives for having—on their own show-
ing-attempted to bounce prisoner into an
incriminating statement. Therefore the detec-
tives stood between two fires whilst the court
was waiting for the verdict. The accused man
charged them with bearing false witness against
him : the Judge charged them with being re-
prehensible “ traps ” if they had told the truth.
Here, then, was an opportunity for the pri-
soner’s lawyer to make the best of the situation
for his client by laying much stress upon the
fact that his client had accused the two police-
men of perjury. Or, if the lawyer didn t care
to make this point, he might have preserved a
diplomatic silence, instead of which he spoke
as follows :—“ If I may be permitted, your
Honor, I would like to say I’m quite convinced,
from what I know of these two detectives, that
there was no wrong intent in what they did—it
may have been done through excess of zeal, but
that is all. Your Honor willrecollect that I didn’t
refer to this phase of the case ”11! ! The unen-
lightened reader will naturally wonder why the
lawyer hastened to make himself so pleasant to
the detectives whom his client had denounced
as perjurers. And the explanation—theonly con-
ceivable explanation —is well worth noting. De-
tectives are to a considerable extent bestowers of
patronage upon police-court lawyers. Thej 7
advise prisoners and prosecutors in the matter
of engaging lawyers, and consequently these
learned amalgams and barristers “ can’t afford
to offend the police.”
The Painful Heretic.
One among the many—the awful many—of
Melbourne’s Presbyterian parsons happens to
be before the general godless public just now
on a charge of heresy, and the g-g.p., in its
usual fat-headed manner, barracks for the
alleged martyr on the supposition that he is a
fine, broad-thinking fellow who has seen the
error of his absurd dogmas and become truly
penitent. When a hard-shell church starts to
“persecute” one of its preachers for not
preaching and teaching strictly according to
contract, the outside worldlings rarely give a
thought to the real merits of the case. Know-
ing the “ persecutors ” to be full of arrogant
bigotry and theological foolishness, the ordinary
sinner sympathises with their victim and en-
courages him to hold tight to his “ noble
cause ” by cutting small jokes at the expense
of the church that wants to shunt him. Never-
theless, the Heretic, as a rule, is a rather
more ridiculous religionist than the unyield-
ing professors of musty dogma. There
is method in their argument, whereas he is
clumsily endeavoring to reconcile certain
reasonable beliefs which have formed them-
selves in his mind with a number of hazy
theories which he wishes to hold sacred in his
suppositious soul. Orthodox religion states, in
effect, that God wrote—or, in any case, edited
—the Scriptures for the so-called Christian
world. Heretical parsons state, with equal
positiveness, that God didn’t quite exactly
write all the Scriptures, but merely intended
them to give a sort of general idea of the Truth
in such a way that it would be open to various
constructions. Heretic parsons are not less
wise than orthodox Scripture-idolators in their
own conceit. Take the Melbourne heretic of
the moment. He is accused of dabbling in the
doctrine of purgatory, such dabbling being un-
doubtedly a breach of contract in his case.
When asked to explain the meaning of his
dabble, the self-satisfied apostle tootled as
follows :
God in His mercy may find ways to extend salvation to
those who are outside the ordinary pale of salvation. If
the savages, therefore, are to be saved, there must be a
spiritual world. The savages are not fit for Heaven at
once, because nothing unclean can enter there. If they
go neither to Heaven nor Hell, the Presbytery, perhaps,
will be good enough to tell me where they go.
The perky heretic is just as cocksure of his new
terewth as the dour old gentlemen are cocksure
of their old terewth. He knows all about the
manner in which Christ rose from the dead,
and can bring seven points along to prove his
case, he says' He has an infallible system for
getting anybody to Heaven, or Hell, or the
other place. He can draw up a plan with the
pale of salvation outlined in red ink if neces-
sary. In short, the evangelical heretic is a
parson of the most dangerous description.

The Cabbage-Tree Creed.
The loud cry of the anti-Billious Dibbs, at last
week’s anti-Billious meeting in the Australia
Hotel, Sydney, “I, for one, refuse to trust
Victoria,” is the whole gospel of the Cab-
bage-Tree Mob in a sentence. Ever since the
days when old Jack Robertson created the
Cabbage-Tree cult there has been, to it, only
two places in the world—its own place and
Victoria. In its short and simple creed every
bad thing that happens is owing somehow to
the amazing cleverness and world - wide
machinations of Victoria. Queensland and
Tasmania put almost prohibitive duties on
N.S.W. coal, anJ Jack Want assured an excited
anti-Billious meeting that the conduct of Vic-
toria in shutting out N.S.W. coal was simply
shameful. When Queensland took the unpre-
cedented step of putting a heavy export duty
on wool to prevent it going to N.S.W. ports,
the general impression was that Victoria was
at it again ; and the fact that Queensland, at
Separation time, quietly repudiated its share of
the common debt has made the Cabbage-Tree
Mob feel ever since that Victoria would
swindle anybody and grab anything. The rivers
question, wherein South Australia battled with
N.S.W. for joint control over the streams
which feed her only decent river, the Lower
Murray, was another thing which showed the
grasping nature of the Cabbage-Garden pro-
vince in the worst possible light; and the
heavy tariffs of Queensland and Tasmania —
about the most heavily-tariffed provinces of
Australia—have made the N.S.W. Foreign-
trader hold forth about the evils of Victorian
Protectionism till he nearly burst with fury.
Even Queensland’s insistance on having a
separate mail-steamer service which wouldn’t
touch any N.S. W. port moved one Cabbage-Tree
orator to state that Victoria’s futile attempt,
some 20 years ago, to establish a similar service
was a most disgraceful proceeding. The why
of all this undignified terror lest Victoria
should do something dreadful to its big neigh-
bor is very hard to comprehend, but somehow
the southern state is to the aboriginal Dibbs
and Want crowd what the bunyip was to
the previous aborigine. Victoria is a very
small place, just about one-fourth the size of
N.S.W. Through sheer want of space it is
bound to fall very far behind in the matter of
population. About a fourth of the little space
it has is unusable and practically uninhabit-
able, which makes it still smaller. It is far
inferior to N.S.W. in mineral resources, which
gives the old province a further advantage in
the scramble for population. Considering all
these circumstances, the unmanly terror of the
Dibbs crowd of anti-Billites lest the little place
next door should hit them is a trifle ridiculous.
And their constant scream about the virtues
of their own province is suggestive of the in-
toxicated lady with her hat over her eye, who
deafens the street with her cries that she is a
“puffick lydy and has always behyved as such.”
One Turnip.
Sydney Telegraph’s newest provincialist
bogey :
WHAT FEDERATION WILL MEAN.
Letter of a Queensland resident.
Brisbane, Friday.—A letter was received this mornina:
by a merchant of this city from Mr. R. M. Stewart, of
Messrs. Scott, Dawson, and Stewart, dated Sydney,
March 8, in which Mr. Stewart says“ I have just
heard this morning, through Mr. A. B. Webster, of
Messrs. Webster and Co., merchants, Brisbane, that Vic-
toria and the other colonies have no intention of admit-
ting our sugar or meat free in case of Federation. We
had it from a man who is one of the three, I think, of the
committee appointed to report on the financial part of
Federation ; so he ought to know. If this is the case,
what are we to get for the extinction of our wine, wheat,
and farming industries? It is stated that Sir George
Turner has spoken with no uncertain sound on the sub-
ject of these duties. If so, the sooner the people of
Queensland ascertain the facts the better. It is also
stated by this expert that New South Wales cannot join
if Queensland holds out. I hope it is not true about the
sugar and meat, although I am assured it is. But, if
true, our Government and people should get at the truth
before it is too late.”
As a tozer, that effort isn’t worthy of the
Telegraph. Seeing that the Federal Constitu-
tion abolishes all interprovincial duties, and,
under Federation, “ Victoria and the other
colonies” can’t prevent Queensland meat
and sugar coming in free, the statement
that they are going to do it all the same
should have been backed up by better evi-
dence. There is something very unconvinc-
ing about the statement that an alleged man,
name unknown, in Brisbane, received a letter
from Stewart, in Sydney, saying that
Stewart just heard from Webster in Brisbane
that he thinks Webster has heard from a man
who was on the Federal Finance Com-
mittee (name not given) that something
impossible was going to happen. And how
they all knew it was because “it is stated,”
by persons not mentioned, that Premier
Turner (who will be only one individual in
the Federal Parliament after all) had said
it to persons not mentioned, though no one
else seems to have heard him doing so. At
all events, the Queensland farming industries
have got on all right despite the duties levied
by the other separated provinces ; though the
news that Stewart received from Webster
assumes that the same duties, if they could be
levied by the federated provinces (which they
can’t), would mean extinction. Under which
circumstances the unknown person in Brisbane
wants to know if the letter that Stewart wrote
him about what the other unnamed individual
told Webster he had heard parties unknown
say that Turner said is a terrifying fact or
only a hollow turnip with a candle inside?
The Bulletin reckons he may rest in peace.
It is only a turnip—and not even a plausible
turnip at that.

Queensmongreland Elections.
The first batch of the Q. elections —duly faked
in the interests of the Ministerial Animal with
the movable head and immortal boodle body
instead of a soul —came off last week, and re-
sulted in the “ overwhelming triumph” of
the Animal, as arranged for. For the electoral
rolls have been so elaborately “ purged, and
the constituencies have been so heavily bribed,
and the “ first batch ” was so judgmatically
chosen, and Q. public spirit and public con-
science are so dead and fly-blown, that ten times
the “ triumph ” might not unreasonably
have beeu looked for. The seats won
for the Ministry were all held by that
nondescript Animal previously, with one
exception. Labor lost one seat, at Brisbane
South, where Turley gave place to Luya—
thanks to vote-association with a nonc-too-
popular pal. Labor gained four seats —at
Cairns, where the traitor Hoolan was beaten
by Givens ; at Fortitude Valley, where
WoRKER-editor Higgs replaced M'Master ;
and at Gympie, where two Labor-men, Fisher
and Rylands, took the Ministerial seats by
a small majority. Hoolan will presumably
now scoot back to Burke, where he came from,
and where there ought to be enough white
manhood to reject him in spite of his ingratiat-
ing bluster. For the defeated at Gympie and
Fortitude Valley there is no hope, unless the
contingent vote helps. It is certain that the
Ministerial Animal will see its strength con-
siderably reduced when the elections close on
March 25, though unluckily not enough to
ensure it being thrown out on the dunghill to
rot as it deserves. After a dozen years of dirty
politics Q. public life has become so corrupt
that three-fifths of the people are not fit to
carry on “representative government.” The
country wants an honest Bismarck to despotise
it for a dozen years, till the decomposing old
generation is wiped out.
Barton Keeps Reid in Office.
The spectacle—rare in Australia, not un-
common in England —of the Premier being kept
in office by the Leader of the Opposition for the
good of the country, was last week witnessed
in N.S. Wales. Now that there is a reasonable
prospect of Federation really arriving (if Aus-
tralians come out of their apathetic tents and
fight for it, as they will most assuredly need to
fight), all the anti-national forces are being
brought to bear upon Parliament; and, since
Reid espouses Federation, a determined effort
is being made to throw Reid overboard.
Sydney Daily Telegraph, champion of Sydney
monopoly at the expense of N.S. Wales and
Australia, is working all the harder because it is
as yet working underground, and has not had
the courage to come out in the open as the
enemy of the nation. Federation frightens the
Sydney-property-owning shareholders of the
Telegr.aph directly, because it will mean pos-
tage on newspapers, and the Telegraph syn-
dicate’s private enterprise will no longer be
subsidised out of taxpayers’ pockets. It
frightens them indirectly because anything
which tends to loosen Sydney importers octo-
pus-grip on N.S. Wales will tend to lessen
their rents and reduce their dividends. If
Sydney Telegraph can help it, the Sydney
property - owners will not let one sucker
go. And Federation —the triumph of Aus-
tralia—has struck fear into the bowels of
the mean-souled little mediocrities who com-
prise three-fourths of N.S. W. Parliament —the
men on the make and the men on the keep ;
who see themselves shorn of influence and
reduced in numbers and salary—at ,a big saving
to the community, indeed, but to the lasting
loss of the politician who lives on the game.
So last week saw the singular sight of the
Ministerial party endeavoring to betray its
chief, and prevented solely because Oppo-
sition - leader Barton refused to take one
step to office at the cost of imperilling
Federation. The shifts and antics of the squirm-
ing Anti-Federalists were grotesque indeed.
“ We can’t move a censure on Reid,” they
said, “ but for God’s sake let the Opposition
move. Any excuse will do—so long as the
country is hoodwinked. Jack Wants trip
the city railway—take any mortal pretext
and we’ll support you.” And the Opposition
wouldn’t—thanks to Barton ; though some
Opposition-men would only have been too glad.
So now the traitor Reidites, at their wits end,
are colloguing with the Telegraph and the
Leg. Council, and praying for any stone, any
stick, to beat the National dog with—any ex-
cuse to prevent the Federal question from going
to a fair vote of the people.
Yet more extraordinary disclosures about the
treatment of Westralian niggers by their sup-
posed protectors. At Roebourne, John Her-
bert Church, J.P., a squatter, of course, was
charged with having assaulted and half-mur-
dered one of his natives, “
Church struck him on the forehead, and then
hit him in the mouth with a heavy whip-
handle, splitting the poor devil’s lip and
breaking a tooth —
The native was covered with blood, and the medical
testimony was that he had on the right temple a wound
three-quarters of an inch long and about a quarter of an
inch deep, a jagged cut on the lip, a broken tooth, and
an abrasion of the chin. The defence was that the native
had been neglectful and impertinent, and that defendant
would not have struck him had he not thought that he
was about to be struck by the native. Defendant was
fined Is., with costs (fcl 6s. 6d. in all). The native was
then charged by Mr. J. H. Church, J.P., with a breach of
contract, and with having absented himself from woi k,
and was sentenced to two months’ imprisonment, with
hard labor.
In a word, Church, J.P., is let off with Is.
fine for half-killing a blackfellow who in turn
gets two months for a breach of contract with
his inhuman “protector.” Only a month pre-
viously two ot her squatter J.P s, in the same
district, were fined £5 each for the same
offence ; but Church was tried before a brother
of one of those previously prosecuted.
The Anti-Billious party in N.S.W. is trying
its hardest to rouse up the animosity of Syd-
ney against the country districts over the 100-
mile limit. There are 680,000 people inside the
100-mile limit, it says, and 666,000 in the
parts of N.S. VY. outside it, and so “ half the
population of the province has been deliberately
shut out from having the capital among them.”
And what, in the name of goodness, has become
of the old hypocrisy of the apostles of “ Decen-
tralisation ” about “opening up the interior ”
and “ attracting population to the country ”
and “settling the people on the land ? ” Is it
all hypocrisy"? The capital will attract popu-
lation,'therefore it should be put where popu-
lation is wanted, not where there is too much
already. Though the capital itself will be on
Federal territory, still the N.S.W. country
around it will fill up with people, and accumu-
late wealth, and make the province richer and
greater. Does the fat hog want all the grease,
so that it can’t spare any of it for its own full
brother, the thin hog ?
