Bijou Rickards Variety Co.
The “ Shadows of a Great City ” tailed out
its shuddery length at Sydney Royal on Saturday
and the people there assembled appeared to
receive it with much satisfaction. The
“Shadows” is a good old-fashioned melodrama
with that slight flavor of decay about it which
belongs to all old melodrama; and it isn’t more
ridiculous than the average. All melodrama is a
S’ddy joke, more or less. The escape of William
ourlay, who is in for stealing a watch, and
Inman, who is falsely accused of prigging jewellery,
when they burst out of their paper dungeon, looks
effective enough. The former is found in the
river and taken back to finish his sentence, but
Inman gets away with a wet infant in his arms,
and lives happily for 15 years, just adjacent to the
gaol, without being discovered. The wet infant
grows up to be a nice, kissable woman, and seeing
that she and the man are deeply in love with each
other the story might just as well end there. But
all this time the person who had annexed the wet
infant’s money, and ejected her mother, and put
the diamonds in the good man’s pocket, is living
in ill-gotten affluence, and the author thinks it
necessary to demolish him, which is done labori-
ously and rather clumsily, and with many compli-
cations, and in a way that is a dreadful shock to
Mr. Gourlay’s nerves. That person being out of
gaol again, and drunk, and very much bowed in
the legs, goes burgling the safe of his old partner
in crime, and when he gets the door open there
falls perpendicularly out of it a long straight white
object that drops on the burglar and then drops off
the burglar on to the floor. It is the heiress who
had bean put in there to die of starvation, and Mr.
Gourlay knows her and flies howling, for she is
the same wet infant that he agreed to drown for
cash 15 years previously—the night when he was
found in the river and taken back to finish his
sentence. Just about here it transpires that
Inman, who has again been put in prison over the
diamonds, and has torn a hole in his dungeon and
escaped a second time, never stole them after all,
and it is agreed not to put the heiress back in the
safe any more. Then the hero starts the weary
old tag to the effect that the sun is again shining
and the calf wobbles over the green grass as of yore,
and the clouds, etc. —but the rush of the feet of
the escaping audience drowns the good old saying.
The audience loathes that tag.
* * * *
A certain Biddy Rooney, played by J. P.
O’Neill, is the brightest character in the
“j3hadows.” O’Neill is a rich, oily, Irish come-
dian, with a mouth whose vastness would take in
a gum-tree crossways, and as a comedy Irish-
woman his only rivai, within this writer’s experi-
ence, is John F. Sheridan—the Widow O’Brien.
Biddy is a big, promiscuous woman, who flies
round on the outskirts of the tale to no definite
Eurpose, but it wouldn’t be much of a tale without
er. Her chief occupation consists in losing the
wet baby, and letting out boats with a hole in
them. John Gourlay, the person whose legs have
got warped in gaol, varies between good comedy
and wild burlesque. John is a spidery person of
immense activity, with a tendency to overdo his
spideriness. Robert Inman does a steady,
respectable piece of work as the hero, but there is
nothing large or lurid or remarkable about
Inman; and Miss Ida Gresham, as the damp
infant grown up, is a nice girl all through —much
too nice to fall out of any safe on to so dilapidated
a burglar as William Gourlay. Making due allow-
ance for the fact that the rest of theco. —especially
Geo. Chalmers —does its work well enough, and
that the drama is carefully staged, and isn’t more
foolish than the average drama, and that Biddy is
a fortune in herself, the verdict is favorable on the
whole.
• • • *
The Holloway co. closes up at the Royal
on the 18th, and reopens at the Lyceum on Ist
April, when proceedings commence with a
perfectly new drama, “ Our Guardian Angel. ” In
the meantime, that rather decayed establishment
is being scoured, and washed, and scrubbed, and
boiled, and disinfected, and painted, and re-
carpeted, and the insect which some pre-
vious patrons forgot to take away with them is
being extirpated. Also, the old drop-scene,
with the saddening boot-advt. on it, has been
scorned and rejected, and will appear on the
premises no more.
«* * *
The ever-welcome Dante returned to
Sydney Palace on Saturday night, and with him
came his Burnt Woman and his other mysteries,
all of which got a warm reception. His pro-
gramme contains a number of new items,
each of them cleverly and neatly done, but the
incinerated female is still the prize feature of the
bill, and when that combusted lady blazes up
suddenly and leaves only a bone and a skull and a
tatter behind her, the audience feels that it has
got good value for its money. Sydney has come
to associate the frizzled sister with Dante’s show,
and if the fried person wasn’t there it would pro-
«k ,
bably rise up and demand its conflagrated virgin,
or its money back. The principal difference
between the present show and the last one is that
the affable American who used to stand by the
gaudy portal and be a long-lost brother—through
his nose—to the passing pressman is there
no longer. Instead, he is going on something
awful in the law-courts, where he is demand-
ing injunctions and conjectures and objurgation!
and such-like against the professor. It is under-
stood that he charges Dante with breach of con-
tract, or dynamite, or criminal procrastination
with violence, or something of the kind ; at any
rate, there are expensive law proceedings between
them, and, at time of writing, the parties file 15 or
16 affidavits against each other daily. Barrister
Knox, on behalf of Dante, assures the court that,
“We have no intention of leaving Sydney ” to
evade anybody—which is the curious barristerial
way of putting things. Knox speaks as if he
were the Burnt Woman in person—which he isn’t.
But why Dante, with his remarkable capacity for
making things disappear into nothing or appear
out of nothing, doesn’t simply meet the American
gentleman in the street, and shake something at
im, and make him vanish —all but his bell-topper,
which would be found unaccountably on the Post
Office tower —and then make a new and friendly
American out of the ruins, is a question that wants
answering.
#* * *
“ The Geisha ” is now well on its fifth
week at Her Majesty’s, and the public books its
seats a week in advance very much as it did five
weeks ago. If the Firm has no other engagements
in the way, and can play out the Japanese show
to the bitter end, it seems possible that that
jumpy little trifle will put up a record for the
longest run in Australia. Last week this writer
mentioned that he knew fat, responsible people—
the kind of people who want the land-tax taken
off and who believe in putting all the unemployed
in gaol—who had been to see “The Geisha” as
many as six times. One of these parties went
twice more last week, which makes eight times.
In the face of such proceedings it is evidently no
use asking the cause of the boom. The sufficient
fact is that the boom is there. The loss of bright
Lily Titheradge, who has departed for the foggy
land of Bull, is a regrettable circumstance, but the
“ Geisha” seems able to survive even that.
** * *
The long-negleeted Sydney Opera House is
to open again on Saturday, March 25, under the
management of Charles Maurice, with Jules
Verne’s story, “Round the World in 80 Days.”
The Firm tried its hand at this spectacle some
years ago, and a hard frost of public disapproba-
tion set in—still, the public is a variable animal,
and that bygone snowball is no earthly reason
why the show shouldn’t boom on a second appear-
ance. There are great spectacular possibilities in
it, and it is to be produced at low prices, and the
management alleges it has gone to vast expense in
renovating the old house—though it is difficult to
imagine that any amount of renovation would lift
the gloom off the Opera House. The red and yel-
low bill contains particulars of a large co., mostly
people whose names The Bulletin never heard
before—but that is probably owing to its miser-
able ignorance. And there are a good many
characters in it that Jules Verne never mentioned
in his story, so, apparently, the book has been
renovated as well as the Opera House.
** * *
No person of any special importance has
arrived at or left Sydney Tivoli during the last
seven days, but some very pleasing girls are near-
ing the end of their engagement, and as usual some
other nice girls are about due to take their places.
Horace Wheatley and Travers and Faning are
still on hand, and the fact that Sampson no longer
breaks his dreary ironmongery at the Tiv. makes
the programme feel a good deal lighter than it did.
Sampson was a rather heavy wonder, and the
present pessimist has felt better since his de-
parture. He made him feel as if some one had
put a very wonderful and expensive fender on his
chest while he was asleep.
** * *
The Fitzgerald Brethren are also due in
Sydney on 25th March, when the big tent will go
up again in the old spot. The latest addition to
the programme is a tiger that rides on horseback
in a perfectly friendly manner, and without eating
up the horse. This is recommended as an item
specially suited to the rather dismal Good Friday
season.
*# * *
“The Brixton Burglary,” the latest of
Brough’s productions at Melb. Princess’s, on
Saturday, is yet another narrative of the respect-
able husband who gets off the chain during his
wife’s absence and shouts oysters for a gutta-percha
girl. There are two girls this time—the Bounding
Sisters of the Air—and the difference between
them and other painted heroines of farcical-comedy
suppers is that they don’t appear upon the scene.
They are merely represented by a photo, which the
harmless profligate brings home in his pocket at 7
a.m. and hides in the same old drawer of the ever-
lasting breakfast-table (farcical-comedy furniture
was ever eccentric) in order that somebody
may loot it from the drawer and use it in
evidence against him. Brough plays the
harmless profligate, and is inhumanly afraid of
his wife knowing what happened to him on the
one night when she wasn’t at home. Also,
Brough has a father-in-law who lives in terror of
his wife discovering that he went for a bike-ride
during her absence ; likewise Brough is afflicted
with a parlormaid who borrowed master’s bike in
the silent watches of the night, what time Brough’s
valet had gone razzling in his master’s clothes.
THE LATE Dr. MAURICE O’CONNOR.
The parlormaid collided with the father-in-law’s
bike on the Brixton-road, and they both ran away
to avoid the police ; therefore Brough’s bike, bear-
ing the name and address of its owner, is arrested
on suspicion of being concerned in a burglary.
* m * *
The arrivals and departures of Majeroni,
as an impossibly elegant police-sergeant, provide
a lot of necessary excitement. The aforesaid
burglary took place at the lodgings of Brough’s
friend who helped him to have supper with the
Bounding Sisters, and the father-in-law is dis-
covered wearing the friend’s clothes, which he
borrowed from Brough’s valet, or else the friend
only thought they were his clothes, but in any case
Majeroni arrests the father-in-law and releases him
in the next act, when the old gentleman swops more
garments with the valet —and all because he can’t
let his wife know that he went for a ride on his
machine. Brough meanwhile is aiding and abet-
ting his friends, relatives, and servants in all
sorts of inane difficulties arising from the bikes
and the burglary. From time to time Brough’s
timid little wife (Miss Noble) remarks that she
can’t quite understand what has happened, and
then Brough turns pale and pours forth a further
torrent of untruths—all for fear of being revealed
by his parlormaid as a husband who went out for
the evening and came back with a photo, in his
pocket. Farcical-comedy humor depends entirely
upon the assumption that a married man must
pretend to be the sort of tame Joseph that no
timid wife would tolerate. Brough, strange to
say, is not held in much subjection by mother-in-
law. She is a sprightly, bouncing person, who
reckons him 90 times wickeder than his real self,
and respects him for not having got found out.
Mother-in-law is the one touch of nature in the
show. Being buxom and festive, she objects to
her husband riding a bike, lest he should fall off
and break himself. She rules her lawful comforter
with a rod of iron, and takes great care of his
health.
• * * *
“ Theßrixton Burglary” weakens towards
the finish, and the final efforts of the author are
pathetic in their desperation. The parlor maid
has to sport the long, baggy bloomers she went
biking in, and Brough closes the list of complica-
tions by appearing in Miss Temple’s pink print
dress and apron. The Bulletin hasn’t much
faith in the future of a farce which puts a muslin
cap on Brough’s head. Besides, it isn’t smart in
dialogue, and Miss Temple is a not-sufficiently
light and airy Perkins, and the male persons in
the cast are mostly unconvincing all the time.
Miss Bessie Thompson scores in the casual part of
the mother-in-law, but Miss Noble has merely to
look nice and perplexed. A change of bill is
likely to happen shortly.
** # *
At Melb. Royal, the death of stockbroker
Brown and the disruption of Walter Baker’s air-
tube are proceeding with great vigor, and Bland
Holt’s habit of turning money away on Saturday
nights appears to grow upon him. Nevertheless,
it is understood that “ The White Heather ” will
be withdrawn in a fortnight or so. The manage-
ment has another huge drama waiting round the
corner, and this will rush to the fore at Easter
unless the public applies for an injunction to delay
Lady Godiva’s departure. All parts of “The
White Heather ” are more impressive than they
were on the opening night, but the item that has
caught on with the tightest grip is the rehearsal-
scene where the nine young daughters of a hundred
earls are instructed in the dramatic distinction
between jiggetty and wiggetty.
** * #
The Rickards show at Melb. Bijou has
shaken off the cold that onetime threatened to
settle upon its chest, and business of late has been
quite up to the old Opera House traditions. Peggy
Pryde is scoring heavily on the new pitch. The
Lorenzis, assisted by the “ Sisters ” dressed in
blue,, put in a cheerful 20 minutes with their
nautical pantomime, and Pope and Sayles seem
none the worse for wear. Moreover, things in
general look brighter since Peggy’s expansive
smile came along. The gaiety of that bustling
little patterer is in the very air of the Bijou, and
time travels quickly during the silent periods
when the stage waits for somebody’s next song.
** * *
A concert at the Town Hall on Saturday
night introduced to notice Miss Bertha Bird, a
light soprano singer who will probably follow the
lead of Miss Ada Colley, and get to Europe in due
course. As a vocal gymnast on the high trapeze
register Miss Bird is already a skilful performer,
although not a finished artist. Her singing
doesn’t appeal to the public soul, but it strikes
one with astonishment and fetches the same kind
of enthusiastic encore that is awarded to Lem-
mone’s marvels on the flute. A new contralto at
Saturday’s concert was Mrs. Leonora Bailey. Her
pleasant voice comes from England with the repu-
tation of having been born in Victoria, and it
might have grown into a larger voice if the lady
hadn’t left her native land when she was a child. 3

Wallace Brownlow has found the Westralian boom re-
coil too severe to withstand, and has quitted his Kal
tfoorlie hotel amid general regrets. He goes he doesn’t
know where for certain ; but quite likely he will soon
again begin to professionally warble. Of course the gold-
fields farewelled him handsomely.

listening to Florence Perry’s love-song in “ The Geisha”*
A typical Englishman can never show admiration till he
gives a lordly “ Thank-you ”at the finish. A Frenchman
or an Italian can evince his delight by pose and manner
right through the song.

subscriptions for the great actor.”

London County Council has sat on the Gimlet, the
Anti-Joy, the Snufflebuster, and other societies. By a
large majority Sunday concerts are to be permitted at
the Alhambra. The Council regulates prices of admission
number of hands allowed for working the house, and to a
certain extent the music. Only high-class melodies
songs, and compositions are to be given, but there is joy
amongst the musical masses, and corresponding dismay
in the camp of the plate-harpies, prudes, and holy Joes.
Will Australia ever throw off the parsonical yoke and
follow suit ?
Last file of London D.T. has a timely interview of Sar-
dou re the play of Robespierre, which he is writing for
Irving. Sardou has more relics of Robespierre than any-
one else, even including Robespierre’s autograph note to
warrant the execution of Danton, with remarks upon it
penned by Saint Just. He has exactly located the humble
place in Paris where Robespierre, at the height of his
power, lodged with a carpenter, and this will be reprot
duced on the Lyceum stage. Coleridge wrote a blank-
verse play entitled “ Robespierre.” It was not this, bu-
Coleridge’s “ Remorse,” which Phil Beck recited complete,
without book, in London, before a distinguished assem-
blage, including Chief-Justice Coleridge.
Ed. Bulletin.— Be “faculty of distinguishing musical
pitch.” For 30 years I have had a close business acquaint-
ance with professional musicians in London and Aus-
tralia, yet I have only met three who could at once tell
the pitch of a note, blindfolded, without adventitious
assistance, and these three could name not only single
notes but discords of several notes struck simultaneously.
The “ infant Mozart,” of Melbourne, a prodigy of 7or 8,
was one. Probably Mr. Kenningham could tell a some-
what similar tale. “ B.” is wholly at sea when he states
that this rare faculty can be imparted. I happened to
possess this gift at the age of 6, when barely master of
the five-finger exercises, and so, with aforesaid experience
in business, can speak with some show of authority.-

Purser Dennis, whose death at Port Said was lately
cabled, was for many years in the Orient service. He was
a most excellent officer, and a tenor voice of great range
and unusual clearness gave him a strong claim on lady-
passengers. In ’97, when purser of the Austral, outward-
bound, Mr. Dennis and a well-known Westralian had a
set-to in the first saloon. The Westralian had got more
Moet than he eould carry, and the late purser, more than
he wanted, as his band was broken.
A terrible incident was the death of Wm. Sinrfjson,
comedian, at Broken Hill, last Saturday night. His cos-
tume caught fire as he jumped over the footlights, and
he was roasted to death.
“X - Ray ” : There is reason to believe that the
reports that Clement Scott intends lecturing in
Australia are unfounded. Those who heard his
lecture in England some years ago on “Thirty
Years of the Drama” agree that it was about
the sickest thing in lectures they ever endured. He
would certainly be a huge frost here. Besides, he would
find when he got back to London that he had lost more
ground than he could make up in many years. Even
Sala, a much cleverer man, found that, after his long
Australian tour. There are so many first-class young and
vigorous writers waiting for chances in London that an
old, jaded stager who drops out for a time is apt to find
his footprints on the sand filled up.
On a sweltering day, 100-and-the-rest in the shade, in
Rockhampton, Q., some members of an opera company
made a visit to the swimming-baths, and were all broken
up to read —“ Closed during the winter months.” Lends
color to the yarn that a Rockhampton man wanted a
door in Sheol kept closed, on account of the draught.
The members of an opera troupe were en voyage from
Havre to New Orleans. They complained to the manager
that five of them were leading tenors. “ Oh,” says he,
“ the yellow fever may kill two of you in a week, and two
more while we are rehearsing.”

Actually happened to a well-known Sydney profes-
sional. Pianist (to aspiring amateur vocalist): “ What
key shall I accompany you in ? ” A.A.Y. : “ Oh, it doesn’t
matter. My voice is always the same. However, you

the piano.”
In Dr. Maurice O’Connor, for many years
medical officer of Darlinghurst prison, there died
last week, at the early age of 42, a man who,
under a somewhat irresponsive manner, possessed
much generosity of heart. Dr. O’Connor, who
came prominently under public notice as the friend
and physician of the late Premier Parkes, had a
yet stronger claim to public regard in the manful
way in which he ever maintained his right to treat
convict-prisoners in the gaol hospital on as liberal
a scale as that on which he would treat his
own private patients outside. The remains of the
deceased were followed to the grave, at Waverley
Cemetery, by many friends.

The splendid Compound Condensing Engines
lately made for the Ballarat Woollen Mills by the Austral
Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne, have been
formally started on their useful career by the Hon. Com-
missioner of Customs, and christened “The Progress.
The engines are fitted with a modified Lulzer type of
valve gear, and indicate 700 horse-power, the fly-wheel
weighs 16 tons, and the total weight of the engines are up-
wards of 120 tons. They are now working in a most satis-
factory manner. The Otis Co. deserves every credit for
turning out such a fine sample of the modern steam-
engine.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
This is how Dr. MacLaurin, M.L.C., the Anti-
Billite boss, professes to prove the impossibility the
Federal financial scheme (daily paper report): —‘ ‘ Dr.
MacLaurin came to the conclusion that ‘ without that
abominable book-keeping system’ a tariff of £7,717,000
would suffice to cover all requirements, and he then pro-
ceeded to show how, under the book-keeping system, a
tariff of £7,717,000 would affect the different States. New
South Wales would have a surplus of £1,011,000 more
than she needed, but all the other States would have
deficiencies: South Australia of £65,000, Tasmania of
£136,000, Queensland of £187,000, Victoria of £228,000,
and West Australia of £395,000. To make good those
deficiencies would run the tariff a long way over an im-
possible £9,000,000, and he held that to impose direct
taxation to make them good would be virtually impos-
sible likewise. That brings out the utterly unworkable
nature of the financial compact entered into.”
As a matter of fact, the figures in question show
beyond a doubt that the Federal financial scheme is
simple and perfectly feasible. Westralia, the worst way
the Anti-Bill man can show things, would be £395,000
a year short of its last year’s revenue. Well, since last
financial year closed Westralia’s revenue has declined at
the rate of £315,000 a year, and the expenditure has been
cut down to the extent of £185,000 in the last two months
—£92,500 a month. Westralia has got to face the loss of
revenue whether it federates or not, and it is doing so.
And W.A. has neither land nor income nor dividend tax
at present; so it has plenty of means with which to make
up the deficit. Tasmania, when interprovincial duties are
abolished, is to be £136,000 short of last year’s revenue.
The island can raise £20,000 a year by increasing its land-
tax from to Id. in the £, and another £25,000, or
perhaps £30,000, by raising its maximum probate duty on
large estates from 3 to 10 per cent.—the latter being the
maximum in five of the other provinces. And last year Tas-
mania had a surplus of about £90,000. Among these mat-
ters, its £136,000 shortage can be provided for as easily as
falling off a log. Queensland, it is estimated, may want
another £187,000. The banana State has neither land nor
income tax, and can raise £300,000 under these heads
quite easily to make up for its diminished customs
revenue. Victoria, it is assumed, will want £228,000 to
make up for the loss of interprovincial customs revenue, and
to keep its income up to last year’s level. Victoria at present
levies no land-tax on estates of less than 640 acres, and
none even then unless the estate is worth over £2500 ; also
if a man has a dozen 640-acre estates worth £2400 each,
they all go free if they are more than five miles apart—
therefore all its valuable town and city properties go free.
It can easily raise another £200,000 by re-organising its
land-tax on N.S.W. principles. Moreover, last financial year
its ordinary expenditure was £208,000 less than its ordi-
nary revenue, which leaves another big margin. And in
the eight months since then the revenue has increased
£218,000 —which is a third margin of no small size. If
N.S.W. has, as alleged, a million surplus, that will just
about enable it to pay the regular expenditure on roads
and bridges and the like unproductive works out of
revenue instead of charging them to loans, so it
has nothing to lament about. It needs that million
badly enough. South Australia’s little prospective de-
ficit under Federation of £65,000 is the only one that
looks like a real difficulty, and that is the smallest trifle of
the lot. The Federal Govt, has absolute power to levy
uniform land and income taxes and probate duties if the
States won’t raise the needful direct taxation themselves,
so there is absolutely no difficulty in the way. The people
who pick dismal holes in the Federal money scheme al-
ways start by remarking that Queensland and W.A.,
which have practically no direct taxation, are so heavily
burdened with direct taxation that they can’t stand any
more, and anything can be made to look true if the pre-
liminary lie isn’t inquired into. With sufficient lack of
inquiry into the preliminary hypotheses, The Bulletin
could demonstrate that Dr. MacLaurin, M.L.0., is the five
Books of Moses.

One very curious feature about the Reid
Government’s hastily-muddled-together Advances to
Settlers Bill is the proviso that every Govt, loan shall “ be
a charge on the land in respect of which the advance was
made in priority to any encumbrance, transfer, or deal-
ing.” There are some quaint possibilities here. If the
Bill said that the State would only lend on land that was
unencumbered so that it would have a first mortgage, or
that any previous encumbrances should be paid off out of
the State loan so as to make the latter a first mortgage,
the position would be intelligible enough. But it doesn’t.
It merely says that the State loan will be a first mortgage.
So far as the exact letter of the Bill goes, a man with £250
worth of land and a £2OO mortgage on it may still borrow
£2OO on his land from the State. The State’s
mortgage will be a first mortgage, and the previous
first mortgage will become a second mortgage, and
therefore absolutely worthless, and the previous mort-
gagee may call to the four cold winds for his
money. Whatever the Reid Government meant, that is
how its Bill puts things. Another remarkable point is
that the Bill doesn’t say that advances will be made to
half or two-fhircjs or three-quarters the value of the
property—it says nothing about that. So far as the Bill
is concerned advances may be made to three times the
value of the property. This matter may be provided for
in the “ regulations ” to be made by tbe Governor, but
there is nothing on the subject in the Bill itself. The
business is to be looked after by a Board, and its fees are
not fixed in the Bill—they are likewise left to the Gover-
nor. And very careful arrangements seem to be made
to prevent the public knowinsr how the scheme pays or
doesn’t pay. The simple method would be for the State
to raise the £500,000 and entrust it to the Board, the
Board to collect the interest from borrowers and pay
interest to bondholders, and if the interest received from
borrowers doesn’t cover that due to bondholders the
deficit to be a charge on the revenue. But instead the
interest on the £500,000 will simply be part of the
ordinary payment on account of the public debt, and the
interest' received from borrowers will be paid into the
Treasury, and thus no one is likely to notice very
definitely how things are getting on.

Montgomerie’s Brewery Co. (Meib.) finished the
year to 31st December, 1899, with a loss of £4194. home
of the recent fortunes and misfortunes of this co. have
been as follows : r r ., ci . A
June,’92 .. Profit £3520 Dec.,’96 .. Loss £1660

June,’94 .. ,> 922 Dec., 97 .. » j

Montgomerie’s Brewery does a very small business the
entire sales for the ‘ year were £9640, and the re-
ceipts from all sources £10,847. And it is staggering
under a fearful paid-up capital of £141,000 the results
of ridiculous inflation in the boom-days—so that even it
all its sales were clear profit it couldn’t pay 7 per cent,
dividends. Also, it has an overdraft of ±75,158--so if it
made 10 per cent, clear profit on its output, instead of
making a heavy loss, it would take 75 3 ears to pay
off the overdraft, and then there would be a prospect of
about 15s. per cent, per annum for shareholders.
Apart from the fact that there seems no possible
hope of Montgomerie’s Brewery 00. covering its expenses
unless it can at least double its output, and very little
show of earning l a 5 per cent, dividend unless it can mul-
tiply its output tenfold, the main troubles are that the
assets are visibly £37,402 less than tiie liabilities. This is
made up of the accumulated shortage of £10,720, and
two purely bogus assets—“ Incidental purchase expenses,
£1682,” and “Goodwill, £25,000.” It takes an awful
amount of assurance to put down the good-will of such a
concern as worth anything, let alone £25,000; few solvent
individuals would take over the concern and its liabilities
at a gift. Another £156,000 of assets are put down at
what they cost in the good days that are gone—
probably they are now worth about ±70,000.
Taking one consideration with another, the con-
cern doesn’t look as if it was worth the £ 1 5,000
overdraft, and the whole capital appears to he lost. And
it is such a very small affair, doing a business of barely
£2OO a week, and tottering under a capital suited to a
brewery of ten times the dimensions, that there is very
little hope for it. The management thinks of trying to
recover by process of law some of the money paid to the
promoters when they passed their obscure little brewery
on to the confiding co., but in most cases of the kind the
promoters have long since lost their profits in other
“ cats,” and law proceedings are usually a throwing of
good bottled beer after bad.
Broken Hill Water Supply Co. realised a profit
of £15,760 (partly, it would seem, by making no allow-
ance for depreciation) for the half-year ended 31st Decem-
ber, 1898. This is the best half-year’s return up to date
as shown by the following list of recent profits :
June, 1894 £7,844 December, 1896 £10,077
December, 1894 5,076 June, 1897 13,365
June, 1895 4,882 December, 1897 15,705
December, 1895 12,901 June, 1898 11,779
June, 1896 6,686 December, 1898 15,760
The usual dividend at 10 per cent, per annum absorbs
£9325, and the other £6435, added to previous accumula-
tions, leaves £24,956 at credit of profit and loss. This
seems a very appropriate time to suggest that the B.H.
Water 00. should do a huge writing-off by way of putting
its accounts straight. The second largest item among its
assets is a purely paper one—“ Water Bights, Leases,
Parliamentary Bills, etc., cost £25,194.” These in-
clude all manner of expenses incurred in engineer-
ing the co.’s scheme though Parliament, getting its
leases in fact, all unavoidable “grease” that has
to be done to get a big concession. There are no
assets to show for all this money, not even anything per-
manent in the way of goodwill, for the co. has only a
comparatively short-dated concession of its rights and
leases, and when it expires the lease won’t be worth good
waste-paper price by reason of the accumulated germs
and finger-marks on the documents. For a whole lot of
reasons, that fictitious “Parliamentary Bills” item of
£25,194 should be wholly written off, and the profit-
balance of £24,956, with a few pounds from next half-
year’s profits, would just suffice to do it. And after that
the co. should start a steady writing-down of its other
assets. The biggest of them, “Construction account,
&c., £158,768,” is still put in at cost, with no allowance
for depreciation, and everything in this world depreciates
more or less —generally a great deal more. And, apart
from the hard facts of the case, a co. which strains things
in this way in order to show large paper-profits doesn’t
know what is good for it. It is these same large paper-
profits which make people dream of starting a rival
system, or getting the water-rates compulsorily reduced by
Act of Parliament, and otherwise focus the public eye on
the bloated monopoly. No wise bloated monopoly goes
out of its way to advertise its bloatedness—especially it
doesn’t try to look more bloated than it is. It rather
bloats unobtrusively, in a cool spot, all by itself where it
won’t excite the envy of the hairy-toothed multitude.
The Broken Hill Co. has to surrender the whole
lease, plant, machinery, reservoirs, &c., without com-
pensation (in other words, to lose its entire capital)
when its concession expires, and for that reason it
should steadily write down the value of all its works and
plant till they get to nothing. Despite its apparent
affluence, it is a very humble and not too profitable affair
when the temporary nature of its monopoly is considered,
and should behave as such.

Apparently it wasn’t Victoria’s former com-
mandant, Major-General Alex. Bruce Tulloch, but a
different Major-General Tulloch who was the mighty
guinea-pig that made such a very bad showing in the
London courts re certain smellful Westralian companies.
The cable simply described him as Major - Gen.
Tulloch, and the daily papers took it for granted and fell
in, and The Bulletin fell in on top of them. This paper
now climbs out again with what dignity it can muster,
and apologises to the wrongfully-maligned Major-General.

An attempt is being made to float the “ Gul-
gong Gold-Mining Co.”; capital, £6OOO in 12,000 shares of
10s. each. Nine thousand shares are issued to the publio at
3d. on application, and 3d. on allotment, paid up to 7s. 3d.,
balance in 3d. calls if required. Prospectus says “ No
brokerage will be charged. The whole of the cash
realised, less necessary initial expenses, will be expended
on the mine.” The block proposed to be floated is part of
the late Shellback property. If the 9000 shares are taken
up, the co. will start with £225, less initial expenses. The
new shaft will have to be sunk 140 ft. before the reef is
struck, and then there is only a 4-head stamp-battery on
the field, which may or may not be available. It is also
stated that, “Preference is stipulated to be given to
the shareholders of the Shellback Co.”; but, judg-
ing from the recent experiences of those gentlemen, this
offer will, perhaps, not be very much rushed. At the
very best the co. has only about £lBOO of available capital
(12,000 shares of 10s. each issued as paid up to 7s. 3d.
leaves a very small margin), and to start any kind of a
mine with £lBOO available capital is, as a rule, as feeble
and ridiculous as starting a big war with six men and a
goat. It is hardly possible that it oan lead to anything
except a few month*’ salary for a few people, and then a
breakdown. Furthermore, the practice of issuing shares
on which nothin* is paid as paid unto 7*. 19 j’
lid. or any other sum, is one that should be suppresses
Every co’s shares should appear in the share-lists as paid
up Jno more than the sum paid on the«n cash,
or in assets that are worth cash, so that the public will
know exactly how things stand It w '? uld '*
to get a hardened mining-promoter of the N.B. W. pattern
into a court of law, and lay him flat on his solemn oath
and ask him what is the exact purpose of this is. id. paid
up idea. , . .
New Bushman’s mine, N.S.W., lock, stock and
barrel, knocked down at £162.5. At least four tunes the
amount spent in plant. At one time a big offer (
£60,000?) was refused. As a rule men who won t sell a
mine are bigger fools than those wanting to buy it.
Often the latter have other fish to fry—generally buying
for a crowd, whereas the former always sell for them-
selves. „
* * *
Girilambone, N.S.W. : <£50,000 cash said to
have been refused with contempt. The amount was
described as “only £50,000,” and shareholders were
assured that the directors were not going to give the
mine away.” Why are the shares so low if the holders
are not prepared to sell at this price ? Price of shares is
not always proof of value, but a low price in open market
proves beyond doubt a want of confidence and that
Girilambone must pass through much tribulation before
becoming a div.-payer. Writer has always regarded the
Girilambone as a good mine with good management and
a sad destroyer of cash without. To try to open a big
low-grade mine with very troublesome ores, in a dry
country, without a working capital worthy of the name,
was a cruel if not a dirty piece of business. They have
tried different managers and methods and with all failed
to make money. They have issued debentures to pay off
old debts, and, under representations which have not been
borne out, issued new sh . :-js which can be bought for
much less than paid on t! u. Shareholders have never
had a clear idea of the loss that necessarily takes place m
concentrating the ores they have to mine, and if they had
the faintest knowledge of the amount of money it will
still take to put the mine in right shape, they would jump
at the chance of getting some of their coin back.
Depend upon it the people offering £50,000 are ready with
another £20,000 to make an entire change in the present
order of things. If not they are as big asses as the
present owners. * » *
Copper has come back to £7O a ton, and the
market is described as flat. Indications point to a further
fall, and the chances are it will run between £6O and £7O
for a long time. Admitted now that the Anaconda, the
biggest producer in the world, turning out about one-fifth
of the world’s output, has weakened, and the life of the
mine as the champion is said to be within measurable dis-
tance. This the main incentive to the extraordinary rise.
The heavy buying by the Standard Oil 00. and other
people in Calumet and Heelas is a second cause ; and, as
to the third, writer is told that a few months ago some
strong men in London and America thought the time ripe
for a pool to buv copper stocks and productions. Some
of the big mines took the bait by selling their outputs for
six and twelve months at a big advance on then ruling
price. Offers were made in Australia, but up to date this
scribe has not heard of any being accepted. Thus a com-
bination of causes sent copper up £2O a ton in a few
weeks —a rise that could not be accounted for in Aus-
tralia at the time. The pool was only made for six months.
* * * ■*
Beacon Gold Mines, N.S.W., is petitioning
Govt, to put up half the money needed to sink another
100 ft., and in support claims to have employed an average
of 100 men for 2£ years, and disbursed £lOOO a month in
wages. Strange such a document should be published.
It could have gone in the usual way, have been
treated on its merits, and no one would have been
the wiser. The Beacon was formerly known as
Taylor’s Reward. First bonded to Charlie Garland
for something considerably under £20,000, then
to Mr. Nat. Cohen for about double, and, finally,
the Exploration Co. of London got hold of it through
Gibbs, Bright and Co., and it was capitalised at a quarter-
of-a-million. A 10-head mill was got going in a few
months, and about £25,000 profit was made during the
first year’s run. What writer predicted all along has since
happened. It was good enough at the price asked by
the owners, but the subsequent capitalisation was un-
warrantable. Only one of many glaring cases of mining
for big quick profits in London. And now that the har-
vest has been reaped, and the boomsters’ victims have
been more or less ruined, the practice is j'umped on by
Vic. Mines-Minister Foster, seconded by N.S.W. Mines-
Minister Cook in an assemblage of persons, some of whom
have been living on the game for years, and they had the
cheek to signify their repentance by loud applause.
* * * *
Cobar notes.—Some Cobar scrip-holders are
moaning over remarks here made. Nothing so awkward
to fight as the truth, and, as usual, the only weapon at
hand is abusive language. One local weekly, formerly
almost wholly a sheet of advts., has jumped into a state
of positive activity on the subject. The present scribe
happens to have never “ dispraised ” any local mine, but
he has frequently found fault with managements who
take a year over six months’ work, put up mills for the
sake of looking at them, and break promises made to
shareholders.
A shower fell the other day, but mainly con-
fined its attention to the copper-mine tank, which now
has water enough to keep the jackets going for nearly
three months—thus cheating Cobar Gold Mines Co. out
of £lO a day, which it hoped to collect from the Copper
Syndicate for the use of “ Lake O’Gorman.” But the G.M.
00. need not weep. It has a fine mine, which could
be made to pay with milling alone. With the addition
of the cyanide plant now in course of erection it
should make a huge success. Several improvements
started months back are now completed. The two big
boilers and a host of other new plant nearly ready for
work. Now waiting chiefly for the cyanide plant, which
must be finished some day.
• * ♦ *
Lake George Mines, N.S.W. : Feb. returns
show an improvement in copper values. Cost of mining,
handling, and smelting, inclusive of all fuels and fluxes,
said to be 14s. Bd. per ton. Month’s output, 5420 tons.
Time it was known how much must be added to total cost
for carriage and treatment of matte, &c., and if it would
not be better to refine the matte on the mine instead of
carting it over 30 miles of heavy roads, then some 200
miles by rail for shipment away. Doesn’t pay Cobar or
Burraga to do this. Then why Lake George ?
* * * *
Shamrock’s, Bendigo, was a short-lived boom.
Three or four weeks sent the shares from 3s. 6d. to 14s,
Taken about the same time to send them back again.
Last week’s crushing, 94 tons, 24ioz. ; previous week
about the same. Makes shareholders wonder if the Mac-
duff is going to act as treacherously. Last week’s crush-
ing, 37 tons, 224f0z., and a div.—£l3oo.
* • * *
Campbell’s Creek dredging, Vic. : Still cleaning
up, and now intimated that it is only a partial clean
representing \ acre of ground. Said to be over an acre
in another report, and that “it is tedious work scraping
out all the nooks and crannies of the bed-rock.” No
doubt this cleaning-up business has been quite overlooked
in the yarn published several times every week that
ground can be broken down, lifted and sluiced all by
water for 2d. a cubic yard.
* * *. *
Mr. William Thow, chief mechanical engineer
of the Railway Department, has recently returned from
an extended trip through the United States and Europe.
It is understood that whilst there Mr. Thow, after making
most exhaustive inquiries as to the latest and most im-
proved pneumatic tools and means of operating same,
purchased for the Eveleigh workshops an Ingersoll-
Sergeant duplex air compressor, with compound air
cylinders and inter-cooler. The local agents for this
company are Parke and Lacy Company Ltd., Sydney.*
Mt. Mercer, Vic. “ Yield for the week, 12oz.
19dwt.” It would not pay for the guzzle given at the
opening a little while back, when big things were fore-
shadowed for this mine by reason of good management
and wine. Changed their manager, but they can’t get
back their champagne.
* • * *
New Moon, Bendigo: Pulling from 18 levels at
one time and preparing to sink to get some more ready.
Is there any other mine in Australia doing this ? Work-
ing a big but disjointed reef —generally a mass of spurs
which average all the year round about 7dwt., out of
which fully half goes in divs. Manager, Bill Bentley,
with whom the writer did some hard graft, as youngster,
trucking, beating an occasional “upper,” and otherwise
learning the miner’s business at £2 5s. a week^
Spring Gully, a claim at the long-neglected
Fryer’s, outside Castlemaine, is turning in comfortable
divs. to a handful of Bendigo men. Six months’ work :
32880 z. from 3986 tons; 7s. 6d. divs., equal to £9OOO.
Battery, &c., have also been provided out of earnings.
Emu Bay railway, Tas. : Less than half the
100,000 preference shares to find extra capital taken up ;
hence the line to Zeehan not yet undertaken, but direc-
tors have got an advance to take the line to Rosbery.
• * - * •
The old-time Blinman, for years one of the most
reliable .of S. A. copper-mines outside the Moonta group,
has answered to the resurrection trump, and arrange-
ments have been made to send off 2000 tons. Blinman’s
distance from port—it is in the Far North —is against it;
on the other hand, it is cheaply worked. As at the
Burra, much of the work will be done by open-cut, which
Broken Hill has popularised.
General-manager Stewart, of Broken Hill, will
take charge of Ohiliagoe next month.
* * * *
From Copperfield, Clermont, Q., 5/3/ 99 :
" Have had good rains ; dams full. Nearly all the reefing
claims are either registered or abandoned. The ‘ Ida
Orompton ’ has crushed 32 tons stone for 7dwt. per ton ;
this has put a damper on things. Stone heavily mineral-
ised and no appliances for proper treatment; reef is from
3 to 4ft. wide and deepest shaft less than 200ft. Several
fresh reefing claims are being worked on Macdonald’s
Flat. Nothing fresh in alluvial; a few shafts going down
in deep ground. Peak Downs Coal and Copper Mining
Co.’s mine has been unwatered and retimbered, and if
reports are reliable, with copper at its present price, this
should be a good thing. The work is being carried on by
a Sydney co. and the mine is expected to change hands
shortly."
Queensland Mining and Prospecting Co. —the
next best copper organisation to the parent co. (Chillagoe)
—is sending one of the founders, Revel Johnston, to Lon-
don to form a co. which will be good enough to provide a
working capital of at least £lOO,OOO, and give present
holders cash and shares. Stated that Mr. J. leaves with
the “ fullest confidence ” that he will succeed.

* * • *
More J. Bull capital invades Australia.
The old Queensland Smelting Co., of Maryborough,
is being taken over by a London concern to be called
the Queensland Smelting Co., Limited. The new co.
is a comprehensive affair, its object being “ to acquire
the business, properties, assets and liabilities, &c., of the
Queensland Smelting Co., Ltd. (incorporated 1888), and to
develop, deal with, work and turn to account the same ;
and, further, to acquire any other mines, mining, water
and other rights, grants, leases, claims, concessions, op-
tions of purchase, metalliferous land, alluvial ground,
&c., in any part of the world, and to carry on the busi-
ness of a mining, milling, smelting and metallurgical
company in all or any of its branches.” Which seems to
give the co. all the latitude and smelting it could possibly
want.
* * * •
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, etc., has just com-
pleted at his engineering works, Footscray, Melb., one of
the largest planing machines every made in any part of
the world. It weighs about 100 tons, and is the machine
to be used in planing and thickening up the edges of
28ft. x Jin. plates required in manufacturing the patent
Lockingbar pipes for Coolgardie water supply contract. All
the steel bars for this contract will be supplied from the
Earl of Dudley works, Eng., but in order to get prompt
delivery the order for the steel plates has been divided
amongst four German firms. The first shipment, of 500
tons will be dispatched by the Maori King, now loading.*

Boulder milling-plant has shut down, the funds
placed at Koneman’s disposal are exhausted, Koneman
has gone to London to consult the directors, and a
ghastly failure is practically admitted. What a nice time
Koneman and the directors will have! Whew ! Their
turn now to go through the mill, to be so well crushed as
to leave nothing in the tailings. If it should so happen
that they are smart or cunning enough to charm or delude
the shareholders into thinking that in this queer-loo,king
business they have been as innocent as babes unborn,
then writer must regard the conversion of Bull collec-
tively, not as a matter of time, but eternity.
Cyanide returns from the S.A. Govt, works
have encouraged the erection of a private plant at the
Westward Ho, on the often-tried and often -abandoned
Waukaringa goldfield. It is the district’s best hope.

The gossip recently to hand about N.S.
Wales’ new Governor reminds The Bulletin of
the lines
“There’s no such thins: in nature Gand you’ll draw
A faultless monster which the world ne’er saw.”
“ Simple a,nd unaffected. ... A really handsome
fellow, with a pleasant smile, showing perfect
teeth. . . . Hobbies about art, education, philan-
thropy, and agriculture. . . . Always religious,
his devotional temperament at one time nearly
landed him in Holy orders. . . . Taking periodic
trips to France to study art.” (“Hum!”)
Rich, not only in pictures by Reynolds, Romney,
and the rest, but also in an unequalled collection
of bijouterie obtained by his canny great-grand-
mother on the cheap in Paris when, after Water-
loo, the pockets of the old nobility were as low as
their hearts. Then, as an entertainer, Beauchamp

bids fair to figure in N.S.W. annals as the “Let

• * * •
Newspapers say it is characteristic of Sydney
to tender handsome farewells to departing Gover-
nors. This was not always so, One Governor
was followed down the bay and hooted. Went-
worth led a chorus of groans at another. But a
group of claqueurs can be counted on generally to
present addresses and lisp valedictories to anyone
who has occupied the post, however unworthy his
achievement. “If Her Majesty could commission
the Prince of Darkness to represent her in N.S.
Wales,” wrote old Dr. Lang, “I doubt not but
his sable Excellency would receive a farewell
address of respect and regret at the close of his
administration.” Parkes and Martin once refused
to sign an address to a departing Governor ; on
the contrary, they drew up a letter for the Secre-
tary of State, pointing out the deficiencies in the
conduct of the august proconsul.
» * * *
Gov. Beauchamp brings out his private
pictures. Duff used to say that the stock lot of
pictures in Sydney Govt. House was probably the
worst in the world. Hampden thought likewise.

That strange conflict, of evidence between
the Bishop of Ballarat and the policemen should
be officially cleared up. It is a serious matter
when you find a bishop contradicting policemen
on oath. It is also a serious matter to find police-
men contradicting a bishop on oath.
* * * *
On a N.S.W. military matter :
Premier Reid (writes a Bulletin correspondent) dis-
likes pomps and ceremonies, and the N.S.W. Lancers,
who at one time formed a guard of honor for his P.0.-ship
in London, got rough handling in return. In answer
to a question by a Labor member, the Premier gave it as
his opinion that other branches of the forces were entitled
to as much “if not more consideration ” than the pig-
stickers. Now, the Lancers paid their whack in full
when the Permanent Artillery (the spoilt darlings of
N.S.W. service) and the Mounted Rifles were liberally
assisted by Govt, to go ’Ome and enjoy the Jubilee
junketings, and the present will be the third trip the
Lancers have arranged free of cost to the country. When
Australian natives, chiefly from the country districts,
plank their money and give their time to learn the real
game of soldiering (and this must be played by the book),
then its rough to have cold water thrown on their
effort at its inception. The real moral of the story
appears to be—Never form a guard of honor to an un-
grateful fat Premier 1

* * * *
Premier Reid’s slap in the face of N.S.W.
Marine Board might have been heard by the 13
drowned Atacama sailors rotting in the Pacific
sea-depths. Upon a recommendation of the Pub-
lic Service Board, the destruction of this tribunal
of mental corpses has been resolved on, but it was
somehow staved off during last Parliament.
Meanwhile the odors of the decaying Board are
anything but pleasant, and Premier Reid tried to
introduce a disinfectant in the shape of a new in-
spector, Capt. Mackenzie. The Board flatly re-
fused to appoint the inspector; whereupon Reid
countered by appointing Capt. Mackenzie and
Sam Smith, M.L.A., secretary of the Seamen’s
Union, to seats on the Board. To anyone
acquainted with the personality of Mackenzie and
Smith and with the starched dignity in which the
Mi.rine mummies are gradually mouldering away
the situation yells with farce. Sam. Smith !
Capt. Mackenzie ! If any spark of life was left
in the Board it would resign ; but it is dead, and
will have to he swept right out.
* * * *
Fortitude Valley, Brisbane, which has
returned the two Laborites, Higgs and M‘Donnell
to the local Parliament, got its name from the old
ship Fortitude, which, 50 years ago, carried to Mor-
ton (not Moreton) Bay Dr. Lang’s first load of Pres-
byterian immigrants. The impulsive pastor had
ordered his consignment without previously ob-
taining the approval of Govt., and when his ship-
load of canny ” Scots arrived, the Govt, of the
day refused to recognise them. Temporarily
they were dumped down on vaoant ground which
was given the name of the ship, and the fighting
doctor went for the Government tooth and nail.
By the time the row was over it was found that
the fortitude people had turned their wilderness
of a valley into fruitful vegetable gardens and

were quite well able to get on without special
assistance. Prom that day the Valley has been
sturdily assertive in political affairs. It loves
democrats, but it ordinarily likes them to be men
of prayer as well.

Re the insufferable controversy as to Mary
“Maudlin’s” morals, The Bulletin notes that

consensus of opinion among the most competent

* * * *
South Australia lately went half-back on
the standard-time arrangement made a few years
ago, and in May it will put its clocks forward
half-an-hour. And thus it lands Broken Hill in
a dust-heap. For after the Standard
Time Act was passed, Broken Hill
pubs, had to shut up at 10 p.m.;
at any rate, since the trains and
mines adhered to Adelaide time,
that was the reckoning that pre-
vailed. It was an awful affliction,
for Broken Hill hasn’t more than
half got rid of its thirst at 10 o’clock.
So its teetotal M.P. rushed through
a Bill, giving Broken Hill an hour’s
grace for drinking and polling pur-
poses ; and this revised time was
called S.A. standard time. Now,
however, it isn’t certain whether
there is any S.A. standard time;
and the Barrier beer-puller has dis-
cussed the riddle until he is des-
perate. At present it looks as if
the weary town were going to have
at least four times—Sydney time,
which the banks and telegraph
office, and some other Govt, offices
observe; the old Adelaide time,
which the hotels will observe ; the
revised Adelaide time, which the
railway and possibly the mines will
follow ; and the local time, which the
man from way-back who goes by the
sun will stick ito.

its own burying ground, the Anglicans and
Roman Catholics, whose grounds adjoin,
have a stone division fence plentifully sown '
with broken glass—evidently to prevent any
over-the-garden-wall business on the spirits’ parb

* * * *
Letter received by elderly spinster—client
of a Melb. registry office—in reply to an advt.
for position as housekeex>er :
Hawthorn P.O. Dear Madam,—Seeing your “ad,” I
thought it possible we might come to some agreement.
I am 35 years of age, and at present am living in lodgings.
In a month or two I am starting business as a dyer, and I
wish to make the acquaintance of a lady as housekeeper
or companion, and should she be willing it might lead to
something better being offered to her. I should like you
to take another situation for awhile, and let me have the
pleasure of your company in the evening. I have no
lady friends, and should like the lady to let me have a
husband’s privileges at once. It should make no differ-
ence to her whatever, as I have every intention of treat-
ing her honorably and well; and if you can agree to this
one thing I am sure you will find everything else satis-
factory, and if you take an interest in the business and
look after the shop now and again I will marry you, if
you wish it, as soon as we get settled down and
see how we get along. You must not think that
I am writing to you like this because I think you are not
respectable, as nothing is farther from my thoughts.
Please let me have a plain answer, and, if you like, you
can make an appointment about 8 any evening, and
please describe the place and your dress, so I may know
you easily, as I am rather backward in speaking to ladies.
In replying, please return this note as a sign of good
faith, otherwise I will consider you are not sincere.—
Believe me to be yours very sincerely, .
And this is “The Way We Love Now ” in the
city of St. Magdalen ! The elderly spinster is
still in hospital, and her condition is considered to
be critical.
* * * *
The woman-vote has brought a revolution
in the working of S. A. election campaigns. King
O’Malley, M.P., recently signalised his advent in
Encounter Bay—a seaside constituency composed
largely of the long, lean boarding-house female
and the W.C.T.U. woman—by a series of coffee-
socials terminating with a dance which “ lasted
until an early hour.” Election agents must now
be young men capable of leading the young ladies
in the waltz and whispering “vote for Jones”
during the polka’s giddy whirl.
* * * *
Notice the slow-going, imitative way in
which changes are introduced into the Argus.
Just lately a column about people has been
opened under the same heading as London Times
uses—“ Personal.” The summary of coming
events, “To-day’s Arrangements,” is a recent
feature, and the heading in that case, too, is bor-
rowed from the Times. And the Argus only
puts into its “Personal” column the sort of dry
“THE RETREAT FROM MOSCOW.”
hash that the Times admits. Imitation is the
poorest kind of flatulency.

“Burton” : Be London vet. suing for
prof, attendance on a cat. A Sydney lady, who
owns a very fine pair of “ Persians,” always en-
gages a veterinary surgeon to see that, the cat goes
safely through her family troubles. And the, vet.
charges a guinea every time.

(2000 copies) of Will Ogilvie’s poems, published by

appeared,” writes Victor Daley.

office to any address a specimen copy of The
Bookfellow, a rapidly-growing literary maga-
zinelet published monthly by The Bulletin.

Some time ago a mythical nugget known
as the “ Sacred Slug of Kanowna ” was the subject
of some statements by the local priest (Father
Long). The Bulletin’s presumption at the time
was that it was an outcome of the delirium of two
sick prospectors. Now there comes a sort of
sequel-incident, as disclosed in the statement of a
West Australian correspondent that when
Father Long was leaving for Perth en route to
Adelaide the railway authorities grabbed his
luggage, and detained and opened it in search of
the “ sickle ” nugget aforesaid.

THAT STRONG MAN.

“Yes; ’n’spoke to’im."

“ Morality lets himself loose in a Sydney
daily re the wickedness of publishing Divorce-
court proceedings, which can be read by the small
boy between bites of the green apple. The curious
thing about this raid against indecent details is
that, while each reader is genuinely distressed
about the effect on other readers, he is never at
all anxious about the effect on himself. Thus no
clergyman comes home from touring Italy without
declaring that certain exhibits (sacred to men
only) should be destroyed as too disgraceful, &c.,
&c., and yet no clergyman ever refrains from going
and taking a look himself. A recent number of
the Arena, in an article on newspapers, tells how
the people of a certain American city, especially
the women, arose and declared against impure
details in the daily papers, and how, when one
high-class paper did take the hint and drop into
morality, all the people of that city, especially the
women, went in for the other papers just the
same. The reformed paper had to reform
back again in double-quick time to save itself
from utter extinction.
* * * *
The one thing clearly revealed by the re-
opening of the enquiry into the murder of the
Murphys at Gatton is the strange inactivity, all
things considered, of their blood relations when
the news came to hand. The P.M. and the police
inspector in charge of the case alike complain that
the Murphy family are not doing their best to
assist the inquiry. Brother Bill Murphy sullenly
describes the effect of the gory tidings upon the
Murphy household. “ Witness, continuing, said
that when his brother-in-law, M'Neill, came up
after discovering the bodies he was much excited.
Witness asked if all were dead, and he replied,
‘ Yes, it is something terrible.’ Witness went to
his mother, but could not remember what she
said when she was told. . . . After repeated
questioning witness said that he remembered his
mother saying ‘Oh, my God, my poor children ! ’
Witness went to fetch his father, who was at the
farm, and who asked what was the matter. Wit-
ness replied that Norah, Helen, and Mike had
been murdered. Mr. Murphy, senr., then asked
if they had been shot. When told the facts he
said he was <dad they attended church, on Sunday.”
[The foregoing is more or less contradictory to
what witness had stated at the first enquiry.]
Witness, continuing, said that “ neither he nor his
father while on the way home spoke of the
probable culprit. None of the Murphys had gone
about the country attempting to find out for them-
selves who committed the murder, but M‘Neill
did. Witness’s father had never tried to urge him
to see what he could do. He had never offered to
help the police, or lend them any horses, although
he had heard the police were hiring horses. There
are 20 horses, seven of them draught, on the
farm.” Sticking right out from this queer story
is the fact that the head of the Murphy family
—who “ takes about an hour every evening to say
his prayers ’’—was glad to think his murdered off-
spring had been to church on Sunday; which
suggests that if the enquiry proves fruitless on its
present lines it had better take a religious turn.

The Canterbury (N.S.W.) inspector of
nuisances recently, during one fortnight, con-
demned 14 animals for cancer and tuberculosis.
Much easier to believe that inspection elsewhere is
a howling farce than that Canterbury is a special
nursing-ground for these diseases. Satisfactory,
in this connection, to note that one N.S.W. squat-
ter has been fined =£2o for slaughtering a cancerous
beast for human consumption. .£3OO would have
been nearer the mark, of course ; still, the thing
looks like a beginning.

“ Perce A.” on a physiognomical point
Your name-crank has omitted to notice that Boot-trunk
Tod is a namesake of Sweeney Todd, the Demon Bar >er.
Dubberke is du Burke, of Burke and Hare, and Thekla is
associated with an atrocity of world-wide celebrity- The
Maiden’s Prayer,” which was perpetrated by Thekla
Badargewska. Many a time in my defenceless infancy,
when compelled to play the ghastly thing did I objurgate
Thekla’s name ! The erotic fools whom you let write of
Dubberke’s “angel face” want suppressing badly.
She has the massive lower jaw of the carnivora—
cruel relentless, and repellent. The comic artist
who ’ draws a burglar or murderer always gives
him a jaw like Thekla, hut it is dotted over with
a week’s growth of stubble, and therefore does
not appeal to the libidinous scribe. It is this stamp of
ordinary sop-head who can’t tell a tigress from a sheep.
In fact, such people rather prefer the pretty tigress, and
when it scratches and bites they wonder how they could
have been such fools. Nun-like eyes have nothing to do
with the ease. The South Sea Island cannibals have most
beautiful eyes. I saw the Peak Hill boy murderer, who
killed his benefactor and mate, and sat alone in the awful
silence of the bush for three days, chopping up the body
and burning it piecemeal. He had large, soft, innocent
blue eyes, hut his lower jaw covered a third of his face
and his upper lip was barely half an inch long.
I have large, innocent blue eyes myself. Of course
many people with weak mouths and big undershot chins
get through life without killing anybody, but let them get
into a tight place, and they have neither discretion nor
mercy.

The facts around a certain Melb.
marriage ceremony of two years ago which missed-
fire owing to the absence (so far unexplained) of
the bridegroom at the eleventh hour, are dramatic
enough to form a playwright’s plot. The bride-
to-be, like Dubberke, had a “friend.” He had
occupied the position for a considerable period,
and objected strongly when she accepted an offer
of marriage. But he expostulated in vain. She was
ennuyee (many women marry because they want a
change), and wed she would. However, the
partnership was not dissolved up to the night
before the ceremony, and at nine o’clock next
morning the “friend ” rooted the expectant groom
out of bed and unfolded to him a tale which
nearly resulted in bloodshed. The deceived youth
was, however, compelled to accept irrefutable
proofs, and the two went out and had a drink
together. At any rate, while the bride was vainly
waiting, with fire in her eye, at the altar, the
missing one was moving out aboard a Tas.
steamer, and the “friend ’ was waving a pocket-
handkerchief at him genially from the wharf.

Not generally known that the main
entrance to Victoria Barracks (Sydney)
was once captured in day’
light and the fort success-
fully held for several hours
by a single individual. The
invader was an escaped
lunatic, laboring under the
delusion that he was a
large foreign army. Arm-
ing himself with a revol-
ver he boldly charged the
ramparts and potted the
sentry on duty in the
shin. Then he made for
the. guards’ quarters, just
inside the gate, and fired
a few shots through the
window, whereupon the
guards left hurriedly per
back door, leaving the fort
to the enemy, who prompt-
ly collected all the stray
rifles and proceeded to
take pot shots at any-
one who came within
range. The defeated
guards retired gracefully,
invoked the aid of the
local policeman, and
“egged” him on to go
and recapture the outpost.
The daring trap crept up
to the window and called
out the “looney,” who
immediately pumped a
charge of lead into his helmet.
Eventually the “cop” succeeded
in convincing the enemy that he was
one of him, and he got in and surrounded
the invading army and marched it off to “ quod.”

“ R.H.” : A. D. Bayldon’s glorifica-
tion of swimmers’ “ lily-like whiteness ” will
find no sympathisers among women. Once, at a
country swimming match, I stood close to two
well-developed young damsels between 17 and 19,
and couldn’t help overhearing the following :
“ Oh, Edith, look ! that’s a nice chap over there
in pink trunks!” Edith, disgustedly : “Him/
Why, Nell, he hasn’t got a hair on his legs ! ”
Neli (disappointedly) : “No ; no more he has ”

A Brisbane Presbyterian Church is deter-
mined to cut out all rivals. Unable to cope with
the attractions of another establishment by ordi-
nary methods, the elders of aforesaid kirk sub-
sidised a ’bus to carry worshippers free to the
door of their particular House of God. If this
goes on, we shall yet see intending church-goers
presented with a tram-ticket and 3d. to put in the
plate—by the zealous kirk authorities. And,
really, a man ought to be subsidised who goes
regularly to listen to a good Calvinistic sermon.
THE A.N.A. ART UNION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One destiny, one mob,
And a fortune for a bob,
Is our screed, we bow the knee alone to boodle ;
Work and graft are out of date,
When a fortune lies in wait,
And the prudent man’s an antiquated noodle.
Parsons run their Godly prize,
An art-union of the skies,
Every weelt on Sunday mornings at eleven ;
Sunday-trading, spite of laws,
And it’s winked at—just because
The good fortune that they offer you is—Heaven.
Yet, the inconsistent Cloth
Grow inordinately wroth
At our yearly A.N.A. Foundation scramble ;
If our sin to them is loss,
Let them meekly bear the cross,
For they taught us in the first place how to gamble.
Vio.
