A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 14th, 1899.
My Dear Myek—
As if to paint in still darker tints the sombre
departure from these shores of the Hampdens,
the death of the Dowager Viscountess Hampden
was cabled just after the vice-regal party had
sailed from Adelaide; so that N.S.W.’s already -
troubled ex-Governor was fated to be met at
Albany with the sad tidings of his mother’s death.
Sydney is simply pouring itself out on to the
Pacific this year. Every fifth person has gone, or
is going, Londonwards, and, of course, those who
have never been there before call it “Home ” with
the loudest note of appropriation. So many are
departing that it really looks as if there would be
no Potts Point in Sydney this season. However,
I hear that some of the Sydney people who went
away a little earlier in the year will be leaving
England again somewhere in April. By the way,
Lord Beauchamp leaves in April. A coincidence,
possibly. Still, we mustn’t overlook the marvellous
superiority of anyone who can say “ I came out on
the same boat with Lord Beauchamp.” To say
nothing of the true inwardness of bringing one’s
fair daughters into his Ex. ’s presence on a long
voyage, especially if Lady Mary Lygon- Liggon,
please—suffers from the sea.
It seems that there isn’t much the shops
don’t know about prominent people’s frock-
ing; especially when the dressmaking has not
been done at their own establishments. I
heard a shop-woman spreading herself with
simply superb authority the other day on the
clothes of one who is considered among the best-
dressed women in Sydney. Her audience was a
customer being “fitted.” “But I thought she
got her frocks from Paris,” said the customer
humbly. “Not she!” said the lady, with her
tongue full of pins. “’Er sister made that rose-
pink satin with the flounces out of the frock she
wore last year to Lady Haitches’ ball, and she’s
havin’ it covered this year with spangled rose-
colored toole, and coral belt ’n’ trimmin’s. That
blue frock with the peplum was made in the ’ouse;
it’s lined with green cashmere, and three green
flounces at the foot. I don’t like her in
that. Nor I don’t like her in that little
black toque with the sables. It don’t suit her.
She got that at B’s sale as cheap as dirt and
altered it herself. That’s where Lady de Jones
got her ’ydrangea hat. I know how much that
cost. She’s getting a lovely frock for the races
made at Miss Jayses, but that’ll be her only knot
walkin’-dress this autumn; the others are just
going to be taken to pieces at ’ome, and made-up
in the knoo shapes, but she has two puffickly
lovely ball-dresses.” On, on, she rattled. She
knew all about Society’s stockings, lingerie, hair-
dressing, cloaks, frocks, hats, and never for a
moment did she speak as “one of the scribes.”
How some of the upper-crust would pride them-
selves on superior inf ormation like this ! To know
all about other people’s dresses is quite the third
biggest fashionable-female disease of the century,
and causes more quacking than any other feminine
complaint going. Yet it seems that the dress-
makers, who seldom get a chance of seeing
the frocks they describe so truthfully and
gloatingly, run the upper-circles very hard in the
race for knowledge. The only explanation of
dressmakers’ superiority is that when they get
together they fall into the historic custom of
talking “skirts, not sentiments.”
A visit to Dorothy Vane is like a trip to toy-
land. I went to see her at Pfahlert’s Hotel in
Wynyard Square, the other day, and found her
room packed with toy monkeys, toy parrots, toy
parasols, and all sorts of Jap. and Sydney dolls
and fans. These are all fixed up on a big screen
in the middle of her gay little room, and represent
the offerings of the gods, as well as of the ordinary
theatre-going mortals. The big Dolly, in a pink
wrapper, sat quaintly on one foot in an arm-
chair, and looked as if La Poupee’s move-
ments had got into her blood. I thought
you might like to hear that her stage-hair is
quite her own, and is a short, silky, golden affair
that doesn’t Curl but isn’t straight. By some
strange freak, she looks alternately 16 and 30, and
owes her stage prettiness more to magnetism than
to anything more substantial. About the convent,
she hesitates a little now, and when we spoke of it,
looked as if she had been teased to the limits of
her liking. She will probably play in pantomime
here next year, so the convent walls are not going
to close down on her just yet. Like every actress
God or man ever made, she likes herself best in
sad parts, and wishes she had more of them, but
says, candidly, “nobody will have mein them.”
lolanthe is her next change of character. As
Pitti-Sing she first felt herself getting hold of the
Sydney public ; until then she hadn’t seemed, to
herself at any rate, to catch them. “ It is very
lonely,” says Dorothy, “coming home from the
theatre after a big reception with all your flowers
and fal-lals, and having none of your people to
talk things over with.” Still, she takes very palp-
able joy in her Australian successes, and is neither
too old nor too artful to tell you so.
Another visit I paid last week was to Miss
Theo. Cowan’s studio, a queer little den up in the
roof of one of the Arcades. The handsome Aus-
tralian sculptress, a black-haired, blue-eyed artist,
was working away in a professionally-dirty holland
qverall that reached to her feet. I had a look at
her latest achievement, and even in its unburnt
terra-cotta state was attracted by its bold, yet
tenderly idealistic treatment. A big, modern-
looking angel, with long, wind-blown hair,
ig the centre figure of the panel, and on either
side is a girlish figure, one sleeping, one
wide-eyed, looking away to the rising sun on the
left of the panel. The destiny of this charming
work is to fill a panel in the front of a red “ Hor-
bury Hunt house ” occupied by Mr. F. White at
Armidale, and the two girlish figures are allegori-
cal of a pathetic incident in the White family,
when one sister was drowned in trying to save
another’s life. Such very low relief has been em-
ployed that the figures are almost flat, but even to
an outsider the high imaginative quality of the
conception, and the gracefulness of the composition
must appeal. As for connoisseurs, Miss Cowan
appears to be equal to the most exacting demands.
Her commissions are becoming more and more fre-
quent, and, generally speaking, in Australia it is
only people with a critical appreciation of sculpts
who care to indulge in them. Among her latest
commissions is a bust of Edmund Barton, and
also an important public matter that may not be
spoken of yet.
Pattie Browne is the next to be benefited. I
hear that her benefit is to be a great, big, brilliant
affair. It is an open secret that Pattie’s part in
the panto.—as far as Pattie was concerned—was
chiefly a matter of shekels, but, before she had
time to gather many of these in, her illness forced
her out of the piece.
Last week, there appeared in the papers the
advt. of a benefit to Miss Gwen Dallas, setting
forth in a long column the date, the hour, and
what you would get when you got— where? For
there was not a mention of where the benefit
was to be. I noticed a good many names set
forth as helpers in connection with the benefit;
in fact, so profuse was the ad. that the only possible
detail that hadn’t been made public was the rather
necessary one of “place where.” Now the omis-
sion of this little fact from a benefit is fraught
with dangers. People who never meant to go can
say, “ So sorry, but I couldn’t find out where your
benefit was to be. ” People who meant to go, but
didn’t want to, can squeeze through the same loop-
hole and give the same reason for staying away.
That nobody ever went to a benefit out of pure
love of going to benefits, is a well-known theatri-
cal axiom.
Sydney runs far too much to benefits. In the
case of a lady of good position and decent means,
who is about to start with hubby on a pleasure
tour, there is grumbling amongst the artistes who
are more or less involuntarily contributing to the
benefit programme. Many of these have had
several pounds’ worth of tickets sent to them to
sell, and as they have neither the power nor the
time to dispose of them otherwise, have been ob-
liged to buy them up themselves, as the committee
delicately refuses to allow them to be returned.
In “Shadows of a Great City,” at the
Royal, Mr. O’Neill deserves special commen-
dation for the womanish way in which he
pats Miss Ada Guildford’s sorrowful head,
as this is a place where most men would re-
veal their sex. And the other matter that always
gives away a man in petticoats—l mean his feet—
turned out to be just a trifle too small for your
genuine Biddy. One of the most painful things
in this play is to see the cruel change in Robert
Inman’s handsome face when he puts on a
moustache. Any man who can’t quite make up
his mind to take a clean shave is here advised to
go and see what happens to the beautiful Robert
after his moustache grows. '
Speaking of theatricals, Falk, the well-known
photographer, is going to make a bold try and
shake off the reputation for putting on his best
style for actresses only. The firm now seeks to
establish its fame in a special branch of children’s
photography, and I saw some original ideas of
turning children into angels, with little ready-
made wings, that will delight the heart of all fond
mothers. One of these angel-studies displayed
itself in a show-case to the admiration of two
women, the other day, One mother was heard to
remark feelingly to the other: “It would be so
sweet to have the children taken like that. You
know, if anything happened to them, with these
photographs one would feel that they really were
angels. ” Many people would rather not enter into
considerations like these, and for them there are
charming and natural ways of taking baby’s like-
ness. Another speciality of Falk’s as the lamp-
light portrait, which can be taken by lamp-liglit
alone. I saw several of these in Mr. Brooke
Thornley’s pretty green-and-blue studio, and,
looking round, was struck by the fact that the
well-dressed woman with smartly ondule hair, and
fashionable frock, is a disagreeable failure photo-
graphically. In fact, if you want to get at the
inner ugliness of an extremely fashionable gown,
see it in a photo., and you’ll be surprised at the
way in which the camera shows up the meaning-
lessness of stylish trimmings.
One of the typical photographs of the day is
that of the Madden-Osborne bride, taken for a
Melbourne weekly. It is called “Miss Daisy
Madden’s farewell to Cloyne,” and was taken just
before the bride left for the Cathedral. With the
long tail of her wedding-dress kicked well out in
front, the bride paused on the way to be married,
and had her photograph taken. She stands alone
on the staircase, making a large picture of veil
and orange-blossom, and yards of white satin, and
plump, calm bride. Nobody but Bride enters into
the picture, but it doesn’t need much imagination to
feel the whole of the Madden family hiding away
round the corner of that staircase.
By the way, this portrait has won itself much
fame as a photographic feat. It was taken at
half-past one on the day of the wedding, and next
morning the picture was all over the country. And
to think that we have no photographs of —well, of
the twelve apostles!
A fashionable just-wedded couple proceeded on
their honeymoon per southern mail last week.
The groom reserved a compartment, on the window
of which an “Engaged” ticket was put. Some
wag, however, pasted over this a card similarly
printed, but labelled “ Married.” It was a cruel
joke.
I hear that Woollahra House ballroom is going
to be the fashionable mise-en-scknc of many private
Society dances this season. The snobility who
haven’t ballrooms of their own will here be able
to experience a keener proprietary feeling than
can be worked up over the ordinary, hired floor,
where common clayey feet may have trod awful
things like “The Caledonians” only the night
before.
Told (by Society, with wounded pride) of a re-
cent addition to Sydney’s inner circles. She was
house-hunting, and was taken to see what Sydney
considers a particularly swagger place, whose in-
mates were going off on a jaunt to Europe and
wouldn’t let tneir house to everybody. Instead of
being dazzled by its grandeur, she merely swished
round for a bit silkenly, then remarked with in-
expressible scorn: “Really I couldn’t think of
living in a house where there wasn’t a bath-room
and a dressing-room to every bedroom.” Since
then Society has hastened to find out that the lady
who was showing them all the way they should go
once lived in a Sydney suburb where dressing-
rooms haven’t even yet penetrated.
Of course you can’t believe all you hear, even
about musicians. But the following smart trick
was positively ascribed by many people to a, well-
known music-teacher. He receives pupils at
so many guineas a quarter, cash advance, lady
pupils preferred. When the fee is paid he gives
the lady a few lessons and then—tries to kiss her.
Of course the victim does not wish to create a
scandal, so she simply rises, leaves the room with
fearful dignity, and never returns to take the
balance of her lessons. Thus the cute professional
gets paid at the rate of a guinea or so a lesson.
But stay. She might have been tenderly playing
“ The Way to Paradise ” with both pedals down,
and he may have nearly kissed her to find if that
was the way there. Or supposing she were gal-
loping presto through somebody’s Funeral March,
naturally he would feel that he must stop her
journey at all costs. I wouldn’t be too sure about
the monetary considerations.
Forty horrors have lately happened to our local
Art Gallery. Somebody with a mania for other
people’s old Masters has just presented us with
forty imitation Raphaels, Titians, Murillos, An-
drea del Sartos, Filippo Lippis, Carlo Dolcis,
and other great dead men. Sydney hasn’t begun
to rave over the gifts yet, for people who like
these things at all, like them real. And people
who don’t like these things at all, don’t like the
imitations any better. The thought of coming at
every turn on “St. John as a Youth,” an “Angel
refusing the gift of Tobias,” “Magdalene with a
box of ointment,” and seven “ Virgin and Child,”
and knowing that they are only copies, does not
raise thrills in this uneducated city.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ ‘ Music hath charms ’: A speaker at an un-
employed meeting lately suggested as a new kind
of sensation that the men should assemble at the
organ recital in the Town Hall in the afternoon
and impress the swells with the desperateness of
their earnestness. They entered the hall headed by
their constant friend, Dr. Maloney, and they all
took front seats. But they did not have the
chance of soiling the skirts of Lady Brassey,
&c., with their hobnails ; the nobility climbed
into back seats and let the unemployed have
the front end to themselves. The recital
was listened to attentively, and, at the con-
clusion, three cheers were given by the men, who
did not know why they did it except that the doc-
tor told them to. Thus ended the demonstration.
It did not turn out as anticipated by the man who
suggested it. It was not a bit dramatic. The
music quelled the turbulence. The men came to
jeer and remained to cheer.
“ That newspaper correspondence re ‘ The Aus-
tralian Woman ’ has set carpers raking up past
peccadilloes of local naughty girls. One resur-
rected shocker treats of a society-leader who made
no secret of her weakness for a bygone beauty-
actor. Apropos thereof, Mrs. Giddy-Gout afore-
said, according to not such very ancient tradition,
once engaged, by wire, rooms at a popular resort
for herself and ‘maid’ for Saturday till Monday.
When ‘ Mistress and Maid ’ arrived, latter proved
to be such a tall, strapping Abigail as never was.
And talk about upsetting ways ! Occupants of
adjoining rooms averred that the unaccountably
pampered hussy spent the livelong Sabbath tied
to her employer’s apron-strings, smoking cigarettes,
whistling and lounging, in the fancied privacy of
the latter’s apartments.
“ Another lively matron loved cycling in its day
as a monkey does nuts. She liked to wheel out of
town with a band of merry pedallers of both
sexes, halting at some country inn for refresh-
ments. After a hot and dusty ride, the leader of
the party would whisk off her “stuffy skirt” and
sit down to dinner with her friends in Eton jacket,
starched shirt-front and tweed knickers.
“One used to hear a good deal at one time of
various devoted but not exactly eligible swains
attached to the Madden household, and the ex-
ample of the parents Madden, who began with a
modest establishment, was quoted as a golden rule
for the young ones to follow. However, these
adorers seem to have gone the way of all in-
eligibles, and the two eldest Misses Madden have
bestowed their affections on well-endowed part-
ners. Miss Madden was offered the choice of
seven homes by her husband-elect —it must have
been almost as interesting as choosing the Federal
capital. And the careful girl chose Yass.
“ It looked almost as though the two who set-to
partners last week had crossed over from their
respective corners of religion. An absence of
church-decorations in the case of the Protestant
bride was due to this being the season of dust and
ashes, and yet there was an exuberant spread at
the R.O. rendezvous in honor of the happy pair.
Considering the trouble they had in getting special
permission for a Lenten splice, the tabernacle’s
festive appearance was somewhat incomprehen-
sible.

“ Women rushed into Autumn dresses while the
thermometer was bubbling over the 90’s, and
rubbed shoulders at Flemington with fragile Sum-
mery things in chiffon, chemisettes and muslin
blouses, through which the lace of their linen and
PD’s stood self-confessed. The greatest splash in
dressing there was achieved by the plump young
wife of the Mount Morgan millionaire, for whom
taste and coin have worked wonders.
“Maggie Stirling cleared £360 by her Metro-
politan concert, and £4O by a minor venture,
while several smaller sums were derived from
successful country ‘farewells.’ Her passage to
England, ’tis understood, was the gift of an open-
handed patroness, and altogether the popular
favorite was sent off in a manner worthy of her
merits.
“The religious people were hard enough on
Prof. Marshall Hall a few months ago, hut they
are ready to seek his aid when he can help them.
Two nuns waited on him recently, and asked him
to work a concert in aid of St. Yincent’s Hospital.
Being a good sort of fellow, with a sense of humor,
he consented at once. But fancy nuns invading
the sanctum of the author of ‘ Hymns Ancient and
Modern ! ’ Really, nuns are the only women who,
unquestioned, can go absolutely anywhere.
“Sydney girls, ’tis said, have their ideas re the
present rush of Melbourne spinsters to local
horticultural grounds to ‘learn professional gar-
dening. ’ Considering the hideous striped uniform
prescribed, one must admit that this sudden
yearning for the society of hoes and sprinklers
looks a scrap suspicious, not forgetting, of course,
that much-canvassed cable as to vice-regal bachelor
Beauchamp’s passion for gardening.
“The accomplished and much-travelled daughter
of an old pastoral family played a successful lone
hand at fruit-growing in a local irrigation colony.
When she joined farms and fates with an adjacent
block-holder, she still stuck to the trade of her
spinster days. Thanks to feminine intuition, this
cute cultivator planted sultanas in the nick of
time, a season or two ago, and sold them to her
own satisfaction, while most of her male neigh-
bors conserved their pet crops in the teeth of a
falling market, and, in consequence, feed their
pigs on dried apricots to this day.
“ An old Northern identity, who took his own
life a while back, evidently regarded marriage as
a desperate expedient. A document found among
his papers proved that some years before he had
been strongly inclined to self-slaughter, balancing
long between the alternatives of death and matri-
mony. The attractions of the latter prevailed for
the time being, and the elderly widower took to
himself a young, handsome and devoted wife.
Later on, the exemplary husband and fond father
once more fell a-thinking of his first love—the
grave. Result—suicide.
“The Prince of Wales, according to a London
paper, never franks his letters, as he might easily
do, but pays postage. In this he sets an example
(not likely to be followed) to certain people in
Australia. When a certain Governor was away,
and there was a difficulty about getting franked
envelopes at Government House, an aide used to
go up to the Govt, offices on mail-days to write
letters to his friends at ’Ome, thereby saving
postage.
“ Top-scoring at cricket seems to have been the
death of one of the Emancipated. Mrs. Keir died
at Koroit last Monday at the age of 24 after a
series of brilliant performances on Koroit cricket-
ing-pitch. This young sporting matron, who
secretaried the Women’s Wicket club, was the
champion and ruler of the ladies of the Lake team.

No. I.— The Baker.
Mr. T. Streater writes : “I have received so
much benefit from Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa I feel
that I must thank you for it. I am a baker by
trade, which as you know, is very hard work, but
I am pleased to say that when I am done of a
night, I go home and have a cup of your Cocoa,
and feel quite fresh .again, and then I go and enjoy
myself. But before I took to Dr. Tibbie’s Vi-
Cocoa, I used to mess about indoors as tired as
could be. ”
Vi-Cocoa will become a household word amongst
us. Prepared from substances of admitted dietetic
value, Dr. Tibbies’Vi-Cocoa is at once digestive
and refreshing, nutritious and invigorating. This
is the secret of its phenomenal success. It seems,
so far as we can gather, to agree with everybody,
and everybody is agreed in giving it a good name.
Apparently it is the accepted beverage of a people
who have recognised and appreciated its remark-
able health-giving properties. And certainly, in
this age of rush and worry, wear and tear, when
the energies of mind and body are so severely
taxed, it is an ideal food.
Dr. Tibbies’Vi Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers, and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.
As an unparralleled test of merit, a dainty
sample tin of Dr. Tibbie’s Vi-Cocoa will be sent
free on application to any address, if when writing
(a postcard will do) the reader will name The
Bulletin.*
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 4 sizes, by post i/4, 2/G,
3/8, 4/8, in N.S.W. only. To all the other colo-
nies 1/8, 2/8, 3/8, 5/2. Beware of imitations. Only
address: Senior, chemist, 24(5George-st., Sydney.*

Lisson has been sent to a lunatic asylum.
Yet certain people wished to send the insane
assassin to the gallows.
Sydney D.T. staff are reported to be
Federalists to a man. However, they are Anti-
Federalists to a man-agement.
Eleven more solicitors added to the N.S.W.
roll—amongst them a great-grandson of the cele-
brated Major Mudie, of Castle Forbes.
Garrard and Gould “ Honorables.” Pro-
bably, in the long run, the most effectual way of
killing the appetite for such distinctions.
T. R. M'KTBBIN, another howler for the XI.
Vic. Minister of Agriculture Taverner was
in early life a coach-driver at Swan Hill. Orator
Deakin’s father likewise drove Cobh’s coaches.
Morpeth (N.S.W.) intends to put forth
its claims as site for the Federal capital. Morpeth
has one strong mortgage on the distinction, for
John Haynes says he was born there.
Arthur Horthun, who, in the early days,
lagged out more of the Barrier than any other
man, but did not succeed in striking anything
rich, died, the other day, wretchedly poor.
Frank Clark, who made way for Edmund
Barton’s admission to N.S.W. Parliament, struck
the public for a testimonial at an apathetic
moment. The subscription-list closes down with
£4O.
“ General ” Booth, on arrival in Adelaide,
looked fit for the grave instead of for a big cam-
paign. He was only able to walk with assistance,
and he seemed 20 years older than when here
last. His illness is attributed to overwork on the
voyage out.
There are points in being “on” a religious
paper. For instance, Sydney Catholic Press, in
announcing the serious illness of a valued lady
contributor of verse, asks its readers to “ pray for
her recovery.” The Bulletin poets, alas, are
past praying for.
Ebenezer Ward, M.L.C., of Adelaide,
joined London Morning Post as a junior at
the same time as Algernon Borthwick (Lord
Glenesk), the present proprietor, and they were
very close personal friends. Ebby talks of going
to England to renew the acquaintance.
What, a marvellous hold Kipling has, fur a
man still young ! No other writer since Tennyson
would have been made the subject of so much
cable fuss when on a sick bed. And yet it is
almost certain that the majority 50 years hence
will be quite unfamiliar with the name of Rudyard.
Barrister St. Leger, who fell before Forrest
and Macdonald Paterson in the contest for N.
Brisbane, was one of the deputies to Sydney at
the centenary celebration of the Irish Rebellion
last year. He fought N. Brisbane in the Labor
interest, and is the type of man bound to be heard
from again.
The fact that. JRudyard Kipling’s grand-
fathers (on both sides) were Wesleyan ministers
induced the Adelaide Conference to pray for his
recovery. The improvement in his condition
occurred immediately afterwards. Unfortunately
the conference did not know of his daughter’s ill-
ness until too late.
“H.R.L.”: People who consider the
character of Thekla Dubberke unique fail to note
its similarity to that of Goneril in “ King Lear.”
Shakespeare—that universal authority on female
character—evidently considered the Thekla Dub-
berke species of importance enough to typify in
perhaps his strongest tragedy.

“J.F.”: Apropos that eccentricity of the
Duke of Northumberland, there is a well-known
family in Northern N.S.W. which keeps a vacant
chair on the right of the head of the house at
each meal, awaiting the coming Messiah.
Parkes loved to tell this story about one
of his most slavish supporters, The latter, who
had in former years been a butcher, was addres-
sing his constituents, and in perorating came to —
“I have served you, ladies and gentlemen”
and then paused. Then a voice at the back
yelled, “ With sausages ! ”
“ Hermes ” : It is an awful thing to live in
even the same terrace as a poet. In print he may
be more or less charming, but in the flesh he is
generally a horror. If you try to take him
home he falls down in the street and makes
libellous remarks about your mother. This
writer knows one, the most decent poet he
has ever met, who celebrates publication-day by
filling-up with strange drinks, buying the first
news-boy’s entire stock, a few second-hand books,
and four tins of lobster. About 2 a.m. he hies
him to a cab-rank and deposits the papers in the
first cab, the books in the second, the lobsters in
the third, himself in the fourth, and drives home
in procession amid rhapsodies of blasphemy.
Geoffrey Eagar, the tedious anti-Bill critic
and organiser, is a son of Geoffrey Eagar, the suc-
cessive holder of portfolios in N.S. W. Parliaments
of the past. Eagar, sen., earned great praise m
his day for his clear dissection of financial state-
ments —which seems to make Eagar, jun., think
himself a dab on the finances of the Common-
wealth.
Wellington (M.L.) Trades and Labor
Council contributed £2 towards the fund being
raised to erect a statue to the memory of Hon
Grey in Great Britain. Auckland and Dunedin
didn’t subscribe a cent. And nobody is anxious
in Maoriland to give of his substance to build up
any local symbol of his grateful memory of the
old chief—who planted the seeds of Democracy in
the land of the Moa.
Sub-Inspector Musgrove, who died, the
other day, at Bathurst, accompanied Supt. Sander-
son atthequellingof theanti-Chinese riots at Lamb-
ing Flat, away back in the early 60’s. Previous to
that he had been stationed at Rockhampton, when
Queensland was part of N.S.W. Originally he
was one of the Queen’s Guards in England.
Kiandra and Lambing Flat, however, supplied
him with his most vivid remembrances.
Parkes, when in power, got his own por-
trait hung in N.S.W. National Art Gallery.
Dibbs followed suit with a portrait of Dibbs.
Now Reid follows suit with a portrait of Reid.
The national Pantheon is being crammed, in the
name of Art, with the effigies of gods who worship
themselves. The Gallery is supposedly educa-
tional ; but it is becoming a mere hoarding for the
blatant self-advertisement of impudent politicians.
Primate Smith’s leave-taking of the ami-
able Gov. Hampden was affectionate and paternal,
but, on the whole, strictly secular. Bishop Barker,
the much-married, was a hotter soldier of the
church army. His way was to seize the outgoing
Governor by the forearms, drag him to his knees,
and pray with him for a long five minutes. If he
could catch the Gov.’s wife and family at the
same time, so much the better. He rounded all
into a group, and, leading the way in knee-drill,
parted from them in prayer.

The envied M.R.C.P. degree has been
secured by that earnest student, Dr. G. E. Rennie,
ex-N.S.\V. Govt. Pathologist, and youngest son
of the N.S.W. Auditor-General. Dr. Rennie,
who greatly distinguished himself during his
career at London University years ago, proposes
remaining in England for a few years to pursue
his studies in the more obscure nervous diseases,
and will, on his return, be heartily welcomed as a
consultant by the medical profession of Sydney.
“ General " Booth, as usual, tried to con-
vert the pressmen who interviewed him in Ade-
laide, but the reporters, as it happened, were the
two hardest cases in S.A. One of them still has
a testament presented to him by Capt. Guise,
Gov. Buxton’s pious aide-de-camp. Three S.A.
Register reporters, by-the-way, are local preachers,
and one bombards the devil in the park lands on
Sundays.
An Australian Judge has a houseful of re-
lations, nephews and nieces too numerous to men-
tion, living together as one family, and a particu-
larly happy family, too. They are all wards of
his Honor’s, and the existence of the collection is
due to a strange series of fatalities amongst the
elder relatives of the Judge. The year 1868 was
a calamitous one ; the unusual run of fatalities re-
curred in ’7B, and again in ’BB, and so impressed
was his Honor with the significance of the coinci-
dence that he was quite convinced that he must
go under in ’9B, and made every preparation for
the sad event—put all his affairs in order, and was
with difficulty dissuaded from resigning his judge-
ship. Evidently the Fates having his family
affairs in hand were off duty in 1898, and his
Honor is still going well and strong, with a new
lease of life.
“ Locust ” obituarises Tyson :
‘‘What a time he might have had, and didn’t?” But
this verdict depends upon the point of view. He may
have thought he was having a good time ; but we know
better. Enough evidence lies in the fact that each of us,
knowing that he didn’t share our particular rapture,
believes that Tyson had no raptures worth mentioning.
He seems to have occupied most of his days in finding
out things he could do without, and doing without ’em.
The number of things he gave up before he finally added
the ghost to the list, must have been remarkable. It is
definitely fixed that he had onty two habits left —sleeping
out and cutting up and rolling tobacco. Most men who .
live to be 40 spend a lot of time in giving up habits which
they have been acquiring from youth upwards - except in
cases where the habits give them up, which not infre-
quently happen. Tyson never did anything that rich
men do—he didn’t even make a will. He never globe-
trotted or bought pictures, or got himself painted by
Longstaff, or collected autographs or blue china, or kept
a harem, or racehorses, or a yacht. True, he was a col-
lector in his way—of bullocks ; but that was his only re-
deeming vice. A wasted life—quite wasted. Now, if—
but speculation is vain.

Sam. Begg, one of The Bulletin’s first
artists—he it was who drew the old-time elaborate
Bulletin heading—has long been a member of the
staff of Illustrated London News, in which
]>ap r many ixn, ortant drawings of his appear.
Vic.’s cheap Agent-General, the military
Clarke, was once so pronounced a Radical that he
polled the entire workingman vote of South Mel-
bourne. But his opinions then were a mere pass-
ing attack. His father was Governor of Wes-
tralia, and he himself for a time Governor of
Straits Settlements. He is now in his 75th year.
George Barton, big brother to Toby, is
about the most consistent pen-wielder in Sydney.
He practically lives at the Public Library in the
daytime, writing hard from the opening of the
Library until dusk, except at lunch-time, when he
tikes ten minutes in the Gardens. Anything
that smells like Old Australia strikes responsive
chords in his chest, and he has “colonial ” history
dropping ready-made off his finger-ends. Per-
sonally he is very tall and meagre, and looks about
as urdike Toby as even a brother can look.
“ Tui Tea ” tells The Bulletin a Grey
anecdote :
Near Hori Grey’s residence on Kawau stood a great
pohutukawa tree, whose sprawling branches made it easy
to reach almost any part of the trunk. On said trunk
“ distinguished visitors ” had made a practice of carving
their initials. One portion was devoted to alleged poets,
and for long Domett crowned the list with his modest
“A.D.” But there came a day when William Richard
Wills, the bard of Otahuhu, was incited by Grey to climb
the tree and place his initials beside the rest. Wills was
proposing to carve his mark at the foot, but Grey said,
gently but persistently, “No, no, I’/fils ; above Domett-
above him ; you will be famous s vhen he is forgotten.
Wills did as bidden ; and told the yarn to this scribe a t
an evidence of Grey’s prejudice against Domett (purely a
political prejudice) ; for Wilis had enough good sense to
know that Domett was far and away his elder brother in
the Muse. It is more than doub'ful, however, that Grey
would so betray himself. More likely it was a little trap
of his to try the bard’s vanity, and Wills was so busy with
his carving that he failed to notice the transverse
wrinkles on the bridge of old Hori’s nose, which were to
the initiated a sure sign that he was hugely enjoying a
lark. Wills, by the way, wrote an “ Ode on the Death of
Sir George Grey ” at a time when be and Hori were in
the full enjoyment of health ; and, it is believed, he
almost prayed for the Grand Old Man’s death so that he
might paralyse the world with his magnificent ode—just
dashed-off, you know—next morning. But poor Wills
took cancer, and died three years before the Proconsul,
and the ode is still unpublished. Domett is still known
to fame, but many will ask—“ Who in thunder wa9
Wills ? ”

Chief Justice Madden is a bad Catholic, if
a Catholic at all. Which explains the sting in
the tail of Father O’Farrell’s rebuke to his Honor
for having, in his summing-up at the boot-box
trial, imputed harlotry to Mary Magdalene (a
mistake, by the way, into which the almost-
omniscient Gladstone fell long ago). Said the
preacher:—
As Mary Magdalene by her example had been the means
of leading many a poor’ sinner to the foot of the Cross, I
hope she will sweetly revenge herself on those who
traduce her by obtaining for them a like grace, and lead-
ing them to the foot of the Cross, there to repent of their
sins.
Mr. Justice Bundey is far and away the
most popular man in S. A., and if there were an
election for Governor, and he would accept the
position, he would be elected by a thumping
majority. After 15 years’ service on the bench, he
is about to take a year’s holiday, and, in pursu-
ance of his old-time yachting predilections, most
of it will be spent on or near the sea. He is 61
years old, a Crow-eater of over 50 years, and a
barrister of 34 years’ standing. He was never de-
feated during his Parliamentary career, and has
put in several terms as Minister of the Crown.
He is a sweet-dispositioned man who loves
society, but, since his elevation to the bench, he
has lived in the strictest seclusion.
Rev. McEachern, at whose movement
Rev. Hector Ferguson has been haled before Vic.
Presbyterian convention on a charge of heresy,
represents the “auld Scots school o’ kail an’
damnation ” in church government. It was his
sort that frightened Gavazi into the utterance —
“ My God ! I turn from Rome as from a tyrant,
to encounter worse in a Geneva gown.” When
Strong was hunted for his heresy, McEachern
was in the van of the wolves. “It’s eneuch,” he
cried, “ to mak’ a raon believe in a pairsonal
deevil.” He is said to he so strict a Sabbatarian,
that he will hear the agonies of a shooting corn
rather than pare the excrescence down on the
Sawhath day.
“ Annie Fraser ” asks Sydney D.T. to re-
print this, from Melb. Age :
Sydney never had the smallest chance of being the
capital of Federated Australia. In the first place, the
climate is not fit for the home of a progressive people. It
was evolved when Dame Nature was surrounded by her
stewpans ; and it has never lost the character she then
gave to it. It must be the depressing atmosphere of Syd-
ney which has ever lain like a pall upon the energies of
hervitizens, and kept them half a century in the rear of
Victorian progress. For this reason alone —the all-dis-
solving and infectious limpness in the air—a Federal Par-
liament would never have dreamed of making its capital
in Sydney, added to which any such preliminary condi-
tion would have barred the Victorian entry.”
Which is all pretty true, making due allowance
for the Age’s natural irritation at the jibes of the
Cahbage-tre© Mob. Furthermore, Sydneyites
know it’s true and don't object to the allegation
when it’s made more gently. As thus : Roderie
Quinn, in his coining book of verses, sings of the
Sydney nor-easter, the Handmaiden of Paralysis :
And so on. The other day, a Bulletin man
asked one of the very biggest anti-Federalists
whether he didn’t think Sydney’s advantages in
the matter of coal would, in the long run, furnish
her with an effectual advantage over Victoria in
the manufacturing struggle. “No,” replied the
Sydneyite—boss of perhaps the biggest industrial
concern in N.S.W. “in the Melbourne climate
you can get so much more out of a man in eight
hours.” Which is a chocolate for “Annie
Fraser ” to turn over in her no doubt pretty little
mouth.
Woe to the weak when the sky is shrouded.
And the wind of the salt-way sobs as it dies 1
Woe to the weak ! for a great dejection
Droops their spirits and drowns their eyes.
Woe to the weak who tire of fetters,
Of grim life-fetters that.gall and bind !
For the Sea tells stories of death made lovely,
And a siren sings in the nor’-east wind.
It wanders the coast like a tombless spectre,
And drips dank dew on the drooping leaf;
And the soul grows pensive with dun suggestions
Of grey old troubles and ancient grief.
Tis grave and low, and with woeful plaining
Sighs death-notes under a sky of grey,
And who hath an ear may hear the voices
Of pale men dead on its streaked sea-way.
In fading twilights o’er sullen seascapes,
A lost, wan wind ’neath a dead grey sky,
It swoons to land like a weary swimmer,
Sobs and falters and turns to die.
Seeking a tomb in dark, coast caverns,
Where the wet rust reddens the fretted stone,
The wandering sea-thing sinks to silence—
Sinks and dies with a last low moan.

Aboriginalities.
No man in broad Australia is a better
judge of stock or knows more about station-
management than squatter O’Bullswool. So com-
pletely indeed did the acquirement of this useful
knowledge absorb the waking moments of his
youth and early manhood that he had no time left
wherein to learn to read and write. He was in
Sydney iately and heard of “The Geisha,” of
course. “Oi wint to see that there comic operer
larst night,” he said. “Yes, I liked it very well
in most ways,” he went on ; “ but in one way it’s
a dashed fraud. They calls it ‘The Grazier,’
and there isn’t a word about squattin’ in it from
start to finish ! ”

“ Victorian ” : The Federal capital should
be called Eureka, to emphasise the most
prominent landmark in Australian history.
Perhaps there is not enough patriotism handy
to insist upon this just now, bub surely there
is sufficient to give a hard kick in the right
direction. Failing this, “and still in the interest”
of patriotism, the name chosen should have no
rhyme in the language. This would encourage
originality, and prevent the capital being dragged
in the mire by budding bards committing atrocious
“ pomes ” thereon, and sending them to The Bul-
letin. Suggested that it be named after some
politician. Look at the possibilities in the hands
of a bardlet: Brunker —drunker ; boot-trunk,
or ; “ chunk,” or. Perish the thought! Again,
Reid—bleed, greed, weed, feed, &c. Call it Grin-
jeegongongalong first!

A correspondent, school-teaching in the
Never Never, on the “ Awful Immorality of Out-
back ”:—
I have wanted to get up for Class 11. for about five
years and have never got a good-enough report. Here,
I never hope to get one. The youngsters are, with few
exceptions, the most miserable and lowest type of kids
that ever I came across. About half of them are the
illegitimate offspring of people who have been bred up in
the hills here like wallabies, and with about as much re-
gard to decency and civilised habits. The other half are
crossbreds between native blacks and the alleged whites.

“ A.E.G. : Bronnigan was a strongman
—stronger than any of vour Samsons. One day
B. was out on his run. Down in a gully he came
across a dead bullock. Knew he couldn’t get a
dray in for carcase, which was wanted by pigs,
Then he skinned it and took out entrails, got in-
side, and off he went with the lot. You could just
see his legs to the knees, but he could see ahead
right enough, and staggered homewards. Just as
he got out on the road, a parson was driving over
a rise of the hill. Parson’s horse got a sight,
wheeled round, and down the hill again as hard as
he could travel. The parson was thrown out on
hia back, the trap was smashed to smithereens,
and the horse has not been seen since. Must have
gone until he dropped, and then rolled down
somewhere. Parson stayed at Bronnigan’s that
night. He told a strange story of a “ skinned
and headless beast ” he had seen capering about
on his hind legs, but Bronnigan said nary a word.
When ho told the yarn in town, his friends put it
down to drink. The parson was very wild.

One of Q.’s leading planters wanted
“boys” for sugar-work, and brought up some
kanakas before the Polynesian Inspector to “ sign
on.” Among them was one named Nulli, who
had, eight months or so previously, been sentenced
to a year in Brisbane gaol. The Inspector, hear-
ing his name called, looked up, and said, “ What
for, Nulli? You been get-em twelve month, and
only eight months gone. What for you no longa
gaol?” Nulla (suavely): “Yes, massa, me
get-em tvvel-low mon’s; but Guv’men’ he been
break-em agreement”

IN THE NEVER-NEVER.
A man with a stake in the country,
“6 x 8”: Sheep are very weak in the
drought-stricken west, and a piteous spectacle is a
ewe defending her newly-born lamb against the
attacks of the fiendish crows. The poor animals
are surrounded by the black marauders as soon as
the lamb is born, and the only way the weak
mother can save the eyes of the offspring is to
stand over and cover them.

“ R.H.” : The newest of new women lives
on the Flinders,N.W.Q. She drives 12 bullocks—
carrying goods from the line to stations in-
country. Have seen her drive (on her “ own ”), but
would like to see her yoke and unyoke.
* * * *
A shanty on the Emu Bay Co.’s line lately
began to dispense doctored grog to its customers.
One of the gangers, getting a bad head, blamed
the “ snake-juice ” for it. Next evening, along
with a couple of pals, he felled a tree across the
shanty and gave warning that any other estab-
lishment vending “ dynamite ” would be similarly
treated.
* * « *
A semi-civilised Port Lincoln-side black
died in the township the other day. The scat-
tered aborigines in the district were called in by
signal-fires, and next day walked to the cemetery
in couples, each one carrying a bough of mallee-
bush.
* * * *
“ Long-’Un ” : Be water-mallee as a thirst-
quencher. Writer’s experience is that the hard
work entailed is not nearly compensated for by
the small quantity of water obtained. A man’s
usually very dry when he has to start groping in
the earth for a drink, and, generally speaking, he’s
drier still as he dejectedly resumes his journey.
A few years ago, travelling through the Nannyah
country (between the Murray and the Broken Hill
railway), came across waggon-loads of heaped-up
roots, and the remains of ancient wurlies. In the
early days, Nannyah (an aboriginal) murdered a
man on the river. Whilst under arrest, two gins
effected his escape into this pretty wide strip of
waterless desert, and, in the course of many years,
the family numbered 30 or 40, with the grey-
bearded Nannyah as king. White people occa-
sionally saw dusky, naked forms disappearing in
the distance, but the tribe was very shy and timid
(probably owing to the tainted early history of its
founder), and lived almost entirely on “ goanner ”
and water from the mallee. At last a couple of
station boundary-riders rounded up a mob of these
natives, and drove them into Chowilla with a
view to establishing a harem. Some drowned
themselves, others escaped in various directions,
and, in the course of time, the once mysterious
Nannyah tribe became extinct. Its only memento
is the heaps of mallee-roots.

“Gr. Mac.”: Be statement of “S.R.”in
B. 18/2/’99 that kangaroo cannot swim. Undiluted
rot! Nor is “ W.C.K.” in B. 4/3/’99 quite correct
as to kangaroo being (necessarily) on bottom when
drowning a dog. Years ago, by Wallamba,
a tidal river up north, I suddenly became aware
of a great “ row ” on the bank, and rushing down
found that a young woman on horseback, with
two big, rough dogs, had driven an “old man ”
kangaroo into the river. My dog, a heavy,
ferocious brute, at once jumped in and joined the
fray. As a dog came within reach the old fellow
gripped it in his arms and held it under, till again
and again we were sure it was drowned. And
without doubt it would have been, had nob the
others kept up the attack, forcing the ’roo, in self-
defence, to release the drowning brute. But he
only let go of one to grapple with another —and
down it went. His style of swimming was up-
right-similar to that of a human treading water,
but that may have been owing to the desperate
fight he waged from shore to shore—about 80yds.
Having battled across he stood with his back
to the steep bank and defied the dogs. On dry
land they would have soon settled him. He was
less than 20min. swimming across, and as . the
depth was nowhere less than 10ft. he certainly
didn’t touch bottom.

“ White Eye ” : Ever hear of a pig work-
ing? Knew an old chap who had one that he
used for plantation-grubbing. Used to tie him by
neck to an almond-tree, and, when piggy had
rooted round it enough to suit the old boy, he was
shifted on to another. This man also had a port-
able fowl-house, and kept fowls shut up in it.. He
used to hitch himself on to house and shift it
about the plantation. Saved feeding hens, he said.
* • * •
You will see more Chows than Maoris
riding bikes in Maoriland (writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent). The latter have never cottoned on
to the machine—they’re too lazy. One of the
first principles of Mr. Day and Martin is to exert
himself as little as possible. The old Maoris
consider the bike the latest instance of the white
man’s folly. Logic of a leading chief: “The
man who rides a horse and shoves it along with
his foot is a fool; he might as well walk.”

A Shoalhaven black gin has been offering
her services about Sydney. Her qualifications are
reading, writing and harmonium-playing, and she
is the growth of one of the mission stations.

Wire from Singleton, N.S.W. :
A few days since a proprietor of a large estate in this
district treated several of his tenants to a trip to Lake
Macquarie. Several of them, between 40 and 60 years of
age, had never seen the sea before, and one of them ex-
pressed surprise at the sight, with the remark that “ the
water must run up-hill, as it is much higher right out
there.”

The Queensland police had no scruples in
arresting and detaining Burgess on suspicion of
having committed the Gatton outrage ; and Bur-
gess is still in gaol—for what Heaven only knows.
If they had only been equally quick to lock-up
everyone about whom there was any sort of prima-
facie suspicion, and let them all stew awhile in
their own gravy, the grisly secret would soon have
been out. There can be no earthly doubt that the
deed was done by a person or persons well-known
to the Murphys, and even now, if the police
could find any sort of pretext for locking-up half-
a-dozen people or so, whom almost any idiot could
sort out, it would probably soon be found what
the Gatton outrage was stuffed with.

A young Bendigo miner, Edward Richards,
lately had a fair run of luck for one shift. Shaft-
sinking is going on at the J. R. Exploration Co.’s
Black Forest mine. There is timbering down to
500 ft., the shaft being divided into three compart-
ments ; and Richards and his mates were sinking
25ft. lower. Five fuses were put in and lighted ;
then the men got into the bucket and the haul-up
began. But somehow Richards’ head and shoul-
ders got into one compartment while the bucket
was going into the next one. In half a second he
would have been dragged out. Instead of waiting
he grabbed the timbers and got his legs free. His
only hope was to drop nearly 25ft.. among the
fired charges. But he did it; and then, in the
dark, he groped about and got out the five burning
fuses. Then he signalled to lower, stepped into
the bucket, and went home.

BACKBLOCKS SATIRE.—“THE FIRST TRAIN TO HILLSTON”
Hillston (Lachlan) people have been agitating for a railway. As a parting and hopeless shot at the Govt, for not granting the line from Carra-
thool some local farmers devised the Hillston street procession above depicted—“the first train to Hillston,” a “one-horse” affair. The costumes
are typical of the drought times now prevailing. The loco, engine is fired with tarred rags and clothed with ditto. The driver and guards are
dummies, and perform their duties well. The “fust"-class compartment is unoccupied (also in accordance with the times), but stock traffic is re-
presented by two goats. The admiring residents in the foreground belong to
the stone age.
He’s a great big bouncing nigger,
And he cuts a curious figure,
For he’s mostly hair and cudgel, and he’s seldom very
clean ;
And he runs ’em in by dozens,
All his dusky, dirty cousins,
And he downs ’em with his bludgeon —for he’s loyal to
the Queen 1
He may not look quite romantic
May this peeler-man gigantic,
But his presence adds a glamor and a glory to the scene,
He’s a sort of living sermon,
For we know, beneath his vermin,
He’s a Briton to the backbone —and he’s loyal to the
Queen.
And we gaze at him and wonder
As he daily grows rotunder —
Was he really once the cannibal that dined upon the
green ?
Did his mem’ry make him grateful
To the race that filled his plate full :
Did his love for British thigh-bone make him loyal to the
Queen ?
Dubberke, who stands on the lowest of fame,
Dubberke, who ought to be blushing- with shame,
Dubberke, the laihj whom no one could vex,
Dubberke, who is a disgrace to her sex,
Dubberke, whom no honest woman would own,
Dubberke, whose heart is as hard as a stone,
Dubberke, accomplice of dear Mr. Tod —dy,
Dubberke, who said she’d rip open the body.
Dubberke, who looks at us straight in the face,
And tells us she crammed the poor girl in the case.
Dubberke, whose heart pumps the coldest of blood,
Dubberke, thank God, she is nipped in the bud.
Dubberke, who dresses in stylish of pink,
Whose conscience and morals are blacker than ink ;
Dubberke, who don’t know what purity means,
Dubberke, who scarcely is out of her teens,
Dubberke, who thinks not of death or of dying,
Dubberke, who laughs where she ought to be crying,
Dubberke, most callous, despicable, mean,
Deserted her mates and turned for the Queen ;
Dubberke, who fears neither Heaven or Hell,
But to save her own body decided to tell;
Dubberke, the worst of the whole blessed three,
It seems most outrageous that you should be free.
Dubberke, you may not get punishment now,
You'll reap it hereafter, I certainly vow,
On the day of atonement—a lovely reward—
From the Judge of the Universe, Almighty God.

Will use Bective : Even if one of Mary Magdalene’s
“ seven devils ” was not the ordinary luring, tempting,
beckoning woman’s imp, and she was as chaste as Caesar’s
wife wasn’t, it doesn’t matter much. What is certain is
that Father O’Farrell’s own church, and all the other
churches, have chosen to take her as the type of a class,
and to found maternity hospitals labelled with her name,
and “ refuges ” decorated with her imaginary portrait;
and all through literature “Magdalene” has been accepted
as a convenient synonym for mealy-mouthed people to
shudder by. The original Magdalene is merely a wraith
of legendary mist; but the current conception of her is a
living fact. When practically all civilisation decides
that a woman of the year 30 or thereabouts was
a fallen woman, then she was a fallen woman
to all intents and purposes of common parlance

NEWS FROM THE LANCEIIS.
SINGLETON, Monday.— A. letter from one of
the local Lancers tells of an accident which
occufred to the kangaroo being taken to England
with the homeward bound contingent. The
marsupial pet was running about the decks when
it ererbalanced itself and went headlong down
the hold, a depth of 40ft. When halfway in its
descefct it struck a sailor, who was climbing
the ladder, and carried him down also. _ Strange
to say, however, neither'was very much injured.

Thorough 1 S.M. Herald advt. : —
■VTOTIOE.—I, Patrick , of -—, will not be respon-
Bible for any Debts contracted by my Wife from this
date. Also, wanted, an elderly Woman, as Housekeeper.
John Farrell has been visiting Tasmania,
and now writes charmingly in Sydney Daily
Telegraph concerning the old house at Both well
where, almost 50 years ago, lived John Mitchel,
the Irish exile :—
I cut a branch from the dying apple tree in front of
Nant Cottage—the apple tree under whose shade in sum-
mer evenings Mitchel wrote much of the Jail Journal, or
taught his children, while his wife, Jenny, sang about the
house, in the current happiness of great, success as a
dairywoman. To her, the grey-haired widow yet living
with her one remaining child, a widowed daughter, in
Brooklyn, U.S.A., I have sent some of its leaves in token
of reverential regard for her and for the memory of the
brave dead man whose helpmeet she was. I looked long
at the place and its surroundings as one notes things
made sacred by the past. Here was the hill with its
eucalyptus and honeysuckle trees that Mitchel has made
known to us ; here the slopes on which he and Martin
often reclined immersed in thought of what had been and
might have been. All about here pattered the feet of
children lying in graves, separated by the seas and con-
tinents. Private William Mitchel, the youngest boy, lost
in the frightful slaughter of Pickett’s division at
Gettysburg; Captain John Mitchel, the eldest, killed
by a shell while commanding officer of Fort Sumter;
James, who passed through the war unscathed,
fighting, like his brothers, against the North;
and Henrietta, the eldest daughter, who became
a Roman Catholic nun, and sleeps in a French
cemetery at Montparnasse. This Nant Cottage was the
peaceable half-way house in the passage of John Mitchel’s
public life, from the storm in which it began to the
greater convulsions to come. It seemed hard to believe
that in this mouldering tenement far away in quiet Tas-
mania once lived a man who afterwards became the lead-
ing press champion of the South for separation from the
North, who was imprisoned with Jefferson Davis in Fort
Monroe, and whose death was publicly mournpd by every
State in the Union as that of a powerful friend or a noble
enemy, as well as, notwithstanding the dead man’s
errors of judgment, by Irish people all the world
over. And poor “ Jenny ” , in the terrible
experiences of the after years, did she not think regret-
fully of the placid sufficiency of life at Nant Cottage? To
her was given the burden of being a militant reformer’s
wife, which she never sought to escape. But when she
was left marooned, with loss of all worldly possessions,
on the desolate shore of North Carolina, after running
the blockade in a heroic attempt to bring succour and
comfort to her soldier husband and sons; or while the
houses burned all round her shell-shattered home when
Richmond fell; or indeed often before and after in the
painful vicissitudes of her sorrowful life, did not the
heart of this sorely-stricken woman turn longingly back
to the years passed here unanxiously by the sweet Tas-
manian Clyde?
The evidence in the Queenscliff shooting
case shows that Pollock was subjected to some such
badgering as Simmons was in Kipling’s fine story,
“In the Matter of a Private.” Pollock “had a
rat,” and was reminded of the fact pretty often,
and eventually the mnn who monkeyed with a
man who “ had a rat ” when the latter also had a
gun, died—as was quite in the natural order of
tilings. Moral: Never consider a man ridiculous
when he holds a gun.

He was Reprieved.
In France, a man sentenced to death is not told
in advance on what day he is to die. The first in-
formation he gets is from the officers who prepare
him for the guillotine. Between sentence and
execution the prisoner’s life is full of terrors, for
any footfall outside his cell may herald the mes-
senger of death.
_ So free and honest men suffer agonies of irngina-
tion when they learn that they have diseased kid-
neys. They may live for years. They may die
before another sunset. Kidney trouble is more
deadly than consumption if neglected or im-
properly treated. It is a great mercy that one
sure remedy exists.
The many old frie.nds of Gf. H. Wood, Esq., ex-
J.P., in Queensland and New South Wales, as.
well as. the newer ones of New Zealand, will read
of his good fortune with interest and pleasure. In
a letter dated Dunedin, 15th July, '1)8, lie says :
“I have much pleasure in bearing testimony to
the efficacy of Warner’s Safe Cure and Safe Pills.
Over five years I had suffered from derangements
of the liver and kidneys, so much so that at times
I felt unfit for business, iny whole system being
affected, but more particularly my head. Some-
times I thought I had congestion of the brain, and
then I was told I had Bright's disease. Even-
tually I found my liver and kidneys were out of
order, and that was the cause of my troubles. I
was recommended by a friend to take Warner’s
Safe Cure and Safe Pills. After the first four
bottles I felt as well as ever I did in my life. I
am quite confident of the good effects it has had.
I take this first opportunity of sending an un-
solicited testimonial, of which the proprietors of
Warner’s Safe Cure can make whatever use they
choose.”*

POLITICAL
POINTS
The N’Yestor of Australian politics —
Reid-
There are nine open enemies and 99 secret
enemies of Federation in N.S.W. Assembly.
Practically the whole N.S.W. country
press barracks for the amended Federation Bill.
The Queensland elections are a duel
between Labor and Leprosy.—Tasmanian Fede-
ralist.
On the discussion re the Federal Enabling
Bill in N.S.W. Assembly Labor-leader M'Gowen
said —nothing.
M‘Gowan, N.S.W. Labor-leader, travails
in spirit. His small party drifts to the rocks, and
the old wheel won’t work. But G. H. Reid lately
assured him of a seat in the Federal House of
Representatives.
Over-confidence may yet be the undoing
of the federalists. G. Republican Dibbs, Dr.
MacLaurin, Jno. Norton and Geo. Pile’s dog are
starting early, and may yet work up a vigorous
crusade against the Bill on the Capital cry.
Sydney anti-federalists at their big meet-
ing the other day found themselves corporeally
possessed of only one M.L.A., the dubious
M‘Laughlin. That should have told the meeting
that the people were going up another street.
Spruson, M.P., in N.S.W. Assembly, re-
ferred to the half-empty galleries as a proof the
public did not want Federation. Premier Reid :
“ The hon. member is out of order in speaking of
the galleries. But, go on ; I perceive he is speak-
ing to the galleries.”
Certain N.S.W. Opposition members, who
regarded as rosy their prospects of portfolios, fear
a total eclipse of hopes as a result of Federation.
One portfolio is worth 40 Federations to them.
However, one Federation is worth 40 million office-
seekers to the people.
The fiscal red h irring again. Sydney
Daily Telegraph: -‘lt 'is believed N.S.W.
National Protection Union will recommend the
acceptance of the Bill.” Now, N.S.W. National
Protection Union, like the N.S.W. Freetrade
Association, is practically defunct.
Melb. Trades Hall Council occasionally
resents the success of the Anti-Sweating League,
and its occasional encroachments into Trades Hall
preserves. A vice-president the other day alluded
to the League’s presiding genius as “ the man who
tried to start a new Trades Hall in Melbourne.”
You can tell a paper’s policy by the letters
it “leads.” When the O’Sullivan writes that
“suspicious people might be pardoned if they
imagine that Premier Reid would barter away
Sydney Harbor or pawn the Blue Mountains to
retain office,” Sydney D.T. “bumps it out.”
Poor Joss !
JOR DON v. ALL-COMERS (simultaneously).
Some sketches at Sydney School of Arts.
Ex-Opposition-leader Lyne is averred to
have prepared a long and elaborate speech against
the new Federal proposals. His contribution to
the debate was an unimportant interjection.
There is hope for Lyne. The politician who
remains silent after charging himself with thunder
is not altogether lost.
The Cairns Chinese assembled lately in
their chief joss-house and decided, on account of
recent ructions, to give neither sleeping nor board-
ing accommodation to Malays. The heathen is
good on the boycott—the Jap carrying the system
to a high degree of perfection. Some day the
white may learn a lesson.
At one time no S.A. public service secre-
taryship carried less than .£6OO salary, but Gar-
diner, the new Public Works sec. and one of the
ablest men in the service, takes the billet at A‘sso.
And S.A. heads of departments complain that,
under the Kingston rSfiime, not only are salaries
sweated, but independence of action is frowned
upon.
In all his speeches Q, Premier Dickson
starts by weeping over his predecessor, “whose
loss was a national one.” Certainly! Any cir-
cumstance, from the death of the Premier to the
marriage of a kanaka, which results in making so
hideously commonplace a property-agent as Dick-
son into a Prime Minister, may well be termed a
national loss.
Hie population of Melbourne inside the
ten-mile radius was estimated on 3lst. Dec ’9B
at 469,680, an increase of 11,196 for the year.’’The
Yarra-bank city is now nearly up to the figures of
the gorgeous boom-days again-provided the esti-
mates are correct. But suspicion always attaches
to estimates, especially when there has been no
census for eight years.
S.A. Laborites intend to run several candi-
dates for country districts at the general election
next month—a decision that has caused much
annoyance to provincial Liberals who agree with
the Labor programme. The party has, by the
way, got out of its delicate position on the
federal question by making it an open matter.
Of prominent Laborites, leader Batchelor opposes.
Brisbane’s nearest approach to a comic
paper is undoubtedly the Courier. It appears to
be edited by a person who would make an admir-
able figure-head for some Little Bethel for
kanakas. Ihe wind-up of a column on the black-
labor question :
i and Japanese have to be kept at arm’s
length, and the kanaka must be cot rid of by decrees ;
but until the millennium comes it will be impossible to do
more than fight the mischief step by step.
Cardinal Moran is quoted as being of
opinion that Sydney should be the Federal seat of
Government because it is “ the key of the Pacific ”
The expression has a good ring about it, like the
noise of a big door-key falling on a brass image of
bt. Peter But the Pacific is pretty large, and
Auckland, Manila, Callao, Thursday Island,
bhanghai, ban Francisco, Valparaiso, Vancouver
and lots of other places are all keys. The place is
full of keys. And what does the Federal Govern-
ment want to sit on a key for?
During the absence of the Q. Dead broke
Cabinet on the serious work of trying to save its
political neck in the electorates, nearly all the
departments are being run by two Leg. Coun-
cillors—Barlow and Grey. Barlow is the bleak-
faced earthly god of the local Yellow Pup, and
Gray is now chief political flunkey to the R C
faction. Had the Bill attempted by the late
Byrnes—giving full executive powers to council-
lors temporarily holding portfolios—been passed
Barlow and Grey would have made things hum in
the Oivii berviee.
It is often asserted in Bananaland that the
Govt, officials in Papua have been using their
opportunities to pick the eyes out of any lands
thrown open. It is alleged that they also do a
large and flourishing trade with the natives in
rubber, sandalwood, and other articles.
Ibis matter would bear looking into.
The scheme of Federation dominat-
ing the minds of Australians 30 years ago
embraced a partitioned Queensland, much
as the residents in the Northern and
Central parts of that province now con-
tend for. Westralia was then a Crown
colony, and, of course, stood out, but
still, in the theories of the time, a Federa-
tion of seven provinces—Tasmania, Vic.,
N.y.W., S.A., Queensland to the 25th
degree of latitude, Captieornia to the 20th
and Carpentaria to the Gulf—was con-
sidered not only feasible, but quite within
practical reach. The residents in the pro-
posed separated territories, supported the
scheme, and were well pleased with the
names suggested for their use, Caprieornia
being the coinage of Sir Thomas Mitchell,
and Carpentaria the invention of church-
man and Parliamentarian Dr. Lang.
At a “Colonists”—quaint old-time,
designation anti-Federation meeting
held in Sydney the other day, Chicago
Dibbs, talking about the proposed Federal
capital, referred to his visit to Canada and
stated that when about to see Ottawa,
Canada s i ederal capital, he expected to
learn some great lessons from it, but found
it a small place, the platform of the station
not being as large as those of some subur-
ban stations around Sydney. He said
further that there was not a decent hotel
in the place, and gave other illustrations
of its insignificance. Which all goes to
prove, if it proves anything, that the
question of the Federal capital is not
worth yapping about; and that the racket
about the ruin of Sydney through not
getting the Federal capital is bosh—all
there is to lose is a handful of people and
a sleepy dog, or thereabouts. Not that
the anti-Federalists believe this, but in a
storm any port will do.
The Yic. Women's Political and Social
Crusade, a lately-formed women’s organisation on
broad democratic lines and free from the holy
element, has created some stir in Melb. by assert-
ing that sinall-boy labor is employed in certain
large factories in defiance of the Act, that children
with their fingers nipped off by machinery are
quite common in some suburbs, and that children
are hidden away in cupboards when inspectors are
sighted.
Why should not a town like Rockhampton,
that returns a Labor-Separatist at the head of the
poll, make an attempt to put in two Demo-
cratic supporters of autonomy ? At present that
sultry city sends in one Democrat and one Con-
servative, who are always, and naturally, at log-
gerheads before they get decently into Parlia-
mentary harness. Every other double electorate
in Bauanaland regularly sends up its two Pro-
gressive candidates, and why not Rockhampton ?

selves ; anti - Ministerialists

dents who will emulate Dick-

whole has not yet shown on the horizon.
Dear Bulletin.— You say that Barton’s
luck is in “ because nobody is attacking him.”
Really, the fact is deplorable. When Barton was
working like steam to effect Federation, and shone
as the greatest man in Australia, there was a sting
for. him in nearly every political tail. Premier
Reid gets all the venomous little digs now, and
Barton—sleeps, or awaits a golden opportunity,
or something.—T.D.
Another N.S.W. Laborifce, Ferguson, of
Broken Hill, grabbed at the first chance to get off
the fence and declare for the Commonwealth Bill.
He, of all the Labor-men, made perhaps the most
violent speeches against the Bill, and had ascribed
the worst motives to its supporters ; but a couple
of amendments which some of his fellow-solids
declare to be quite valueless induce him to accept
the measure. Also his constituency, despite his
yells, accepted the Bill without any amendment
at all.

Premier Reid sneered at O’Sullivan,
M. for making disparaging references to the
holidaying Attorney-General Want, and W. J.
Lyne a little later sneered at Reid for disparaging
the absent O’Sullivan. “It’s unfair to speak ill
of a man behind his back,” was the burden of
both. But is a man who occupies a paid public
position ever absent in the sense that should
shelter him from public criticism ? While he
holds an office and takes public pay he is officially
befpre the court of opinion, wherever his unim-
portant body may be.
No one who reads in last week’s Truth
John Norton’s parochial blast and curse against
Australian union and Australian nationhood in any
shape, would dream that but a few months ago
John was a candidate for Fitzroy division in the
Federal and Bartonite and Old Bill interest. And
the Old Bill wasn’t nearly so advantageous to
N. as the New Bill is. One of the joys of
the Federal movement is that it gives amusing
people like John a chance to explode in so manf
new places—and John’s explosions are like an
immensely long beer in a dry land.

Premier Reid, hoping to avoid personal
embarrassments—how his Ministry does evade
personal responsibility ! has placed N.S.W.
Supreme Court Judges—their claims for leave of
absence and pensions, their retirement and ap-
pointment—in the Department of the Attorney-
General. In these respects the change is directly
in the face of the often-expressed opinion of the
late Parkes. The appointment of a Supreme
Court Judge he held to be solely in the Premier’s
province, and contended that to make it even a
Cabinet matter was derogatory to the judicial
position. Iso real embarrassment is avoided by a
barrister-Premier turning over the duty to a
barrister-Attorney-General. In either case a
practising barrister appoints and otherwise con-
trols the Judge before whom he is likely to plead.
If, indeed, the power of appointing, etc., were
given to some layman of the Cabinet, the cause of
the embarrassment referred to would be removed.

THE LOST CHORD.

In the old days when “ Braddon’s Blot”
hadn’t one respectable friend in the country (The
Bulletin stood by it all through, but then The
Bulletin isn’t respectable), the old man from
Tasmania was allowed undisputed possession.
Nobody ever attempted to suggest that it was
anybody else’s Blot. Now it has become respect-
able ; six Premiers have struggled with it and
found that they couldn’t invent anything better
among them ; and about a dozen people are rising
up to say that it is really their Blot. Poor Brad-
don feels his Blot slipping. And yet if he had
attempted to offer it to any of these claimants
only three months ago he would have been re-
ceived as cordially as if he was trying to leave a
serpent on the dinner-table.
T. A. Dibbs, of tlie Commercial Banking
Co., says a daily paper, is of opinion that “half-
a-dozen of the commercial men of Sydney could
draft a satisfactory scheme of Federal finance ”
quite easily. But alas ! these alleged men of stu-
pendous intellect who could put everything right
never do it. They are so beastly selfish that
though they know all about it they won’t divulge
their splendid knowledge. Before the referen-
dum of June, ’9B, Attorney-General Want de-
clared at a public meeting that he could produce
in an hour a Draft Constitution which would
satisfy all the provinces, and when some hairy
per.- on asked him why he didn’t do it, he said
wildly that he would do it when the referendum
was over. But he never did. None of them ever
do.
A Bare Majority party is rising in the
anti-Federal interest, whose plaint is that it is all
wrong to let a bare majority put any province into
Federation unless the scheme is so adjusted that a
bar.- majority can take it out again :
A simple majority should be able to do only what a
simple majority at any future time will have the powerto-
undo, and this rule has always been recognised in Parlia-
mentary Government in the fact that Parliament by a
simple majority vote can revoke any of its own or its pre-
decessors’acts.
And the community replies, sadly, “Rabbits!”
The world isn’t built that way—it would be a
great deal better world if it was. A simple
majority passed the law which floated £22,000,000
of N.S.W. loans in 1883-6, but 25,000 majorities
can’t undo that law and prevent the country
owing the money. A simple majority passed the
hanging statutes of N.S.W. and all the majority
in the world can’t unhang the people who were
sttangled thereby. All the best lands in Aus-
tralia were sold' under the sanction of a bare
majority and there is no majority in the solar
system large enough to revoke the fact that they
are sold. The majority couldn’t even undo so
small a thing as the Parkes grant if it raged from
now to Doomsday, for Mrs. Parkes has spent the
mon°y on provisions. As an argument against
Federation the Bare Majority theory is something
awful.

Monday’s wires re the Queensland elec-
tions showed nothing significant except the win-
ning of the Valley and Gympie seats (three in all)
by the Labor-party, and the loss of South Bris-
bane (one only) by them. Whereupon Sydney
D.T., in a ponderous leader, points out the reasons
which have caused a steady decline in public feel-
ing for the Labor-party on the part of “ the people
of Queensland.” It laboriously traces their fail-
ing fortunes to their bygone trampling upon the
prejudices of the law’norder elements ofQ. society.
The Labor-party only wants to keep On failing
at this rate to be running the Big Sugar province
by the time the present elections are over. But
the proprietary controllers of the D.T., instinc-
tively hostile to labor movements, can’t see it.
The fact that, where the party has got a hold, it
can afford the luxury of running “solidarity”
candidates against independents of the Hoolan
and Cross stamp—and get them in—is not con-
sidered by the D.T. to afford any indication of
anything ; and the name of Bulcock is not even
breathed. The “people of Queensland” express
their views with extreme difficulty through the
medium of electoral rolls, manipulated with as
much exact science as theirs are, but, when the
numbers go up, it will still probably be found that
the Labor-party’s backers run the Government
supporters encouragingly close.
Arthur Russell, American dentist, 8 Bourke-
street E., Melb., makes beautiful teeth or teeth
beautiful, whichever is necessary. Also painless
operations.*
The new industry that has arisen in Free-
trade N.S. W. is technically known by the initiated
as “ monniker snatching.” As in most Freetrade
industries, the manufactured product is frequently
adulterated. The signature-lists of the Anti-Bill
petition are by no means all genuine. One mis-
guided and conscientious collector, who had deter-
mined to go straight, was sent out to a popular
suburban resort and scored 15 signatures as his
day’s work—and earned nothing. The price paid
is ss. a hundred (the Anti-Billites are still collect-
ing), but 40 is regarded as a good day’s work.
Indeed, signatures are so hard to get that any
number per diem from 25 up is paid for at the
same rate as a century. For a second hundred, or
portion thereof, 2s. 6d. extra is paid as an incentive
t 6 energy. The collectors present themselves at 9
o’clock every morning, and annex the dibbs —they
can’t be anything else but Dibbs in this connec-
tion. A winey collector, who had festivated on
yesterday’s good fortune, left his list in the
common room of his restaurant. Everybody
signed : Tottie Eewclothes, Snorky Snigger, Lew
Larrikin, &c., &e., over 50 signatures—and they
were paid for in due course next morning. The
writer has seen half-a-dozen demi-mondaines of
the ultra hard-shell variety waiting to give a
“bloke” they knew a lift with his signatures.
The decent men nearly all sign each other’s lists—
several times. Friends who care more about the
price of meat than Federation (either way), and
friends who care very much, all sign; for who
would stand in poor Jones’s way when there was
five bob hanging to it? And, to the man who has
been hungry himself, what plea is more irresistible
than that couched in an apostrophe to the Deity,
and concluding with a reference to the way things
are at home? Besides, any name will do !
