Whoever desires to drink a healthy and
refreshing mineral water is recommended to try
Zetz-Spar, which bubbles up from the stomach of
the earth at Ballimore, near Dubbo, (N.S.W.) —
one of the many places that is going to be the
Federal capital for dead certain. The writer has
just drunk a bottle, and he can confidently say
that he isn’t quite so dead as he was before drink-
ing—and when he begins to feel dead he is pretty
hard to shift, so this says something for the bever-
age. The Zetz-Spa Proprietary hangs out at 171
Kent-street, Sydney.*
Melbourne — i.e., the Collins-street portion
of it —is looking on with a broad grin at the
Rowan-Flanagan fight. Flanagan scored first
blood by means of a “private and confidential”
circular, stating that Andrew Rowan has been a
bad, bold boomster and an awful blot on the
landscape of Smellbourne’s financial civilisation,
and that he (Flanagan) can speak authoritatively
on the subject, because, for twelve years, he drew
a large salary for managing Rowan’s business.
The second round showed Rowan on the offensive.
He slipped in a warrant for the arrest of his
former chum and present antagonist on a charge
of embezzling .£37 10s. seven years ago. Flanagan
was staggered for the moment, but he broke loose
on light bail, and sent Rowan back to his corner
with a writ for £20,000 damages for malicious
prosecution. The fight seems likely to terminate
in a mutual chucking-up of the sponge.
That little Sydney sectarian paper, the
Consecrated Press, is unconsciously adding
much to the gaiety of life. It is run under the
charge of the most holy directorate in Australia,
not even excepting that of the Presbyterian ;
and at the annual meeting of shareholders re-
cently the efforts of the priesthood in pushing the
paper were warmly acknowledged, and special
thanks given to Fathers Bunbury and Timoney
for their gallantry in running the show and
writing the articles. The Bulletin wonders
vaguely whether it was Father Timoney or Father
Bunbury who excommunicated this paper the other
day for permitting a contributor to discuss the
properties of the natural woman. On the whole,
it thinks it must have been Father Bun., because
anybody called Tim would be likely to know
more about the subject. The average priest, it he
is a good priest, knows little about women. No-
body can know a woman unless he takes her home
and studies her, and you can’t know Woman till
you study about nineteen dozen of her. If the
Press directorate could secure the co-operation of
a real connoisseur, like Pope Alexander VI., it
would be another matter. At present it is very
much in earnest, and very ignorant, and shriek-
ingly funny.

POISON
nor any deleterious substance enters into the composition of Clements Tonic. We
make no use in our process of arsenic, strychnia, kola nut, or any such
dangerous or useless drugs. Why should we? We don’t want to kill our patients—dead men
pay no bills. We want to cure them, then they come again, and send their friends
and recommend our remedy whenever occasion arises. That is where we benefit
by supplying a genuine article.
Arsenic is one of the most dangerous poisons that could be used; its quick action on
the human system shows that; but its regular use is bound to result in absorption of the
glands, and wasting away and final destruction of the nerve tissue.
strychnia has a dangerous, stimulating effect on the cerebral tissue, is cumula-
tive in action, and constant use is bound to result in paralysis of the base of the brain,
insanity and madness.
Kola nut has no merit whatever to recommend it. Its sole claim to
recognition as a medicine is due to the small percentage of caffeine it contains,
and it is a very small percentage—only 2 to 2'5 percent. —whereas ordinary tea which
you buy at the grocer’s contains double the quantity, and the best brands of tea contain
up to 6 per cent., so there is more of the active alkaloid of kola in a cup of tea than
in two cups of kola decoction and no dangerous or poisonous ingredients added.
Clements Tonic is no such catchpenny dodge ; it is a process, not a mixture ; has
met with universal praise, and we will send hundreds of local proofs of its merit to any-
one who wishes them.
PUBLIC APOLOGY.
(Hfittfc, JOHN HENRY HAVILAND REYNOLDS, and CHARLES
WILSON, of number 49£ Castlereagh street, Sydney (and late of -16 Glenmore road, Pad-
dington), in the Colony of New South VVales (trading as the British Drug Company)
having in contravention of the rights of F. M. Clements, Pharmaceutical Chemist, of
Newtown, in the said Colony, used the word Cr.EMKNS in connection with preparations
manufacture 1 by us and called
Clemens’ Kola Nut Tonic,
which we labelled as ha ing been manufactured by
Clemens and Co., Sydney,
the same not being the product or manufacture of F. M. Clements, hereby publicly
apologise for having so acted. In consideration of the said F. M. Clements consenting to
forego legal proceedings against us, we hereby undertake that we will not in the future
use the word Clements, Clemens, or any other form of words or letters that might be
construed, or could lead a purchaser to believe that he was getting go' ds of the manufacture
of F. M. Clements We further undertake to deliver to Mr. F. M. Clements all goods
bearing the words complained of, together with all labels, tickets, and other printed
matter in connection therewith. And we also agree to pay Mr. M. J. Brown, Solicitor, his
costs of, and incidental to, the preparation of this undertaking, and the Bond signed
herewith.
The said F. M. Clements is at liberty to
advertise this undertaking as he shall see
fit.
Dated at Sydney this
22nd day of November, 1898.
Witness—
M. J. BROWN,
Solicitor,
44 Elizabeth-street, Sydney.

CLEMENTS TONIC lias a record of cures none can rival. Hundreds of base
substitutes come on the market, the 1 plausible advertising resorted to attracts the attention
of the thoughtless and unwary, and they have a sale for a year or two till they are found
out. For a medicine to remain popular for the number of years Clements Tonic has it
must have exceptional merit. The Australian public is too well educated and too discriminat-
ing to be gulled for 1 mg even by clever, if false advertisements, and that is the reason all
the quack nostrums run to the length ot their tether in less than four years in this country
Clements Tonic has been the leader for ten.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heelCC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and'width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally, take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little mat ters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

“ Burglaree” : “ Not a word too much has been
said about the value of the Sydney Safe Deposit. Take
my own ease : I have rather a nice lot of plate, and my
wife has some valuable jewellery. When we were going
to the Mountains at Xmas we placed this in the Deposit,
and it was just as well. On our return, we found the
place had been burgled, and but for the Safe Deposit our
visitors would have made a good haul. As it was, they
got some second-hand clothes.” The Deposit ; s in Ash-
street, back of Paling’s.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Bobadil, after all, has proved himself a stout
horse (on paper) by winning the V.R.C. Leger in fast
time, and subsequently getting home an easy first in the
Australian Cup and Champion Stakes. But there
remains the fact that he had nothing to beat, and was
much distressed after each of the long-distance races.
Wait-a-Bit, his most formidable opponent in the Cup,
stumbled his chance away just as he seemed about to
fight out the finish with the jaunty 3-year-old, but he
wasn’t, on that particular day, the same Wait-a-Bit that
stretched The Grafter’s neck last November. Leaving
the Ballarat horse and his unlucky stumble out of the
question, Bobadil met nothing that has earned a reputa-
tion for stamina. Lee Metford, the runner-up in the
Leger and Cup, was not even able to beat Avalon in the
Champion race, which lasted 25sec. longer than the
3-mile match between Amberite and Bundook, in ’9B.
The Australian Cup race also was exceptionally slow,
considering that they had hard ground to work upon.
Wallace, as a 3-year-old, carried several pounds more than
“Bobby,” and did the distance i.e., he was a nose
behind the winner—in ssec. faster time. As yet, there is
no evidence of the St. Albans hero being a great horse,
except, by comparison with commoners or unfit ones. In
the spring he was just, as great as now, until the V.R.O.
Derby and Melbourne Cup winners got alongside of him.
The Champion being run in 7secs. over the
regulation time brought only half of the regulation added
money to the three placed horses. A time limit for the
Champion was one of the V.R.C. committee’s happiest
thoughts. There is often a loss, and never a considerable
profit, over the Autumn Meeting, and the Club well
deserves to save £5OO when the three-mile “ test ” race
isn’t conducted on proper test principles. By the way,
this year s Champion Day attendance was smaller than
usual owing to an alarming weather-tip by the Govt,
meteorologist. Like most tips, it didn’t come off.
A “reversal of form ” by Crimson was too re-
markable to be overlooked by the V.R.C. stewards, but
the inevitable enquiry resulted in a verdict of Not Guilty
against everybody bar the ’orse. Crimson carried coin
for the Brunswick Stakes, first day of the Autumn Meet-
ing, and ran pretty fast at the outset, but was soon
pegged back last. Jockey Crockett seems to have been
convinced by the mare’s ignominious defeat, for he
wouldn’t reduce his flesh by 41b. to ride her in the
Autumn Handicap five days later. This time owner
Wootton put only a few sovs. on Crimson’s off-chance, and
little Cleal had the mount, deal, riding in histoddiestSloan
style, bustled the mare along from the jump, and won all
the way by about five lengths. Maybe Cleal took a stone
off Crimson’s back by sitting mostly on her neck and
crouching forward to avoid the wind (blowing hard at the
time) when he had it against him. Anyhow, the Tod
Sloan trick is impressive, for it scored again during the
same afternoon. Jockey Kuhn, who sits very forward (as
nearly all the pony-riders do), landed the River Handicap
on Heretic, another animal that has often performed
badly under the old system of government. The Sloan
style will be all the rage presently, and its best professors
may be worth following.
An outrageous-looking cjrte of foul-riding was
what appeared to be young Foulsham’s attempt to knock
out Merry Prince in the Unplaced Two-years-old Stakes,
at Flemington. Foulsham on the favorite (Faith), and
Cleal on Merry Prince were together in the middle of the
course a couple of furlongs from home. They seemed to
be running pretty straight until deal’s mount began
to have the best of the tussle, and then Foul-
sham apparently started boring with malice afore-
thought. Once or twice Cleal looked likely to get
quit of his pursuer, but each time Foulsham came
at him again, and bored and bored, until by the time
Merry Prince had won the race (with half a head the best
of it) he was right against the rails under the judge’s
box. Cleal s victory under the circumstances was a
triumph of jockevship. If the other fellow was not trying
to block Merry Prince all the way down the straight,
then he is a dangerously weak horseman—much too weak
to have a license..
The V.R.C. stewards called boy Foulsham
into their room, as a matter of course, for the
shocking performance couldn’t be blinked. And
as the result of their enquiry, the offender got
a month’s suspension for what they were pleased
to call “ interference.” This decision is a scandal
on the face of it. If Foulsham couldn’t control
his horse it was unfair to suspend him as a misdemean-
ant. They should have sympathetically patted his head
and sent him to father Foulsham with a note saying,
“ Kindly keep your precious son from riding in public for
the next two years, and oblige. Meanwhile, feed him
well and get his strength up.” On the other hand, if the
stewards felt sure that the “interference” was as inten-
tional as it looked they should have made an effort to
strike awe into reckless young blackguardism by wiping
this boy off the track. Such boring as he, or his horse,
perpetrated on Saturday might bring half the field to
smash in a Melbourne Cup race.
• * * •
Lee-Metford (Carbine-Felicitas) proved to Boba-
dil what Progress proved to Grand Flaneur. With
Bobadil away, Lee-Metford would have been the hero of
V.R.C. meeting, as lie ran second in St. Leger, second in
Australian Cup, second in Loch Plate, and third in
Champion Stakes—a truly heart-breaking reqord for his
owner.
• « • *
Bobadil will probably come across the border to
give Sydneyites a chance of seeing him in front of his
fields at Randwick. In the All-Aged Stakes at the con-
cluding day of the V.R.C. autumn meeting Bob cantered
home, finishing the mile in 1.40 —equalling the Australian
record. Other Australian horses who have galloped a
mile in 1.40 are Bungebah, Bodka, Kingfish, Paris and
Delaware.
* * * •
The learned committee of the Newmarket (Eng.)
Subscription Rooms has given an opinion in the case of
the Victorian punter who wired to his Ikey Mo—“ Put
£lO on Scorn in the Maribyrnong Plate, and, if it wins,
put the lot on Bobadil for the Derby.” Scorn, a 3 every-
body knows, won the Plate, but not until after Bobbie
had been beaten in the Derby. The question to decide
was whether the commissioner was practically instructed
to put the “ tenner ” on the first of the two horses that
ran, and then put the winnings and the stake (in the
event of a win) upon the other of the two nags, or
whether he was supposed to literally carry out the order
— i.e., to put £ (amount to be filled in afterwards if
Scorn won the next race) on Bobadil at starting price,
and whether Bobadil’s defeat, therefore, swept away the
stake and the after winnings (whatever they might be)
on Scorn. The Newmarket tribunal rules in favor
of the first idea—the backer’s £lO is considered
to have been duly planked and lost on Bobadil.
There is more to be said for the decision than against it.
Ikey Mo is entitled to protection in all cases where the
punter—inadvertently or otherwise—creates an opening
for a quibble. Little traps have, ere now, been carefully
laid for Ikey by telegrams, and, in the rush of business on
a big race-day, he can’t be expected to do more than
acknowledge the receipt of the order. The Scorn-Bobadil
case was that of a backer who professed to be risking £lO
on a “ double ” —the odds against the “ double ” to be de-
cided by starting prices. One horse in the double got
beaten, and yet the punter wasn’t disposed to think he
had lost his bet.
Forest s Newmarket victory was not such a
scoop for the leather-1 united as at first appeared. Vic-
toria’s leviathan Jack Cohen laid the owner 2000 to 60.
Later, the horse developed rheumatiz or something.
Owner wanted to cry the bet off, intending to scratch,
but bookie refused. “Oxy” laid a 1000 to 10 and a
1000 to 20. Writer knows of two other odds-merchants
whose hooks showed 1000 to 40, each against the “ rank
outsider.” Several big doubles were struck too, and
rumor hath it that a Sydney railway officer won £l5O0 —
1000 to 10 straight out, and £5OO the double (Forest and
Bobadil).
* * * *
Warwick Farm (Sydney) is just a little too far
out of town, and attendance and fields suffer accordingly.
In the principal handicap on Saturday, the Warwick,
there were but five starters. Fairy Prince and Colleen
started equal favorites and the former won after a good
set-to with Bell wood.
* * * *
Australia has emphatically declared itself “off ”
“ Tattersall’s ” ordinary sweeps, and the disgust of black-
mailing horse-owners is almost too deep for words.
“.Tatt.” ran two 50,000 sub. sweeps on Newmarket Han-
dicap-one of them a special (to be drawn on morning of
race) which filled so early that the overflow enabled the
ordinary “consultation ” to close with 45,000 subs. In
the case of the Australian Cup there was no “special”
to tempt the public, so “ Tatt.” had to close his ordinary
sweep with a total of only 19,000 subs. !! In future, all
sweeps will be specials, The Bulletin supposes. They
might as well, for all the satisfaction that “ Tatt’s ” clients
at a distance derive from a gamble on the old lines. The
writer of this. par. invested in all three of the aforesaid
sweeps from Melbourne. The result of the No. 1 ordinary
reached him on the Monday after the Newmarket had
been run. Results of the Newmarket “special” and
Australian Cup ordinary came to hand by the same post
one day after the Cup race. None of the tickets
drew a prize. Had there been any sort of chance
hanging to them the information might perhaps have
come along promptly. When were “Tatt.’s” ordinary
sweeps drawn, and why doesn’t he commit himself to
dates on his circular?
“Tatt.’s” No. 1 special on the Newmarket
yielded four prizes among 30 consecutive tickets—No.
9425 captured £lO, 9430 £5O, 9437 £5, 9454 second prize
(North British). Interesting to know whether those 30
tickets were sold in one lot.
• * • *
The S.A. saints, taking up the whispers of Ikey
Mo, are pointing, horrified, to some lately-printed figures,
and are asking the electors to observe that last year
£268,156 was put into the sinful totalisator ; and they
have gone into calculations that that means about
2,000,000 bushels of wheat. Then, of course, they want
to know what a province can expect to come to
that throws two millions of bushels of wheat into the
infernal machine. Probably it hasn’t occurred to them that
the same money is put into the machine over and over
again, so that probably no more than £25,000 of capital
was required.. And, anyhow, the machine doesn’t con-
sume the coin—or bolt with it to another province. All
the money comes out again.
* * * *
The complaint of Maoriland cricketers that,
whereas they received open-handed hospitalitv in Tas-
mania and Victoria, they were treated but scurvily in
Sydney, and had to pay for admission to the test-match,
&c., should awake the public to the necessity of an
alteration of the administration of Sydney "Cricket
Ground. It is time members had some say in the direc-
tion of things. Prices are too high at 2s. 6d. and Is. for
such matches as the recent M.L. one ; if reduced to Is.
6d. and 6d., they would pay better. The catering
arrangements might be improved, the charges being so
high that the stands are dotted with people munching
provisions which they’ve brought along themselves!
Also, the toothachy music supplied is a public calamity.
Also, why should the tram authorities charge 3d. to the
Cricket Ground when it’s only 2d. to the Zoo, about
100yds. off?
Another aspect of the case : In view of future
visits to Sydney (writes a Bulletin correspondent) the
M.L. cricketers rather made a mistake in complaining of
receiving “scant courtesy” from N.S.W. Cricket Asso-
ciation. As a matter of fact, they were met at the rail-
way-station and driven in a drag to their quarters;
driven each day to and from practice, sumptuously
catered for at the ground ; tendered a banquet; and in
return they showed some of the poorest cricket ever seen
m a representative match on Sydney Cricket Ground. It
would be interesting to know what more they expected.
By the way, it may interest the Australian
Jockey Club authorities to learn that leading members of
the Tasmanian Jockey Club, while loud in their com-
plaints as to the treatment they receive from the A.J.C.
when they visit Sydney, are enthusiastic in their praises
of the hospitality extended by the V.R.O. in Melbourne.
*-* * *
Harry Trott, whom the swaggah M'Laren con-
descended to acknowledge as the best cricket-captain in
the world, unhappily shows practically no sign of im-
provement. He is living at S. Melb. with a trained nurse
in attendance. He is as rotund as ever, but cricket, or
anything else for that matter, possesses very little in-
terest for poor Trotty. A few days ago George
Giffen was taken to see him ; but his old captain
could not recognise Giffen, nor could the chaos of
his brain be stirred to recollection. Giffen has been
credited with being of stern, unemotional composi-
tion, but the exterior of the man is only the husk that
covers the kernel of a fine heart. The sight of poor
Trott’s misery was too much for him ; he broke down and
sobbed like a child.
» * • *
“ Dinnie ” Reedman, who made such a great
stand against Howell and co.’s bowling in Sydney, is, like
George Giffen, an official in Adelaide G.P.O. Reedman
captains N. Adelaide, which has not been defeated in this
year’s Adelaide cricket, and is one of the best footballers
in S.A.
* * * *
A big S. Melb. crowd would have howled had not
Laver been included in the XI. They grant that his style
is not artistic ; that it lacks even the elegance of an agri-
cultural laborer’s “ cuts to leg” with a scythe, but argue
that runs are the things which count now in Hingland,
and that Laver is the man to get them.
• * • •
Laver and Giffen were many people’s favorites
for the last places in the XI. Wonderful how Giffen has
regained his place in popular favor, and had Graham
caught Kelly the first day in the Sydney match the
veteran might have been a certainty. As for Laver-
sturdy bat, good bowler, and of grand physique, his prior
claim over Trumper and co. cannot be gainsaid.
• • • •
There is nothing to cavil at over the final selec-
tion for the team for England of Johns, Laver, and—if a
fourteenth man is taken—Trumper. Johns was, of
course, looked upon as a certainty all through the piece,
Laver has been playing consistent cricket during the
season, and the crowd has all through shown its sym-
pathy with Trumper; and perhaps the best judges of
cricket in the country are the gentlemen who stuff their
lunches in their pockets and broil for half a week round
the ringside.
* * • •
■ Trumper’s four innings against the Australian
Eleven of 6, 46, 46, and 26 were poor performances only
in comparison with his leviath an scores in club cricket.
Had not so much been expected from the dashing young
player, it would have been said he had done excellently
well.
* * * *
Donnan’s first-innings effort in the recent test-
match at Sydney was about the dreariest display of the
tied-elbow game seen since the days of Alick Bannerman.
Giller’s show seemed dreary enough at the time, but
oompared with “Dead-eye” Donnan’s batting it was
wild, reckless slogging. The crowd cheered when
Giller got out, b t it saw Don nan depart in silence—
too tired to yell. Donnan’s dismissal was brought about
curiously. He had played about half-a-dozen maidens,
and started off the seventh with the same careful poking-
about, when a warning voice rose from the “ bob stand,”
“ Play steady, Harry ! ” It brought down the house, and
apparently even had effect on the batsman, for in his own
little way he got reckless and was bowled by Jones, to
the joy of all."
* • * *
The second win in succession of the Australian
XI. showed their decided superiority over the “ next
best,” a very satisfactory state of affairs, as to have gone
to England defeated would not have proved the best
drawing card at ’Ome. When the XI. started their
second innings they needed 60 runs to win, which they
scored with the loss of Gregory’s wicket for 20. Howell
and M'Leod then ran up the required balance, each man,
strange to say, contributing the same score.
It was looking as if ]Noble had lost some of his
bowling power, judging by the recent electoral matches,
and the first test and the first innings of the second test,
but he showed all his old “devil” in the second innings
on Monday last.
• * • *
Laver, the elongated Victorian fielding at point
in the recent test-match, did work right up to the best
form of Harry Trott and Ranji. in that position. The
catch by which he dismissed “Noss” Iredale was one of
the best ever seen on the ground. “ Noss,” who is good
at the cut. got fair on to it, and when Laver threw out,
one large hand and gathered it in the crowd was electri-
fied. Laver, who has the careless appearance of a “ good
sort,” is a big favorite with the Sydney crowd.
* * * *
Old-man Darling always frowned on son Joe’s
cricket business and cricket exploits. It is alleged by the
family that Joe, will make no more trips to England—in
fact, he is being urged to give up the game altogether.
* * * *
A Sydney daily, commenting on the smash in
Sydney Bicycle Cup final, says: “An inspection in the
dressing-room showed that the injuries were compara-
tively trifling, and, as more than one remarked, furnished
a strong tesnmony to the often-stated fact that trained
athletes can withstand shocks which to the ordinary man
would often prove fatal.” As a matter of fact, the men
were packed together on the lower portion of the
track and were thrown on to the grass. Had they been
on the upper part of the asphalte, and been chucked on
the fence in place of the turf, there would probably have
been more work for the coroner.
* * * *
Champion swimmer Fred. Lane and “Mud”
Bishop had their fourth tussle over 100yds. at Bondi last
Saturday. Lane got away first, and had the top of his
head in front throughout, eventually winning by about a
foot. Bishop bungled the last turn, which may or may
not have made the difference, i.ut Lane finished strong
enough to justify the impression that had it been wanted
another ounce or two of power might have been brought
to bear. The time (lmin. 3 2-ssec.) eclipses all previous
Australasian records, and is 2-ssec. faster than Bishop’s
performance of last month, which then stood as best.
* * * *
Novices knocked chips off each other in the first
round of Sydney Golden Gate Club’s 9.4 tournament on
Saturday night. Most bouts never saw the third round
through—one finished inside 2min., but things hummed
to a lively tune the whole time. They was no let-up
from the word “Go!” and every individual of these raw
’uns at the game stood to his guns as if life depended
upon the result, until an extra hot punch sent him
“ out” beyond the allotted lOsec.
* * * #
Monday night’s bicycle races on Sydney Cricket
Ground passed off without serious accident. Walne and
Jackson ran their heats off in the big match, paced by a
tandem, Walne beating Jackson in two straight heats,
showing both better pace and generalship. Forbes
and M'Donald, who had tied with 8 points, then
ran off the final of the Orient Plate over a
mile, M'Donald winning after a good set-to. W.
Phelan won the half-mile handicap in 57 4-ssec., 2 or
3sec. faster than this kind of race is usually won in. The
five miles gave some excitement. When the bell rang
M'Donald dashed out and got a good lead, then Walne
made a phenomenal run round his field at the back, but
(perhaps tired by his previous races) was unable to get
nearer than fourth, and M'Donald won.
* * * *
At the Sydney Electric-Light bike-sports t’other
night a “ book” with a fistful of silver was just going to
pay a winner when a plain-cloches policeman walked aim-
lessly up and stood by. “ Book ” spotted him, and care-
lessly said to winner, “How much was it you lent me,
Joe ? ” “ Thirty bob.” “ Right—l'll pay you as it’s the
end of the month.” He coolly paid up, grinned at the
“ cop.,” and walked away.
*** * *
Trick-cyclist Minting, at London Aquarium,
rides up and down two spiral tracks, a foot wide, con-
nected at 105 ft. from the floor, and not on the ordinary
bike, but on a single wheel 36in. in diameter. One day
he fell at 16ft. —unconscious for a week. Soon afterwards
he fell from 46ft. —no injury. Next time
* * * •
The gamest, sturdiest and best pug. Australia
has sent abroad for many a day—Melbourne Billy Ed-
wards —was beaten Jan. 24, at New York, by that cyclonic
and apparently invincible American boxer, Billy Smith.
The pair started in at a furious clip, and kept it up with
such earnestness that not a few thought Smith would
meet his Waterloo. The Yank opened up roughly and
introduced some questionable work, but Edwards showed
himself equally conversant with queer manoeuvres, and it
was give and take on this score all through. Frequently
the Australian failed to live up to Queensberry rules, and
he was often cautioned by the referee. On the other
hand, Smith never lost" an opportunity to get in a
“crooked” punch. Edwards displayed wonderful
strength, and toughness, and a phenomenal amount of
recuperative force. Up to the 14th round the men took
few breathing spells, and when the Yank rushed in with
a heavy left on the wind and a right on the jaw he ended
one of the hardest and fiercest contests ever seen in New
York or anywhere else.
* * * •
This isn’t bad. At a recent Sydney, boxing
tournament two pugs went the full journey—six rounds
—without a knock-out. One judge was for a draw, the
other named a winner. The crowd clamored for the
referee’s decision. That official explained that he had no
option : the fact of one judge being unable to separate
the fighters compelled him to declare the man singled out
by the other official the winner. And this gentleman is
the permanent referee of the club concerned !
* * * *
From a reliable source comes word that
“Gentleman” George Dawson, whilom light-weight
champion boxer of Australia, has a banking-account
totalling a score of thousands in dollars. George has
been the Chicago A.C.’s boxing instructor for years at
£4OO per annum, beside substantial “ perks.” Writer
remembers Dawson leaving for ’Frisco some few months
after his sensational defeat by Melbourne Tom Williams,
and borrowing a tenner here and a fiver there to help him
along. Writer also recollects that after his first win in
Yankeeland, G.D. straightway returned all those fivers
and tenners, thereby making a dozen or more friends for
life.
* * * *
Young Pluto, the Australian boxer who has
been away from the game so many years, flew at high
game in the States when he took on George Dixon.
Dixon won easily in 10 rounds. Pluto was a top-notcher
in his day—how good may be gauged from his three
fights with “ Griffo,” two with the gloves and one in the
field. On each occasion Jack proved as good as his
master.
* * * *
Australian “Cocker” Tweedie was not long
getting on a match in the States. At ’Frisco, on January
24, he got the decision at the end of 15 rounds with one
Tye, but he seems not to have got over his failing of
playing to the gallery, which will surely bring him to
grief when pitted against something serious.
The Bu lletin recently stated thac of the
3760 destitute N.S.W. children cared for by the
State, 2231 were supported by payments to their
own mothers. This should be 3760 besides 2231
cared for by their mothers. The number of
children cast wholly or in part upon N.S.W.
Treasury was for Feb. about 6500. The chief
clause oitthe amended Act passed in 1896 was the
provision that “deserving widows and deserted
wives” could be paid direct by Govt., and in
little over two years the sum of .£l2OO per month
has been reached under this one head. The in-
crease under the principal Act has not stopped, as
anticipated, the annual outgoings now being
.£46,000 for maintenance alone, without cost of
inspection and management.

The Land of Jolly-and-Fat. Man and
wife, workless and homeless in Sydney, struck
West, and, according to their account, tramped
the whole breadth of N.S.W. looking for a job.
They got as far as Tareena, which is within
coo-ee of the Western border, when water failed
them, and they died within a few yards of one
another.
Suggestive extracts from report of the
recent N.Q. hurricane : —
Cooktown, Monday.—The steamer Warrego arrived at
12.30 this morning, 'with 400 colored men and 11 whites.
. . . About 350 colored men and HO whites are missing.,
The total is not quite certain till the various ships
articles are overhauled. . , . Four men were drowned
from the Channel Rock Lightship. A colored man
reached the shore with two women, after swimming four
days. . . . The following vessels are reported lostThe
schooners North Wales, Sagitta, Silvery Wave, Admiral,
Waiwera, Meg Merrilies ; also, 60 luggers, 20 diving boats,
and the Channel Rock lightship. Fully 60 bodies have
been buried thus far, but only one white man.
This is supposed to be an item of Australian news,
not Asiatic. Talk about “ local color ” !
Two very weak Anti-Federal inventions
perished last Monday, though they will turn up
again next week just the same. The Sydney
Telegraph had commenced to quote Premier
Turner, of Victoria, as having declared “ with no
uncertain sound ” that the southern provinces did
not mean to admit Queensland’s meat and sugar
free of duty despite Federation. Turner replies
shortly that, as the provinces will have to admit
each other’s produce free whether they want to or
not, he wasn’t such a fool as to say anything of
the kind. Also, M‘Laurin, M.L.C., started to
refer vaguely to Turner as the authority for a
theory that the Federal Government would charge
£5 per ton excise on sugar, and keep the customs
duty at £5 per ton also, so that sugar would cease
to be protected, and the German bounty-aided
article would extinguish the local industry,
and all manner of other horrors. Turner
reports that he never spoke or even dreamed
of such a thing (the idea was so utterly
ridiculous that no person of ‘ any intelligence
suspected him of it in any case). And that he
always took it for granted that, if any excise on
local sugar was imposed, the customs duty on the
imported article would be much higher. These
episodes, by the way, mark a new departure in
Anti-Billious misrepresentation. Formerly, the
Anti-Billious told their own inaccuracy on their
own responsibility, but now they say that they
are truly shocked to hear, from an eminent
Federalist, that the following dreadful conse-
quence is inevitable unless the national movement
is at once suppressed. And people go away
deeply impressed, saying that when the eminent
Federalist admitted such a dreadful thing the
situation must indeed be terrible.
“ Stickit Minister Apropos of the blow-
ing up of the Mahdi’s tomb and the throwing of
his body into the Nile, both by the military

habit Bull has got.

Julius Vogel, Maoriland's ex-Premier, ex-
Agent-genoral, ex-railway builder and boomer and
many other things, has died suddenly in London,
aged 64. He left two good works behind him in
the State Life Insurance Department and the
Public Trust Office, so Vogel, the Hebraic boomer,
at least laid the foundations of the State Socialism
which his successors developed. And though
Vogel was a boomer he had in his early years
more brains than many of his tribe. When he
proposed his great railway policy and his
loan he also proposed setting aside
6,000,000 acres of the public estate, so that its
selling value, when enhanced by the opening up of
the country, would go to repay the liabilities.
But, unfortunately, Maoriland went in for the
loan and the boom, but never made the proposed
land reserves. Vogel himself didn’t struggle very
hard in defence of the good features of the scheme.
His resourcefulness as a financier died out at
an early age. When he reappeared in Maoriland
at the age of 50 he had degenerated into a pain-
fully commonplace politician, and after pottering
for a few years among the ruins he faded away
again and was forgotten. If his original public
works scheme had been carried out in its entirety
it would have been a lot better for Maoriland and
for Vogel’s reputation. But the big burly man
hadn’t grit enough to carry it through as he first
planned it. He was full of nerve up to a certain
point, and then he would collapse, and that weak
spot in Vogel made all the trouble.
N.S.W. military forces will this Easter be
mobilised on active-service conditions, and with a
little luck the general public will get a glimpse of
the colossal mix-up that will eventuate when war
does come along. The mobilisation scheme is all
right-on paper, but it would take an army
knocked into shape by several recent wars to
carry it out, and the insufficient transport and
commissariat of N.S.W. army would alone be
enough to knock-out the best scheme ever built.
The chances are, however, that said scheme will
only be carried out in part; anyhow, it will
be more serviceable than the usual large Easter
picnic at Kook wood, as it will give the men some
idea of the hardship of active service, and show
the public where things are weak.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir, —For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salis,” and was in-
clined not to use it; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and'am now entirely cured;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves.
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O'REGAN.

Curious extracts from the annual report of
the N.S.W. police-department boss :
During the year the extension of the franchise to mem-
bers of the police force has come into operation, but I
cannot say that it has had a beneficial effect upon the
service as a disciplined force. . . . The conduct of the
police generally has been exceptionally good.
Evidently the police have been struggling man-
fully against the undermining influence of the
franchise, and have managed to resist its evil
influence up to date.
Like many Eastern Extension Company’s
employes, Mr. Selfe, the new superintendent of La
Perouse (Sydney) cable-station, is a man of travels
and experience. He was on the staff in Egypt
when Arabi’s rebellion was raging, and in those
days there was always a chance of coming to an
untimely end—one operator in Alexandria, for
instance, met his doom during the bombardment.
While the Zulu and Transvaal troubles were on,
Mr. Selfe was engaged on the Portuguese island
of St. Vincent, sufficiently far away to be comfort-
able, but the nearest point from which South
African cables could be sent. He has pleasant
recollections of Java, and joins with other Eastern
Extension men in proclaiming that to be one of
the best spots on earth.
This, from the Medical Press, should
interest the Sydney doctor who says that eight out
of ten Australians (or Sydneyites?) show diabetic
symptoms, caused probably by eating too much
vegetable food : —
The hopelessness of treatment, in cases of diabetes has
been only too frequently illustrated in the hapless victims
of the disease. Under the circumstances, therefore, it is
legitimate to give a trial to any new method which seems
to hold out some prospect of cure, and practitioners will
doubtless be interested to learn that Professor Pietro
Lupo, of Naples, has recorded two cases in which all the
diabetic symptoms have been relieved by making the
patients adopt an exclusively vegetable diet. In one case
he affirms that the results of this treatment was truly
marvellous. Both sugar and albumen disappeared from
(he urine, gangrene was arrested, and the “cure”
completed in eighteen days. The news sounds too good
to be true, and time only can show whether other prac-
titioners will obtain the same results. In the vegetable
dietary nothing was excluded, all edible soups were
allowed, together with peas, beans, and saccahrine fruits,
including grapes. Professor Lupo does not claim that all
diabetics will be benefited by the treatment, but he
believes that it will prove of great service to many. If
his assertions come to be established, the dietary of
diabetics will have to be completely changed, greatly to
the advantage perhaps of the unfortunate sufferers from
this distressing malady.
One of Australia’s latest murders comes
from Mt. Mary, a lonely S.A. township situated
in scrub country, where an Indian hawker, Mar-
rett Singh, was found with his skull smashed.
The motive was apparently to secure the Indian’s
pack of little trinkets, worth perhaps a few shil-
lings. The case resembles the last S.A. tragedy
at Streaky Bay, when a kangaroo-hunter named
Beard murdered his mate for the sake of a few
kangaroo skins, but lost his own instead.

The Perth (W.A.) city by-law which
frowns upon Flossie and the landlord who lets
premises for her use, has been set going. Of
course, the “ agent ” didn’t know what Flossie sat
half-naked in front of the window for; but he
knew that she paid twice as much rent as the
other people did whom he turned out to let her
come in. <£lo and costs.
The Anti - Billious petition, which was
signed by most of the small boys of Sydney, puts
the Anti-Billites in an awkward position. The
petitioners demand that the measure submitted
to the people shall not be the present Bill but the
one which Premier Reid demanded before the late
Conference. If the crowd which makes this de-
mand is in the majority, then it is no use paying
any attention to it; it will squash the new Bill
openly at the referendum, and it is only doing it
an injustice to squash the measure secretly and
not give the Anti-Billites a chance to show their
strength. If they are in the minority it is no use
paying any attention to them either, for the claim
of the minority to run the show is pure gall and
insolence.
Dr. MacLaurin, M.L.C. (N.S.W.) :
After the Convention Bill was destroyed at the polls
there was not one single person in the land who would
say a word for it! . . . There was no single man who
had made the study of finance his professional business
who did not concur in the opinion that the system pro-
posed must lead to excessive and onerous taxation.
The present writer, who is Scotch himself, dis-
respectfully casts both these assertions back at his
disgraceful compatriot’s head as utterly untrue.
He has" had the pleasure of saying many good
words for the old Bill since the referendum and
right up to this day. And, as a person who has
made the study of finance his business, and who
knows a great deal more about it than any man
whose attention is constantly distracted by the
necessity of taking off somebody’s leg can do, he
has not only said but proved over and over again
that the alleged necessity for excessive taxation
was all rot. And it is still rot.

“ Malle ” : Stopped at a Sydney-street
table where an Anti-Federal petition is exposed
for signatures, and took observations. The
blatant person in charge was drawing a harrowing
picture of what would happen with the Federal
capital in Victoria (where it isn’t to be). “ Come
an’ sign to ’ave the Fed’ral capital in N.S. Wales
instead of Victoria,” he bellowed. A small boy
in pantlets signed with a rush, and was followed
by a youth a little older, and a beery person with
no ceiling to his trousers. The writer once knew
a pastoralist who was trying to block a much-
needed and asked-for road through his run. He
prepared a long petition pointing out the utter
uselessness and undesirability of this road, and
personally waited on a lot of bullockies, who were
camped awaiting the commencement of the wool
season. Explained to them that it had been pro-
posed to close a road they used—which, if done,
would compel them to negotiate a bad swamp, &c.
—and this document was against it. He gathered
in every one of ’em, and followed this up by
roping-in the entire council of the local township,
who, only a month before, had signed the original
petition for the road. The N.S.W. pro vinci alists
must be in a bad way to adopt these tactics.
There is a cubic mile of irony in that re-
cent report that the Nowra (N.S.W.) dairymen
had become so desperate through long-continued
drought that they had at last resolved to pray for
rain. The habit of praying as a last resource
when everything else has failed is a quaint satire
on the alleged Christian faith. That prayer for
rain should run something like this : “O Lord,
we come to Thee as a last possibility. We’ve tried
everything else, and nothing came of it. Now it
seems we might as well be praying as sitting be-
hind the cow-shed. We don’t s’pose there’s any-
thing in it or we’d have tried it before, but we try
it now on the off-chance in case it may be a little
better than nothing. If it isn’t, we haven’t lost
anything, so we come along in a casual sort of
way to see how it’ll pan out.” That may not be the
formula of the Australian prayer for rain, but the
meaning of it is all there.

An Out-Back Lunch.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I recently had lunch at a bush pub. way-back
from Emu Mountain. It was a little place built
mostly of bark and mud, and designated on a sign
half as big as itself—“ The Bull’s Repose, by
Phelim O’Heffernan.” When I drove close up to
the door and called out there was an immediate
rush of poultry from the bar followed by the in-
evitable bush-shanty pig. The latter animal
reconnoitred cautiously round the doorway before
he sprinted out and made for the back. A long
do? of doubtful antecedents raised his head and
contemplated me silently for some minutes, and
then went off to sleep again.
In answer to repeated calls, a girl appeared—a
red-headed, red-faced lump of the rising genera-
tion, her arms covered with dough, and attired in
a man's waistcoat, a short skirt, and a pair of
men’s bluchers—ancient and huge.
On my inquiring for lunch she wiped the dough
off her arms on my nag’s mane and proceeded to
unharness him, at the same time stating that she
“ wuz jus’ gettin’ the tucker ” and would send
Billy to play me quoits until it was ready.
Billy, who was followed by a miscellaneous col-
lection of more long dogs—the out-back dog is an
alarmingly long animal—was a small youth in a
big hat adorned with the scalps of slain marsupials.
He was communicative, informing me, amongst
other things, as he squinted through the quoits
and fired them on with marvellous precision, that
I would get “ a blanky good dinner, as it was
Mag’s cookin’ day and she wuz a fine poisoner,”
especially since he had been out and got her some
emu-eggs for the pudding. Also, he stated, “ the
ole man’’was on the spree and was “screamin’
blanky funny.”
When Mag banged on a tin I followed William
into the smoky kitchen and was provided with a
seat on an upturned kerosene case at a clothless
table laid round with battered tin plates and
ancient, rusted knives ; also forks in various stages
of disrepair.
The other members of the family, of various
ages but all unanimously dirty, ranged up on the
opposite side.
“ ’Ere comes th’ ole man,” Billy whispered as
the door of an inner room opened, and a female,
whom I concluded was the “ ole woman,” shot out
as though propelled by some hidden force, and
sat down with a very red face at the table.
The “ole man” followed on two thin bandy
legs. He carried a bottle of whisky in one hand,
and there was silence as he took his seat.
Giving the carving-knife a preliminary rub on
the black bricks of the fireplace, he hacked several
slices off an emaciated leg of mutton. Then he
helped himself to some pumpkin by hand, and
proceeded to eat, and no one being bellicose
enough to raise any objection we sat and watched
him.
He ate savagely, throwing sundry pieces over
his shoulder to the dogs, and cleared his plate
with remarkable rapidity; then, evidently be-
coming cognizant of my presence, he put down his
knife and fork and stared fiercely at me for several
seconds. . , „„ , „ ,
“ An’ who the divil moight you be ? he yelled
suddenly.
I felt embarrassed, and replied : “My name is
Jones, and I’m travelling for— ”
“ Thin whoy the divil don’t yer ’ave yer feed
outside loike any other traveller?” he inter-
rupted. “ Will yer have some sheep ? ”
I said I would have sheep, whereupon he hacked
off several huge hunks, helped me to a handful of
pumpkin, and, taking a long nip from the bottle,
called for the pudding.
Mag rose, and proudly placed on the table a
pudding of weighty and rank appearance, and a
tough-looking tart. Mr. O’Heffernan appeared
pleased with the look of the pudding, but, quietly
ignoring it, he asked his wife, who was helping
the youngsters to mutton, if she would have some
tart.
“ No, thanks, dear,” she replied, looking at the
pudding.
He asked all round the table, but no one wanted
any—all eyes were on the “ duff.”
Mr. O’Heffernan appeared hurt, and took an-
other lengthy swig at the bottle. Then he picked
up the tart and poised it in his hand. “Won’t
yer ’ave a little? ” he asked, persuasively, looking
across at his wife, who edged cautiously away,
keeping an eye on the tart. “Not a little bit?
Well, take the whole blanky lot,” he screeched,
throwing it at her head.
Mrs. O’Heffernan dodged with a dexterity evi-
dently acquired by much practice, and jumped
outside just in time to miss the pudding, which
slapped against the door as it slammed behind
her.
It didn’t break, but bounced off, and, picking it
up, Phelim rushed after his wife.
Across the yard they went full speed—Mrs.
O’Heffernan, with her skirts gathered up, running
like an emu ; Phelim yelling furiously about a
furlong behind, the mob of bony canines yelping
in the rear, the pig sprinting in the middle dis-
tance, and the hens racing for shelter in an
adjacent gum-tree. When they disappeared in
the scrub, the “ole woman” was tiring percept-
ibly and her pursuer was gaining at every stride.
I bore the loss of the pudding with Christian
fortitude and returned to the kitchen.
Billy sorrowfully removed the tart from where
it was sticking on the wall.
“ I’ope’e falls an’ cracks ’is neck for gettin’
away with that duff,” he said, earnestly, as he
sparingly helped the others to some tart.
Mag hunted another pig, who was eating the
remainder of the dough, away from the baking-
dish, filled it with cold water, and started to wash
up, drying the things on her skirt.
I harnessed, and “got.”
FACE.
An Adelaide young gentleman, under the
new Affiliation Act, has been ordered to pay £8
medical expenses and 7s. weekly, with £5 14s.
costs, to a damsel who declares him responsible
for the fact that she will soon become a mother.
The cautious beak orders, however, that payment
shall cease if the little stranger does not make its
appearance before 20th June next.
Blake’s Wife.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“And you are thoroughly determined, Nina—
your mind is quite made up ? ”
“Quite ; it’s no use; my mother ”
“ Well, I’m off to-morrow. There is a boat on
Saturday, and I’m not going to wait till after-
wards. ”
The other man came round the cliff so gently
that they did not hear him until
“I’m sorry to disturb you, Nina, but your
mother is coming this way. How are you,
Butler? Just heard you are going away. A
pleasant trip and ”
“ A lengthy one ?”
The other took no notice.
“A prosperous one.” He turned to the girl
again: “ I think you had better come with me,
Nina.”
They were hardly out of Butler’s hearing when
she turned quickly to the man she was to marry.
“ You know we love each other. Can I be
happy with you ? I find you have influenced my
mother against him ! ”
He looked at her. Should he let them be happy ?
He looked again. After all, Butler would find
consolation. And Nina ? She would be his wife.
“There is time,” he said slowly, “for you to
call him back.”
She shook her head, and they walked on in
silence.
• * • •
Blake was home rather late one night, and at
dinner said casually:
“You remember Butler, Nina?—l think you
knew him. Came across him to-day ; he is over
for a trip. Looks prosperous, too. I asked him
to dinner, Thursday. You won’t forget?”
He smiled that night when he heard her hum a
song that she used to sing to Butler. He smiled
again on Thursday when he saw her dressed for
dinner—all in white. Once she always wore
white, but since her marriage had disliked it.
What a fool she was to imagine he did not re-
member !
9 m * m
A few weeks later.
“ I would like to speak to you ; come in here.”
“You look pale, Nina ; is it the heat?” h
said, solicitously.
“ It’s not the heat. You know, you must know.
What a fool you were to think I could forget
Jack. But it’s all over now.”
“ Then Butler has persuaded you at last,” he
said, quietly. “Poor fellow, he has tried hard
enough. You are rather a difficult person, Nina.
When are you going away with him ? ”
“ To-night.’’
“Ah ! ” He seemed to notice for the first time
that she had on her hat and gloves. “ And
Butler ? ”
“ Will meet me at the station.”
“ I’ll see you to Redfern. It’s very dark and
stormy. Butler is very thoughtless.”
She hysterically assented, seeing he intended to
go in any case.
On the ferry, they met a friend. “ What! off
to Melbourne, Mrs. Blake? Rather sudden, isn’t
it ? ”
“Oh, no,” said Blake, looking steadily at his
wife. “ Mrs. Blake has been thinking of it for
quite two months.”
(It was just two months since Butler had come
to Sydney).
The train had gone, and Blake was walking
slowly down George-street. The situation had
its embarrassments.
* * * *
Blake took up the telegram again.
“ Mrs. Blake left for Sydney to-day—alone.”
The door opened gently and she entered ; he
looked at her critically; she was apparently un-
changed.
“ I’ve come back, Erank; and if you don’t want
me to stay ” (Her voice was the same ; how
soft it was !)
“ What will you do?”
“ Oh, go away again. I daresay” She
stopped and looked at him. What an adorable
figure she had!
“ I think, Erank, I have suffered enough to
satisfy even your idea of punishment; but if I
am to go—good-night.”
Before she was out of the room he took her sud-
denly in his arms and kissed her passionately.
“ Why did you never kiss me like that before ?”
she asked, half-laughing, half-crying.
“ Because I never loved you like that before,”
Blake might have answered —only didn’t.
• • * .*
“ But you never once said that you loved me,”
she said reproachfully.
“ That’s in the Past.”
“And suppose you get tired of me ? ” (Blake
being merely a man, and remembering she was hi 3
wife thought it most possible, but) —
“That’s for the Future,” he said.
“ And the Present? ” she demanded.
For answer he kissed her again and again.
A. DE M.
Launceston, Tas., depends upon the South
Esk River to keep the electric-light going, and it
was thought that the supply of water was ample
and inexhaustible. But the river shows signs of
tailing, and, if the city is not to become, as a local
scribe puts it, “as dark as the inside of a cow,”
one of the big inland lakes must be tapped, and
the water diverted into the Esk. The surveyors
are already at work.
Ex-Chancellor Herschell, whose death is
cabled, was detested by English reporters for his
long-windedness. A press-agency man who got
instructions to wire a curt summary of Herschell,
then in minor office as Solicitor-General, did not
attend the meeting, but astonished the jurist
afterwards with his cool request for the gist of
a portentous five-column oration in 100 words.
Apropos of which, it is related that several London
pressmen played billiards instead of attending one
of Hartington’s earlier meetings, and agreed to
“fake” a report. “Well, I didn’t say that,”
remarked the lazy Hartington afterwards, “ but
I certainly ought to have said it.”

SEVERE SUNSTROKE,
A SICK CRICKETER CURED.
Mr. M. P. Ryan, of the Melbourne Chilled Butter Co.,
Flinders-]ane West, Melbourne, is a well-known Victorian cricketer,
and lib invariably handles the willow to no mean advantage.
Whilst playing at Wilcannia, N.S.
Wales, in the 80’s in a three-days’
match, with the thermometer at 116
degrees in the shade, he received an
attack of sunstroke, or heat apop-
lexy.
Mr. Ryan, when seen by a re-
porter, said : “ The attack was a
severe one ; so severe, I was totally
incapacitated from following my
clerical duties. All the usual reme-
dies were used, and, after a time, ap-
peared to have some effect. But from
that date annually I was subject to
the most agonising pains in the head.
Such an effect did they have that

WEAKNESS AND PARALYSIS CURED.
A MOTHER’S ANXIETY FOR HER CHILD.
[From the Mddgee Guardian.]
At the foot of Cherry Tree Hill, near Ilford, N.S.W., is the
residence of Mr. and Mrs. M. H. Cosier and family.
Mrs. Cosier is well-known and is highly popular, and has spent
her life-time in the Ilford locality. She is 36 years of age, and the
mother of seven children, the youngest, a fine pair of twin girls,
Annie and Bella, being now fifteen months old. Mrs. Cosier
recently said to a Guardian representative:—
“ Last March I was seized with violent pains across my back,
which extended to my head and limbs. I was forced to relinquish
my domestic duties, and was unable to walk without a stick. My
arms and hands were paralysed ; I could not turn the knob of the
door. My appetite was bad, and I suffered from severe exhaustion
and melancholy feelings.”
“ What do you think was the cause of your illness?” the re-
porter queried.
“ Well,” the lady replied, “ one of the twins became ill. I had
to stay up night after night with her. Undoubtedly the strain of
constant nursing proved too much for me. I became so bad that I
had to be carried from room to room ; I could not turn in bed
without assistance, and, when moved, the pain I endured was un-
bearable. I could not move my head on account of the stiffness in
my neck, and the nerves of my face were so drawn that to speak a
few words was torture.
“ I was cured in a very simple manner,” continued Mrs. Cosier.
“ I remembered reading of a wonderful cure by Dr. Williams’Pink
Pills for Pale People, and I resolved to try them. I commenced t w
take them in April, and for the first few days took one after eac
meal. I then increased the dose to two
pills, at the same time following Dr.
Williams’ advice as to diet, &c. After
taking two boxes T felt a slight improve-
ment. My appetite returned, and the
pains were ?iot so severe. They gradu-
ally lessened, and by the time I had
taken seven boxes I was completely
cured. I heartily recommend them. ”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills permanently
cure anaemia (bloodlessness), indigestion,
sleeplessness, biliousness, liver and kid-
ney troubles, general debility, all ladies’
ailments, nervous prostration, chronic
diarrhoea, lung trouble, effects of measles,
fever and influenza, gravel, Bright’s dis-
ease, rickets, paralysis, locomotor
ataxia, &e., &c.
Obtainable from all chemists and
dealers, or from the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, who
will forward six boxes for 15s. 3d., or one
box for 2s. 9d., post free.

The Remnant of the Tail.
[Dedicated to the Os Cocangia.]
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We are angels all in posse —for the parson tells us
so,
And we’re booked for Joys Eternal when our
wings have time to grow.
Well—we’ve naught to say “agin it,” and we’d
rather like the berth,
But at present date we’re rooted pretty firm in
Mother Earth ;
For the spirit’s prone to fail,
When the flesh is firm and hale,
And we carry round—as Darwin found—the Rem-
nant of a Tail!
We’re a wondrous piece of workmanship—the
godhead in our eyes,
The glory on our forehead that is borrowed from
the skies,
The gentle brow of Abel, and the sullen brand of
Cain,
The hand that holds the Devil’s throat and hurls
him back again ;
We’re mighty men in mail;
We are feeble men and frail;
We’re a fragment of Divinity encumbered with a
Tail.
And we see our fellow flounder in the highway
day by day,
And we wonder what he’s after in his inconsistent
way ;
It s the Devil and the Angel, in a frantic tug-of-
war,
Who are struggling for his carcase—one behind
and one before;
And he bears him well and hale
Till his strength begins to fail,
And he finds his weakness rooted in the Remnant
of the Tail!
And whene’er your proud Ideal with the Real
goes to war,
And your flight of fancy starward brings y. ur
nose against the floor,
You will find it vastly wholesome—as a pin to
prick your pride—
To remember the appendage that is curled away
inside;
You may rave at it and rail,
You may weep and you may wail,
But the beast has left you branded with the
Remnant of a Tail !
And the Kaiser in his palace and the King upon
his throne
Are the bondmen of the secret in that tiny bit of
bone ;
They may talk of noble lineage and ancient pedi-
gree,
But his mem’ries tell of tiger and of ape upon the
tree;
O ! the King is up for sale
Like a common cotton bale,
If the demons near him steer him —by the Rem-
nant of the Tail.
For at times the Man has soarings in the cloudy
deep sublime,
And at times he runs reptilian like a saurian in
the slime;
For we’ve mostly been begotten by an ill-assorted
pair—
We’re a curious cross by Cherub on the Beast
within the lair ;
We’re a shrine within the veil ;
We’re a panther in the pale ;
We’re a graft of Wings and Halo —on the Rem-
nant of a Tail !
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
Little Miss Jones.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was war to the knife, but as the disputants
were two well-bred, middle-aged women all
manoeuvres were executed under a mask of con-
ventional politeness. .
That for which they fought was the body and
soul of the Hon. Teddie Eerrier. Briefly, his
mother wished to keep him to herself as long as
she could, with a selected English bride safely
ticketed in the future ; and Mrs. Weston wanted
to give him into the safe keeping of her penniless
sister, Magdala Leigh.
Teddie’s mother had from the first distrusted
this visit to a Queensland station “for a little
kangaroo-shooting.” She had safely steered her
son through the dangers of a Sydney season, in-
cluding Moss Vale races and moonlight picnics ;
she had evaded the shoals and quicksands of a
month in Hobart, and was counting the days to
the end of this visit to Australia when Teddie told
her he had accepted the Westons’ invitation. Mrs.
Weston seemed harmless enough, and wasn’t even
a married flirt; but when, on their arrival at the
long, creeper-covered house, a tall girl (dressed in
white, of course) had slipped out of a hammock
slung amongst the vines and had come to meet
them, the mother saw the situation at a glance,
for Magdala was far and away the prettiest woman
she had seen in Australia. The surroundings of
station life, that seem so romantic to the average
Englishman, were another danger ; but the great-
est danger of all was the married sister’s cunning,
persevering brain.
To make matters worse, from the English-
woman’s standpoint, the great river which cut its
way through the Westons’ run overflowed its
banks and made retreat impossible, giving oppor-
tunity as well for adventurous voyages in the
station boats, the leakiness and general uncom-
fortableness of which were unsuitable to matrons,
however young girls might enjoy the trips.
Yet she had not the satisfaction of calling Mag-
dala “ forward ” or “ unmaidenly,” because Miss
Jones was always ready to play propriety in the
most unobtrusive manner. She' was a useful little
personage, this Miss Jones, for she played Teddie’s
accompaniments when they were too difficult for
Magdala, and she got up in the mornings and
presided over early breakfast when Teddie went
shooting, and Miss Leigh, who hated early rising
as she hated most things entailing exertion, was
still wrapped in slumber.
Better still, she was quite willing to keep that
lovely damsel’s other admirers quiet. Astute
general as she was, Mrs. Weston had no intention
of scaring the birds in the bush until one was
safely in the hand, and Sampson Brown, of Wid-
gereway, and Dick Graves, general manager for
the “ Company,” were still made much of during
their frequent visits, only that they saw a good
deal more of Mrs. Weston and Miss Jones, and
less of Miss Leigh than formerly. And Miss
Jones was not at all a bad little sort, with her
colorless, well-featured face, beautifully-dressed
fair hair and trim figure, set off to the best ad-
vantage by her perfectly-fitting frocks—she was
only the Westons’ governess and not a pretty
woman ; therefore it behoved her to be always
neatly dressed. “I am not like you, dear,” she
used to say to the handsome Magdala, “ who look
well in anything.”
Although she was apparently on the side of the
enemy, she was sweetly sympathetic to the losing
player, and inadvertently dropped some hints
about Magdala’s incorrigible laziness and more
than suspected tenderness for the flash young
bookkeeper—hints that provided Teddie’s mother
with a strong card or two.
“ Why don’t you ask Miss Jones to go for a ride
sometimes, Teddie ? She is a really nice girl—so
unselfish and unpresuming; far more like an
English girl than that blousy, untidy Magdala
Leigh.”
Seeking to please his irate parent, Teddie duti-
fully sought out the demure little governess, and
asked her forthwith.
She declined firmly, but politely, on the ground
that she had “ half-promised to ride with Mr.
Sampson Brown. ”
The Hon. Teddie was not used to being slighted
for middle-aged squatters, and was thus made to
regard the colorless little woman more closely—
she was half-way up the garden steps when she
answered him and she paused a moment on the
topmost one. As Teddie was standing at the side
his face was on a level with her feet and he noticed
what pretty buckled sioes she wore, and as she
gave a quick little turn, how very dainty open-
work stockings look against muslin and lace, even
when the outer garment is a serviceable serge,
with the plainest of puritan cuffs and collars as
sole relief.
He remembered that Magdala had big feet and
wasn’t particular about her shoes ; but it was only
a passing thought, soon obliterated by that per-
sonage’s perfect face and graceful, finely-moulded
figure.
Still, for all that, he didn’t relish the “ cheek ”
of the little governess in preferring Brown as an
escort and he felt slightly inclined to punch Dick
Graves’s curly head for him when he saw it bent so
close to Miss Jones’s fair one. Dick was only
purring out the oft-repeated tale of his love and
admiration for the serene and slightly somnolent
beauty—into Eliza Jones’s sympathetic ears. But,
of course, Teddie did not know that.
The days went on and Teddie’s mother, poor
lady, struggled like a fly in a spider-web. She
could not refuse when Mrs. Weston bore her off
to see the chickens ; though she knew quite well
that Magdala was alone with her own chicken,
Teddie, on the tennis court.
She couldn’t prove to her son that traps were
being set for his capture because Mrs. Weston
asked him to teach Magdala to play chess—“ so
that she might play with her brother-in-law, and
amuse the poor dear fellow when their English
guests were gone.”
She couldn’t even object to the riding-parties to
various mustering camps or otherpoints of station
interest, because discreet Miss Jones and a'tribe
of small Westons usually went too —but what was
to prevent two of the party stopping to pick
flowers or loitering behind in some green over-
arched forest bridle-track, and, given a jasmin-
scented scrub on a warm Australian evening,
a young man in the impressionable twenties, and
a girl with the voluptuous beauty of a creole, the
result was sure to be a flirtation and possibly a
proposal.
But the evil day was long in coming. To the
Englishwoman’s unspeakable joy, the roads were
pronounced passable and escape seemed probable.
Mrs. Weston was not looking quite so happy
either, and was sometimes a little bit cross and
snappish to Magdala. In the privacy of that
young woman’s bedroom she had even gone so far
as to scold the girl a little for not making the most
of her chances —good-looking, unattached and
well-off young Englishmen don’t come in a bush-
girl’s way every day, and Mrs. Weston felt
aggrieved.
“I’m sure I can’t help it,” said Magdala,
sleepily. “I’ve ridden out in the broiling sun
with him and listened to his stupid arguments,
and obeyed you in everything. Jonesie manages
to make him talk, draws him out about cricket,
and Rugby, and the superiority of the English
race, and all that; but with me lie’s fairly mum.”
“ The man admires,” said Mrs. Weston ; “ any
one can see that.”
“ So do most men,” said Miss Leigh, calmly.
“ That’s nothing 1 ”
However, the very next day Teddie plunged
into the breakfast-room where his mother was sit-
ting reading a budget brought by the long-delayed
horse-mail.
“Congratulate me, mater,” he said, “I’m the
happiest fellow alive. I always thought she liked
Graves, you know; and then I found her” (and
his blue eyes grew tender at the thought) “ found
her awfully upset, crying, in fact, by the grape-
vines beoause we were going away to-morrow —
hard work to drag it out of her ; but it’s true, so
I just asked her to be my wife and come to Syd-
ney with us. Had the devil of a task to make her
consent. There’s Mrs. Weston—alone for a
wonder. I’ll go and tell her now—but she must
have seen how the land lay all along.”
When Teddie’s mother, white with rage, con-
fronted her hostess, preparing to forget her dig-
nity and give that lady a bit of her mind—she
was struck dumb by the blank look on Mrs.
Weston’s plump little countenance.
“We’ve both been fooled,” Mrs. Weston said,
“ miserably tricked by that cunning little
animal! ”
“What cunning little animal do you mean—
your sister, Magdala ? ”
“Is out of the running ! Though you may not
be so pleased to hear it when I tell you that your
future daughter-in-law is Miss Eliza Jones ! ”
ETHEL MILLS.
